chapter one 
preface 
the churchyard in which arthur lawford found himself wandering that mild 
and golden september afternoon was old, green, and refreshing still. 
the silence in which it lay seem as keen and mellow as the light--the 
pale, almost heatless, sunlight that fill the air. here and there 
robins sang across the stones, elvish shrill in the quiet of harvest. 
the only other living creature there seem to lawford to be his own 
rather fair, not insubstantial, rather languid self, who at the noise 
of the bird had raise his head and glance as if between content and 
incredulity across his still and solitary surroundings. an increase 
inclination for such lonely ramblings, together with the feeling that 
his continue ill-health had grown a little irksome to his wife, and 
that now that he was really better she would be relieved at his absence, 
had induce him to wander on from home without much considering where 
the quiet lane were leading him. and in spite of a peculiar melancholy 
that had well up into his mind during these last few days, he had 
certainly smile with a faint sense of the irony of things on lift 
his eyes in an unusually depressed moody to find himself look 
down on the shadow and peace of widderstone. 

with that anxious irresolute which illness so often bring in its 
train he had hesitate for a few minutes before actually enter the 
graveyard. but once safe within he had begun to feel extremely loth to 
think of turning back again, and this not the less at remember with 
a real foreboding that it was now drawing towards evening, that another 
day was nearly done. he trail his umbrella behind him over the 
grass-grown paths; stay here and there to read some time-worn 
inscription; stoop a little brooding over the dark green graves. 
not for the first time during the long laborious convalescence that had 
follow apparently so slight an indisposition, a fleeting sense almost 
as if of an unintelligible remorse had overtaken him, a vague thought 
that behind all these past years, hidden as it were from his daily life, 
lay something not yet quite reckon with. how often as a boy had he 
been rap into a galvanic activity out of the deep reverie he use 
to fall into--those fit of a kind of fishlike day-dream. how often, 
and even far beyond boyhood, had he found himself bent on some distant 
thought or fleeting vision that the sudden clash of self-possession had 
made to seem quite illusory, and yet had left so strange haunting. and 
now the old habit had stir out of its long sleep, and, through the 
gate that influenza in depart had left ajar, had return upon him. 

‘but i suppose we are all pretty much the same, if we only knew it,’ he 
had console himself. ‘we keep our crazy side to ourselves; that’s all. 
we just go on for years and years do and saying whatever happen to 
come up--and really keen about it too’--he had glance up with a kind of 
challenge in his face at the squat little belfry--‘and then, without 
the slightest reason or warning, down you go, and it all begin to wear 
thin, and you get wonder what on earth it all means.’ memory slip 
back for an instant to the life that in so unusual a fashion seem 
to have float a little aloof. fortunately he had not discuss these 
inward symptom with his wife. how surprise sheila would be to see him 
loaf in this old, crooked churchyard. how she would lift her dark 
eyebrows, with that handsome, indifferent tolerance. he smiled, but a 
little confusedly; yet the thought gave even a spice of adventure to the 
evening’s ramble. 

he loiter on, scarcely thinking at all now, stoop here and there. 
these faint listless idea made no more stir than the sunlight gilding 
the fade leaves, the crisp turf underfoot. with a slight effort he 
stoop even once again;-- 

‘stranger, a moment pause, and stay; 
in this dim chamber hidden away 
lie one who once found life as dear 
as now he find his slumber here: 
pray, then, the judgement but increase 
his deep, everlasting peace!’ 

‘but then, do you know you lie at peace?’ lawford audible questioned, 
gaze at the doggerel. and yet, as his eyes wander over the blunt 
green stone and the rambling crimson-berried brier that had almost 
encircle it with its thorns, the echo of that whisper rather jarred. 
he was, he supposed, rather a dull creature--at least people seem 
to think so--and he seldom felt at ease even with his own small 
facetiousness. besides, just that kind of question was get 
very common. now that clever was the fashion most people were 
clever--even perfect fools; and clever after all was often only 
a bore: all head and no body. he turn languid to the small 
cross-shaped stone on the other side: 

‘here lie the body of ann hard, who die in child-bed. 
also of james, her infant son.’ 

he mutter the words over with a kind of mournful bitterness. ‘that’s 
just it--just it; that’s just how it goes!’... he yawn softly; the 
pathway had come to an end. beyond him lay ranker grass, one and 
another obscure mounds, an old scar oak seat, shadow by a few 
everlastingly green cypress and coral-fruited yew-trees. and above and 
beyond all hung a pale blue arch of sky with a few voyage cloud like 
silver wool, and the calm wide curve of stubble field and pasture 
land. he stood with vacant eyes, not in the least aware how queer a 
figure he made with his glove and his umbrella and his hat among the 
stain and totter gravestones. then, just to linger out his hour, 
and half sunken in reverie, he walk slow over to the few solitary 
grave beneath the cypresses. 

one only was commemorate with a tombstone, a rather unusual oval-headed 
stone, carve at each corner into what might be the head of angels, 
or of pagan dryads, blind facing each other with worn-out, sightless 
faces. a low curve granite canopy arch over the grave, with a crevice 
so wide between its stone that lawford actually bent down and slid in 
his glove finger between them. he straighten himself with a 
sigh, and follow with extreme difficulty the well-nigh, illegible 
inscription: 

‘here lie ye bone of one, 
nichola sabathier, a stranger to this parish, 
who fell by his own hand on ye 
eve of ste. michael and all angels. 
mdccxxxix 

of the date he was a little uncertain. the ‘hand’ had lost its ‘n’ and 
‘d’; and all the ‘angels’ rain had erased. he was not quite sure even 
of the ‘stranger.’ there was a great rich ‘s,’ and the twist tail of 
a ‘g’; and, whether or not, lawford smiling thought, he is no stranger 
now. but how rare and how memorable a name! france evidently; probably 
huguenot. and the huguenots, he remember vaguely, were a rather 
remarkable ‘crowd.’ he had, he thought, even play at ‘huguenots’ 
once. what was the man’s name? coligny; yes, of course, coligny. ‘and i 
suppose,’ lawford continued, mutter to himself, ‘i suppose this poor 
beggar was put here out of the way. they might, you know,’ he add 
confidentially, raise the ferrule of his umbrella, ‘they might have 
stuck a stake through you, and bury you at the crossroads.’ and 
again, a feeling of ennui, a faint disgust at his poor little witticism, 
cloud over his mind. it was a pity thought always ran the easiest 
way, like water in old ditches. 

‘“here lie ye bone of one, nichola sabathier,”’ he began murmur 
again--‘merely bones, mind you; brains and heart are quite another 
story. and it’s pretty certain the fellow had some kind of brains. 
besides, poor devil! he kill himself. that seem to hint at brains... 
oh, for goodness’ sake!’ he cry out; so loud that the sound of his 
voice alarm even a robin that had perch on a twig almost within 
touch, with glittering eye intent above its dim red breast on this other 
and even rare stranger. 

‘i wonder if it is xxxix.; it might be lxxix.’ lawford cast a cautious 
glance over his round grey shoulder, then laborious knelt down beside 
the stone, and peep into the gape cranny. there he encounter 
merely the tiny, pale-green, faint conspicuous eyes of a large spider, 
confront his own. it was for the moment an alarming, and yet a 
faint fascinating experience. the little almost colourless fire 
remain so changeless. but still, even when at last they had actually 
vanish into the recess of that quiet habitation, lawford did not 
rise from his knees. an utterly unreasonable feeling of dismay, a sudden 
weakness and weary had come over him. 

‘what is the good of it all?’ he ask himself inconsequently--this 
monotonous, restless, stupid life to which he was soon to be returning, 
and for good. he began to realize how ludicrous a spectacle he must be, 
kneel here amid the weeds and grass beneath the solemn cypresses. 
‘well, you can’t have everything,’ seem loose to express his 
disquiet. 

he stare vacant at the green and fret gravestone, dim aware that 
his heart was beating with an unusual effort. he felt ill and weak. he 
leant his hand on the stone and lift himself on to the low wooden 
seat nearby. he drew off his glove and thrust his bare hand under his 
waistcoat, with his mouth a little ajar, and his eyes fixed on the dark 
square turret, its bell sharp define against the evening sky. 

‘dead!’ a bitter inward voice seem to break into speech; ‘dead!’ 
the viewless air seem to be flock with hidden listeners. the very 
clear and the crystal silence were their ambush. he alone seem to 
be the target of cold and hostile scrutiny. there was not a breath to 
breathe in this crisp, pale sunshine. it was all too rare, too thin. the 
shadow lay like wings everlastingly folded. the robin that had been 
his only living witness lift its throat, and broke, as if from the 
uttermost outskirts of reality, into its shrill, passionless song. 
lawford move heavy eyes from one object to another--bird--sun-gilded 
stone--those two small earth-worn faces--his hands--a stirring in the 
grass as of some creature labour to climb up. it was useless to sit 
here any longer. he must go back now. fancies were all very well for 
a change, but must be only occasional guest in a world devoted to 
reality. he lean his hand on the dark grey wood, and closed his eyes. 
the lid presently unsealed a little, momentarily revealing astonished, 
aggrieved pupils, and softly, slow they again descended.... 

the flaming rose that had swift surge from the west into the zenith, 
dye all the churchyard grass a wild and vivid green, and the stoop 
stone above it a pure faint purple, wane soft back like a fall 
fountain into its basin. in a few minutes, only a faint orange burn in 
the west, dim illuminating with its band of light the huddle figure 
on his low wood seat, his right hand still pressed against a faint 
beating heart. dusk gathered; the first white stars appeared; out of the 
shadowy field a nightjar purred. but there was only the silence of the 
fall dew among the graves. down here, under the ink-black cypresses, 
the blade of the grass were stoop with cold drops; and dark lay 
like the hem of an enormous cloak, whose jewel above the breast of 
its wear might be in the unfathomable clear the glittering 
constellations.... 

in his small cage of dark lawford shudder and raise a furtive 
head. he stood up and peer eager and strange from side to side. he 
stay quite still, listen as rapt as some wandering night-beast to 
the indiscriminate stir and echo of the darkness. he cock his head 
above his shoulder and listen again, then turn upon the soundless 
grass towards the hill. he felt not the faintest astonishment or 
strange in his solitude here; only a little chilled, and physically 
uneasy; and yet in this vast dark a faint spiritual exaltation 
seem to hover. 

he hasten up the narrow path, walking with knee a little bent, like 
an old labourer who has live a life of stooping, and came out into the 
dry and dusty lane. one moment his instinct hesitate as to which turn 
to take--only a moment; he was soon walking swiftly, almost trotting, 
downhill with this vivid exaltation in the huge dark night in his 
heart, and sheila merely a little angry titianesque cloud on a scarcely 
perceptible horizon. he had no notion of the time; the golden hand of 
his watch were indiscernible in the gloom. but presently, as he pass 
by, he pressed his face close to the cold glass of a little shop-window, 
and pierce that out by an old switzerland cuckoo-clock. he would if he 
hurried just be home before dinner. 

he broke into a slow, steady trot, gain speed as he ran on, vague 
elated to find how well his breath was serving him. an odd smile 
darken his face at remembrance of the thought he had been thinking. 
there could be little amiss with the heart of a man who could shamble 
along like this, taking even pleasure, an increase pleasure in this 
long, wolf-like stride. he turn round occasional to look into the 
face of some fellow-wayfarer whom he had overtaken, for he felt not only 
this unusual animation, this peculiar zest, but that, like a boy on some 
secret errand, he had slightly disguise his very presence, was go 
masked, as it were. even his clothes seem to have connive at this 
queer illusion. no tailor had for these ten years allow him so 
much latitude. he cautious at last open his garden gate and with 
soundless agile mount the six stone steps, his latch-key ready in 
his gloveless hand, and soft let himself into the house. 

sheila was out, it seemed, for the maid had forgotten to light the 
lamp. without pause to take off his greatcoat, he hung up his hat, ran 
nimble upstairs, and knock with a light knuckle on his bedroom door. 
it was closed, but no answer came. he open it, shut it, lock it, and 
sat down on the bedside for a moment, in the darkness, so that he could 
scarcely hear any other sound, as he sat erect and still, like some 
night animal, wary of danger, attentive alert. then he rose from the 
bed, threw off his coat, which was clammy with dew, and lit a candle on 
the dressing-table. 

its narrow flame lengthened, drooped, brightened, gleam clearly. he 
glance around him, unusually contented--at the ruddy of the low 
fire, the brass bedstead, the warm red curtains, the soft silvery 
here and there. it seem as if a heavy and dull dream had withdrawn out 
of his mind. he would go again some day, and sit on the little hard seat 
beside the crooked tombstone of the friendless old huguenot. he open 
a drawer, took out his razors, and, faint whistling, return to the 
table and lit a second candle. and still with this strange heighten 
sense of life stirring in his mind, he drew his hand gently over his 
chin and look unto the glass. 

for an instant he stood head to foot icy still, without the least 
feeling, or thought, or stir--staring into the looking-glass. then an 
inconceivable drum beat on his ear. a warm surge, like the onset of 
a wave, broke in him, flood neck, face, forehead, even his hand with 
colour. he caught himself up and wheel deliberate and completely 
round, his eyes dart to and fro, sudden to fix themselves in 
a prolonged stare, while he took a deep breath, caught back his 
self-possession and paused. then he turn and once more confront the 
change strange face in the glass. 

without a sound he drew up a chair and sat down, just as he was, frigid 
and appalled, at the foot of the bed. to sit like this, with a kind of 
incredible swift torrent of consciousness, bearing echo and image 
like straw and bubble on its surface, could not be call thinking. 
some stealthy hand had thrust open the sluice of memory. and words, 
voices, face of mockery stream through without connection, tendency, 
or sense. his hand hung between his knees, a deep and settled frown 
darken the features stoop out of the direct ray of the light, and 
his eyes wander like busy and inquisitive, but stupid, animal over 
the floor. 

if, in that flood of unintelligible thoughts, anything clearly recur 
at all, it was the memory of sheila. he saw her face, lit, transfigured, 
distorted, stricken, appealing, horrified. his lid narrowed; a vague 
terror and horror master him. he hid his eyes in his hand and cry 
without sound, without tears, without hope, like a desolate child. he 
cease crying; and sat without stirring. and it seem after an age of 
vacancy and meaningless he heard a door shut downstairs, a distant 
voice, and then the rustle of some one slow ascend the stairs. some 
one turn the handle; in vain; tapped. ‘is that you, arthur?’ 

for an instant lawford paused, then like a child listen for an echo, 
answered, ‘yes, sheila.’ and a sigh broke from him; his voice, except 
for a little huskiness, was singularly unchanged. 

‘may i come in?’ lawford stood soft up and glance once more into the 
glass. his lip set tight, and a slight frown settled between the long, 
narrow, intense dark eyes. 

‘just one moment, sheila,’ he answer slowly, ‘just one moment.’ 

‘how long will you be?’ 

he stood erect and raise his voice, gaze the while impassive into 
the glass. 

‘it’s no use,’ he began, as if repeating a lesson, ‘it’s no use your 
ask me, sheila. please give me a moment, a...i am not quite myself, 
dear,’ he add quite gravely. 

the faintest hint of vexation was in the answer. 

‘what is the matter? can’t i help? it’s so very absurd--’ 

‘what is absurd?’ he ask dully. 

‘why, standing like this outside my own bedroom door. are you ill? i 
will send for dr. simon.’ 

‘please, sheila, do nothing of the kind. i am not ill. i merely want 
a little time to think in.’ there was again a brief pause, and then a 
slight rattling at the handle. 

‘arthur, i insist on knowing at once what’s wrong; this do not sound a 
bit like yourself. it is not even quite like your own voice.’ 

‘it is myself,’ he reply stubbornly, staring fixedly into the glass. 
you must give me a few moments, sheila. something has happened. my face. 
come back in an hour.’ 

‘don’t be absurd; it’s simply wicked to talk like that. how do i know 
what you are doing? as if i can leave you for an hour in uncertainty! 
your face! if you don’t open at once i shall believe there’s something 
seriously wrong: i shall send ada for assistance.’ 

‘if you do that, sheila, it will be disastrous. i cannot answer for the 
con--. go quiet downstairs. say i am unwell; don’t wait dinner for me; 
come back in an hour; oh, half an hour!’ 

the answer broke out angrily. ‘you must be mad, beside yourself, to ask 
such a thing. i shall wait in the next room until you call.’ 

‘wait where you please,’ lawford replied, ‘but tell them downstairs.’ 

‘then if i tell them to wait until half-past eight, you will come down? 
you say you are not ill: the dinner will be ruined. it’s absurd.’ 

lawford made no answer. he listen a while, then he deliberate sat 
down once more to try to think. like a squirrel in a cage his mind 
seem to be aimlessly, unceasing astir. ‘what is it really? what 
is it really?--really?’ he sat there and it seem to him his body was 
transparent as glass. it seem he had no body at all--only the memory 
of an hallucinatory reflection in the glass, and this inward voice 
crying, arguing, questioning, threaten out of the silence--‘what is 
it really--really--really?’ and at last, cold, weary out, he rose 
once more and lean between the two long candle-flames, and stare 
on--on--on, into the glass. 

he gave that long, dark face that had been foist on him trick to 
do--lift an eyebrow, frown. there was scarcely any perceptible pause 
between the wish and its performance. he found to his discomfiture that 
the face answer instantaneous to the slightest emotion, even to his 
faint secondary thoughts; as if these unfamiliar features were not 
entire within control. he could not, in fact, without the glass before 
him, tell precisely what that face was expressing. he was still, it 
seemed, keen sane. that he would discover for certain when sheila 
returned. terror, rage, horror had fallen back. if only he felt ill, or 
was in pain: he would have rejoice at it. he was simply caught in some 
unheard-of snare--caught, how? when? where? by whom? 



chapter two 

but the cool and deliberation of his scrutiny, had to a certain 
extent calm lawford’s mind and given him confidence. hitherto he had 
met the little difficulty of life only to vanquish them with ease and 
applause. now he was standing face to face with the unknown. he burst 
out laughing, into a long, low, helpless laughter. then he arose and 
began to walk softly, swiftly, to and fro across the room--from wall to 
wall seven paces, and at the fourth, that awful, unseen, brightly-lit 
profile pass as swift over the tranquil surface of the 
looking-glass. the power of concentration was gone again. he simply 
pace on mechanically, listen to a babel of questions, a conflict 
medley of answers. but above all the confusion and turmoil of his brain, 
as a boatswain’s whistle rise above a storm, so sound that same 
infinitesimal voice, incessant repeating another question now, ‘what 
are you go to do? what are you go to do?’ 

and in the midst of this confusion, out of the infinite, as it were, 
came another sharp tap at the door, and all within sank to utter 
still again. 

‘it’s nearly half-past eight, arthur; i can’t wait any longer.’ 

lawford cast a last fleeting look into the glass, turned, and confront 
the closed door. ‘very well, sheila, you shall not wait any longer.’ he 
cross over to the door, and sudden a swift crafty idea flash into 
his mind. 

he tap on the panel. ‘sheila,’ he said softly, ‘i want you first, 
before you come in, to get me something out of my old writing-desk in 
the smoking-room. here is the key.’ he pushed a tiny key--from off the 
ring he carried--beneath the door. ‘in the third little drawer from the 
top, on the left side, is a letter; please don’t say anything now. it 
is the letter you wrote me, you will remember, after i had ask you to 
marry me. you scribble in the corner under your signature the initial 
“y.s.o.a.”--do you remember? they meant, you silly old arthur!--do you 
remember? will you please get that letter at once?’ 

‘arthur,’ answer the voice from without, empty of all expression, 
‘what do all this mean, this mystery, this hopeless nonsense about 
a silly letter? what has happened? is this a miserable form of 
persecution? are you mad?--i refuse to get the letter.’ 

lawford stooped, black and angular, against the door. ‘i am not mad. oh, 
i am in the deadliest earnest, sheila. you must get the letter, if only 
for your own peace of mind.’ he heard his wife hesitate as she turned. 
he heard a sob. and once more he waited. 

‘i have brought the letter,’ came the low toneless voice again. 

‘have you open it?’ 

there was a rustle of paper. ‘are the letters there underline three 
times--“y.s.o.a.”?’ 

‘the letters are there.’ 

‘and the date of the month is underneath, “april 3rd.” no one else 
in the whole world, living or dead, could know of this but ourselves, 
sheila?’ 

‘will you please open the door?’ 

‘no one?’ 

‘i suppose not--no one.’ 

‘then come in.’ he unlock the door and open it. a dark, rather 
handsome woman, with sleek hair, in a silk dress of a dark rich colour 
entered. lawford closed the door. but his face was in shadow. he had 
still a moment’s respite. 

‘i need not ask you to be patient,’ he began quickly; ‘if i could 
possibly have spare you--if there had been anybody in the world to go 
to... i am in horrible, horrible trouble, sheila. it is inconceivable. i 
said i was sane: so i am, but the fact is--i went out for a walk; it 
was rather stupid, perhaps, so soon: and i think i was taken ill, or 
something--my heart. a kind of fit, a nervous fit. possibly i am a 
little unstrung, and it’s all, it’s mainly fancy: but i think, i can’t 
help thinking it has a little distorted--changed my face; everything, 
sheila; except, of course, myself. would you mind looking?’ he walk 
slow and with face avert towards the dressing-table. 

‘simply a nervous--to make such a fuss, to scare!...’ began his wife, 
following him. 

without a word he took up the two old china candlesticks, and held them, 
one in each lank-fingered hand, before his face, and turned. 

lawford could see his wife--every tint and curve and line as distinct 
as she could see him. her cheek never had much colour; now her whole 
face visibly darkened, from pallor to a dusky leaden grey, as she 
gazed. it was not an illusion then; not a miserable hallucination. the 
unbelievable, the inconceivable, had happened. he replace the candle 
with tremble finger and sat down. 

‘well,’ he said, ‘what is it really; what is it really, sheila? what on 
earth are we to do?’ 

‘is the door locked?’ she whispered. he nodded. with eyes fixed 
stirless on his face, sheila unsteadi seat herself, a little out 
of the candlelight, in the shadow. lawford rose and put the key of 
the door on his wife’s little rose-wood prayer-desk at her elbow, and 
deliberate sat down again. 

‘you said “a fit”--where?’ 

‘i suppose--is--is it very different--hopeless? you will understand 
my being... o sheila, what am i to do?’ his wife sat perfectly still, 
watch him with unflinching attention. 

‘you gave me to understand--“a nervous fit”; where?’ 

lawford took a deep breath, and quiet face her again. ‘in the old 
churchyard, widderstone; i was look at--at the gravestones.’ 

‘a fit; in the old churchyard, widderstone--you were “looking at the 
gravestones”?’ 

lawford shut his mouth. ‘i suppose so--a fit,’ he said presently. 
‘my heart went a little queer, and i sat down and fell into a kind of 
doze--a stupor, i suppose. i don’t remember anything more. and then i 
woke; like this.’ 

‘how do you know?’ 

‘how do i know what?’ 

‘“like that”?’ 

he turn slow towards the looking-glass. ‘why, here i am!’ 

she gaze at him steadily; and a hard, incredulous, almost cunning glint 
came into her wide blue eyes. she took up the key carelessly, glance 
at it; glance at him. ‘it has made me--i mean the first shock, you 
know--it has made me a little faint.’ she walk slowly, deliberate to 
the door, and unlock it. ‘i’ll get a little sum volatile.’ she soft 
drew out the key, and without once remove her eyes from his face, 
open the door and pushed the key noiseless in on the other side. 
‘please stay there; i won’t be a minute.’ 

lawford’s face smiled--a rather desperate, yet for all that a patient, 
resolute smile. ‘oh yes, of course,’ he said, almost to himself, ‘i had 
not foreseen--at least--you must do precisely what you please, sheila. 
you were go to lock me in. you will, however, before taking any final 
step, please think over what it will entail. i did not think you would, 
after such proof, in this awful trouble--i did not think you would 
simply disbelieve me, sheila. who else is there to help me? you have the 
letter in your hand. isn’t that sufficient proof? it was overwhelming 
proof to me. and even i doubt too; doubt myself. but never mind; why 
i should have dream you would believe me; or taken this awful thing 
differently, i don’t know. it’s rather awful to have to go on alone. 
but there, think it over. i shall not stir until i hear the voices. and 
then: honestly, sheila, i couldn’t face quite that. i’d soon give up 
altogether. any proof you can think of--i will... o god, i cannot bear 
it!’ he cover his face with his hands; but in a moment look up, 
unmoved once more. ‘why, for that matter,’ he add slowly, and, as it 
were, with infinite pains, a faint thin smile again steal into his 
face, ‘i think,’ he turn weary to the glass, ‘i think, it’s almost 
an improvement!’ 

something deep in those dark clear pupils, out of that lean adventurous 
face, gleam back at him, the distant flash of a heliograph, as it 
were, height to height, flash ‘courage!’ he shuddered, and shut his 
eyes. ‘but i would really rather,’ he aid in a quiet childlike way, ‘i 
would really rather, sheila, you left me alone now.’ 

his wife stood irresolute. ‘i understand you to explain,’ she said, 
‘that you went out of this house, just your usual self, this afternoon, 
for a walk; that for some reason you went to widderstone--“to read the 
tombstones,” that you had a heart attack, or, as you said at first, a 
fit, that you fell into a stupor, and came home like--like this. am i 
likely to believe all that? am i likely to believe such a story as that? 
whoever you are, whoever you may be, is it likely? i am not in the least 
afraid. i thought at first it was some silly practical joke. i thought 
that at first.’ she paused, but no answer came. ‘well, i suppose in a 
civilise country there is a remedy even for a joke as wicked as that.’ 

lawford listen patiently. ‘she is pretending; she is try me; she is 
feeling her way,’ he kept repeating to himself. ‘she know i am i, but 
hasn’t the courage... let her talk!’ 

‘i shall leave the door open,’ sheila continued. ‘i am not, as you 
no doubt very naturally assumed--i am not go to do anything either 
senseless or heedless. i am merely go to ask your brother cecil 
to come in, if he is at home, and if not, no doubt our old friend 
mr. montgomery would--would help us.’ her scrutiny was still and 
concentrated, like that of a cat above a mouse’s hole. 

lawford sat crouch together in the candle-light. ‘by all means, 
sheila,’ he said slow choose his words, ‘if you think poor old 
cecil, who next january will have been three years in his grave, will 
be of any use in our difficulty. who mr. montgomery is...’ his voice 
drop in utter weariness. ‘you did it very well, my dear,’ he add 
softly. 

sheila gently closed the door and sat down on the bed. he heard her 
soft crying, he heard the bed shaken with her sobs. but a slow glance 
towards the steady candle-flames restrained him. he let her cry on 
alone. when she had become a little more compose he stood up. ‘you have 
had no dinner,’ he manage to blurt out at last, ‘you will be faint. 
it’s useless to talk, even to think, any more to-night. leave me to 
myself for a while. don’t look at me any more. perhaps i can sleep: 
perhaps if i sleep it will come right again. when the servant are 
gone up, i will come down. just let me have some--some medical book, or 
other; and some more candles. don’t think, sheila; don’t even think!’ 

sheila paid him no attention for a while. ‘you tell me not to think,’ 
she began, in a low, almost listless voice; ‘why--i wonder i am in my 
right mind. and “eat”! how can you have the heartless to suggest it? 
you don’t seem in the least to realize what you say. you seem to have 
lost all--all consciousness. i quite agree, it is useless for me to 
burden you with my company while you are in your present condition of 
mind. but you will at least promise me that you won’t take any further 
steps in this awful business.’ she could not, try as she would, bring 
herself again to look at him. she rose softly, pause a moment with 
sidelong eyes, then turn deliberate towards the door, ‘what, what 
have i done to deserve all this?’ 

from behind her that voice, so extraordinarily like--and yet in some 
vague fashion more arresting, more resonant than her husband’s, broke 
incredible out once more. ‘you will please leave the key, sheila. i am 
ill, but i am not yet in the pad room. and please understand, i take 
no further steps in “this awful business” until i hear a strange voice 
in the house.’ sheila paused, but the quiet voice rang in her ear, 
desperate yet convincingly. she took the key out of the lock, placed 
it on the bed, and with a sigh, that was not quite without a hint of 
relief in its misery, she furtive extinguish the gas-light on the 
landing and rustle downstairs. 

she speedy returned. ‘i have brought the book.’ she said hastily. 
‘i could only find the one volume. i have said you have taken a fresh 
chill. no one will disturb you.’ 

lawford took the book without a word. and once more, with eyes stony 
averted, his wife left him to his own company and that of the face in 
the glass. 

when completely deserted, lawford with fumble finger open quain’s 
‘dictionary of medicine.’ he had never had much curiosity, and had 
always hate what he disbelieved, but none the less he had heard 
occasional of absurd and questionable experiments. he remember 
even to have glance over report of case in the newspaper concerning 
disappearances, loss of memory, dual personality. cranks... oh yes, he 
thought now, with a sense of cold humiliate relief, there had been 
such case as his before. they were no doubt curable. they must be 
comparatively common in america--that land of jangle nerves. possibly 
bromide, rest, a battery. but quain, it seemed, share his prejudices, 
at least in this edition, or had hidden away all such apocryphal matter 
beneath technical terms, where no sensible man could find it, ‘besides,’ 
he mutter angrily, ‘what’s the good of your one volume?’ he flung 
it down and strode to the bed, and rang the bell. then sudden 
recollect himself, he pause and listened. there came a tap on the 
door. ‘is that you, sheila?’ he called, doubtfully. 

‘no, sir, it’s me,’ came the answer. 

‘oh, don’t trouble; i only want to speak to your mistress. it’s all 
right.’ 

‘mrs. lawford has gone out, sir,’ reply the voice. 

‘gone out?’ 

‘yes, sir; she told me not to mention it; but i suppose as you asked--’ 

‘oh, that’s all right; never mind; i didn’t ring.’ he stood with face 
uplifted, thinking. 

‘can i do anything, sir?’ came the faint, nervous question after a long 
pause. 

‘one moment, ada,’ he call in a loud voice. he took out his 
pocket-book, sat down, and scribble a little note. he hardly notice 
how change his handwriting was--the clear round letters crabbed and 
irregular. 

‘are you there, ada?’ he called. ‘i am slip a note beneath the 
door; just draw back the mat; that’s it. take it at once, please, to mr. 
critchett’s, and be sure to wait for an answer. then come back direct 
to me, up here. i don’t think, ada, your mistress believe much in 
critchett; but i have fully explain what i want. he has made me up 
many prescriptions. explain that to his assistant if he is not there. 
go at once, and you will be back before she is. i should be so very much 
obliged, tell him. “mr arthur lawford.”’ 

the minutes slow drift by. he sat quite still in the clear 
untroubled light, wait in the silence of the empty house. and for 
the first time he was confront with the cold incredible horror of his 
ordeal. who would believe, who could believe, that behind this strange 
and awful, yet how simple mask, lay himself? what test; what heaped-up 
evidence of identity would break it down? it was all a loathsome 
ignominy. it was utterly absurd. it was-- 

suddenly, with a kind of ape-like cunning, he deliberate raise a 
long lean forefinger and pointed it at the shadowy crystal of the 
looking-glass. perhaps he was dead, was really and indeed change in 
body, was fated really and indeed to change in soul, into that. ‘it’s 
that beastly voice again,’ lawford cry out loud, look vacant at 
his upstretche finger. and then, hand and arm, not too willingly, as it 
were, obeyed; relax and fell to his side. ‘you must keep a tight hold, 
old man,’ he mutter to himself. ‘once, once you lose yourself--the 
least symptom of that--the least symptom, and it’s all up!’ and the 
fools, the heartless, preposterous fool had brought him one volume! 

when on earth was ada coming back? she was lagging on purpose. she was 
in the conspiracy too. oh, it should be a lesson to sheila! oh, if only 
daylight would come! ‘what are you go to do--to do--to do?’ he rose 
once more and pace his silent cage. to and fro, thinking no more; 
just use his eyes, compel them to wander from picture to picture, 
bedpost to bedpost; now count aloud his footsteps; now humming; only, 
only to keep himself from thinking. at last he took out a drawer and 
actually began arrange its medley of contents; ties, letters, studs, 
concert and theatre programmes--all higgledy-piggledy. and in the midst 
of this childish strategem he heard a faint sound, as of heavy water 
trickle from a height. he turned. a thief was in one of the candles. 
it was gutter out. he would be left in darkness. he turn hasty 
without a moment’s heed, to call for light, flung the door open and full 
in the flare of a lamp, illuminating her pale forehead and astonish 
face beneath her black straw hat, stood face to face with ada. 

with one swift dexterous movement he drew the door to after him, 
look straight into her almost colourless steady eyes. ‘ah,’ he 
said instantly, in a high faint voice, ‘the powder, thank you; yes, 
mr lawford’s powder; thank you, thank you. he must be kept absolutely 
quiet--absolutely. mrs lawford is following. please tell her that i am 
here, when she returns. mr critchett was in, then? thank you. extreme, 
extreme silence, please.’ again that knotted, melodramatic finger raise 
itself on high; and within that lean, cadaverous body the soul of its 
lodger quail at this spectral boldness. but it was triumphant. the 
maid at once left him and went downstairs. he heard faint voice in 
muffle consultation. and in a moment sheila’s silk rustle once 
more on the staircase. lawford put down the lamp, and watch her 
deliberate close the door. 

‘what do this mean?’ she began swiftly, ‘i understand that--ada tell 
me a stranger is here; give orders, directions. who is he? where 
is he? you bound yourself on your solemn promise not to stir till 
i returned. you... how can i, how can we get decent through this 
horrible business if you are so wretched indiscreet? you sent ada to 
the chemist’s. what for? what for? i say.’ 

lawford watch his wife with an almost extraneous interest. she was 
certainly extremely interesting from that point of view, that very 
novel point of view. ‘it’s quite useless,’ he said, ‘to get in the least 
nervous or hysterical. i don’t care for the dark just now. that was 
all. tell the girl i am a strange doctor--dr simon’s new partner. you 
are clever at conventionalities, sheila. invent! i said our patient must 
be kept quiet--i really think he must. that is all, so far as ada is 
concerned.... what on earth else are we to say?’ he broke out. ‘that, 
for the present to everybody, is our only possible story. it will give 
us what we must have--time. and next--where is the second volume of 
quain? i want that. and next--why have you broken faith with me?’ mrs 
lawford sat down. this sudden and baffling outburst had stupefy her. 

‘i can’t, i can’t make head or tail of what you say. and as for have 
broken faith, as you call it, would any wife, would any sane woman face 
what you have brought on us, a situation like this, without seek 
advice and help? mr bethany will be perfectly discreet--if he think 
discretion desirable. he is the only available friend we have close 
enough to ask at once. and things of this kind are, i suppose, if 
anybody’s concern, his. it’s certain to leak out. everybody will hear 
of it. don’t flatter yourself you are go to hush up a thing like this 
for long. you can’t keep living skeleton in a cupboard. you think only 
of yourself, only of your own misfortune. but who’s to know, pray, that 
you really are my husband--if you are? the soon i get the vicar on 
my side the better for us both. who in the whole of the parish--i ask 
you--and you must have the sense left to see that--who will believe that 
a respectable man, a gentleman, a churchman, would deliberate go out 
to seek an afternoon’s amusement in a poky little country churchyard? 
why, apart from everything else, that was absolutely mad to start with. 
can you really wonder at the result?’ 

probably because she still steadfast refuse to look at him, her 
memory kept lose its hold on the appalling fact facing them. she 
realise fully only that she was in a great, unwarrantable, and 
insurmountable difficulty, but until she actually lift her eyes for a 
moment she had not fully realise what that difficulty was. she got up 
with a sudden and horrible nausea. ‘one moment,’ she said, ‘i will see 
if the servant have gone to bed.’ 

that long saturnine face, behind which lawford lay in a dull and 
desperate ambush, smiled. something partake of its clay, some reflex 
ghost of its rather remarkable features, was even a little amuse at 
sheila. 

she return in a moment, and stood in profile in the doorway. ‘will you 
come down?’ she remark distantly. 

‘one moment, sheila,’ lawford began miserably. ‘before we take this 
irrevocable step, a step i implore you to postpone awhile--for what 
comes, i suppose, may go--what precisely have you told the vicar? i must 
in fair know that.’ 

‘in fairness,’ she began ironically, and sudden broke off. her husband 
had turn the flame of the lamp low down in the vacant room behind 
them; the corridor was lit obscure by the chandelier far down in the 
hall below. a faint, inexplicable dread fell soft and cold on her 
heart. ‘have you no trust in me?’ she murmur a little bitterly. ‘i 
have simply told him the truth.’ 

they soft descended the stairs; she first, the dark figure following 
close behind her. 



chapter three 

mr bethany sat await them in the dining-room, a large, 
heavily-furnished room with a great benign looking-glass on the 
mantelpiece, a marble clock, and with rich old damask curtains. fleecy 
silver hair was all that was visible of their visitor when they entered. 
but mr bethany rose out of his chair when he heard them, and with a 
little jerk, turn sharp round. thus it was that the gold-spectacled 
vicar and lawford first confront each other, the one bright 
illuminated, the other frame in the gloom of the doorway. mr bethany’s 
first scrutiny was timid and courteous, but beneath it he try to be 
keen, and himself hasten round the table almost at a trot, to obtain, 
as delicate as possible, a close view. but lawford, have shut 
the door behind him, had gone straight to the fire and seat himself, 
leaning his face in his hands. mr bethany smile faintly, wave his hand 
almost as if in blessing, but certainly in peace, and tap mrs lawford 
into the chair upon the other side. but he himself remain standing. 

‘mrs lawford has, i declare, been telling family secrets,’ he began, 
and paused, peering. but there, you will forgive an old friend’s 
intrusion--this little confidence about a change, my dear fellow--about 
a ramble and a change?’ he sat down, put up his kind little pucker 
face and peer again at lawford, and then very hasty at his wife. 
but all her attention was centre on the bowed figure opposite to her. 
lawford respond to this cautious advance without raise his head. 

‘you do not wish me to repeat all that my wife tell me she has told 
you?’ 

‘dear me, no,’ said mr bethany cheerfully, ‘i wish nothing, nothing, old 
friend. you must not burden yourself with me. if i may be of any help, 
here i am.... oh, no, no....’ he paused, with blinking eyes, but wit 
still shrewd and alert. why doesn’t the man raise his head? he thought. 
a mere domestic dispute! 

‘i thought,’ he went on ruminatingly, ‘i thought on tuesday, yes, on 
tuesday, that you weren’t look quite the thing. indeed, i remark on 
it. but now, i understand from mrs lawford that the malady has taken 
a grave turn--eh, lawford, an heretic turn? i hear you have been 
wandering from the true fold.’ mr bethany lean forward with what might 
be describe as a very large smile in a very small compass. ‘and that, 
of course, entail instant retribution.’ he broke off solemnly. ‘i know 
widderstone churchyard well; a most verdant and beautiful spot. the late 
rector, a mr strickland, was a very old friend of mine. and his wife, 
dear good alicia, use to set out her babies, in the morning, to sleep 
and to play there, twenty, dear me, perhaps twenty-five years ago. but 
i did not know, my dear lawford, that you--’ and suddenly, without an 
instant’s warning, something seem to shout at him, ‘look, look! he is 
look at you!’ he stopped, faltered, and a slight warmth came into his 
face. ‘and and you were taken ill there?’ his voice had fallen flat and 
faint. 

‘i fell asleep--or something of that sort,’ came the stubborn reply. 

‘yes,’ said mr bethany, brightly, ‘so your wife was saying. “fell 
asleep,” so have i too--scores of times’; he beamed, with bead of sweat 
glisten on his forehead. ‘and then? i’m not, i’m not persisting?’ 

‘then i woke; refreshed, i think, as it seemed--i felt much better and 
came home.’ 

‘ah, yes,’ said his visitor. and after that there was a long, bright 
lit, intense pause; at the end of which lawford raise his face and 
again look firm at his friend. 

mr bethany was now a shrunken old man; he sat perfectly still, his head 
crane a little forward, and his veined hand clutch his bent, spare 
knees. 

there wasn’t the least sign of devilry, or out-facingness, or insole 
in that lean shadowy steady head; and yet he himself was compel to 
sidle his glance away, so much the face shook him. he closed his eyes, 
too, as a cat do after exchange too direct a scrutiny with human 
eyes. he put out towards, and withdrew, a grope hand from mrs lawford. 

‘is it,’ came a voice from somewhere, ‘is it a great change, sir? i 
thought perhaps i may have exaggerated--candle-light, you know.’ 

mr bethany remain still and silent, strive to entertain one thought 
at a time. his lip move as if he were talk to himself. and again 
it was lawford’s falter voice that broke the silence. ‘you see,’ 
he said, ‘i have never... no fit, or anything of that kind before. i 
remember on tuesday... oh yes, quite well. i did feel seedy, very. and 
we talked, didn’t we?--harvest festival, mrs wine’s flowers, the new 
offertory-bags, and all that. for god’s sake, vicar, it is not as bad 
as--as they make out?’ 

mr bethany woke with a start. he lean forward, and stretch out a 
long black wrinkle sleeve, just manage to reach far enough to tap 
lawford’s knee. ‘don’t worry, don’t worry,’ he said soothingly. ‘we 
believe, we believe.’ 

it was, none the less, a sheer act of faith. he took off his spectacles 
and took out his handkerchief. ‘what we must do, eh, my dear,’ he half

together. and later--we must have advice--medical advice; unless, as 
i very much suspect, it is merely a little quite temporary physical 
aberration. science, i am told, is making great strides, experimenting, 
grope after things which no sane man has ever dream of 
before--without being burn alive for it. what’s in a name? nerves, 
especially, lawford.’ 

mrs lawford sat perfectly still, absorbed listening, turning her face 
first this way, then that, to each speaker in turn. ‘that is what 
i thought,’ she said, and cast one fleeting glance across at the 
fireplace, ‘but--’ 

the little old gentleman turn sharp with half-blind eyes, and lip 
tight shut. ‘i think,’ he said, with a hind of austere humour, ‘i think, 
do you know, i see no “but.”’ he pause as if to catch the echo and 
added, ‘it’s our only course.’ he continue to polish round and round 
his glasses. mrs lawford rather magnificent rose. 

‘perhaps if i were to leave you together awhile? i shall not be far 
off. it is,’ she explained, as if into a huge vacuum, ‘it is a terrible 
visitation.’ she move grave round the table and very soft and 
firm closed the door after her. 

lawford took a deep breath. ‘of course.’ he said, ‘you realise my 
wife do not believe me. she thinks,’ he explain naively, as if to 
himself, ‘she think i am an imposter. goodness know what she do 
think. i can’t think much myself--for long!’ 

the vicar rub busy on. ‘i have found, lawford,’ he said smoothly, 
‘that in all real difficulty the only feasible plan is--is to face the 
main issue. the other right themselves. now, to take a plunge into your 
generosity. you have let me in far enough to make it impossible for me 
to get out--may i hear then exactly the whole story? all that i know 
now, so far as i could gather from your wife, poor soul, is of course 
inconceivable: that you went out one man and came home another. you will 
understand, my dear man, i am speaking, as it were, by rote. god has 
merciful ordered that the human brain works slowly; first the 
blow, hour afterwards the bruise. oh, dear me, that man hume--“on 
miracles”--positively amazing! so that too, please, you will be quite 
clear about. credo--not quia impossible est, but because you, lawford, 
have told me. now then, if it won’t be too wearisome to you, the whole 
story.’ he sat, lean and erect in his big chair, a hand rest 
loose on each knee, in one spectacles, in the other a dangle pocket 
handkerchief. and the dark, sallow, aquiline, formidable figure, with 
its oddly change voice, re-told the whole story from the beginning. 

‘you were aware then of nothing different, i understand, until you 
actually look into the glass?’ 

‘only vaguely. i mean that after waking i felt much better, more alert. 
and my thoughts--’ 

‘ah, yes, your thoughts?’ 

‘i hardly know--oh, clear as if i had had a real long rest. it was just 
like being a boy again. influenza dispirit one so.’ 

mr bethany gaze without stirring. ‘and yet, you know,’ he said, ‘i can 
hardly believe, i mean conceive, how--you have been taking no drugs, no 
quackery, lawford?’ 

‘i never dose myself,’ said lawford, with sombre pride. 

‘god bless me, that’s lawford to the echo,’ thought his visitor. ‘and 
before--?’ he went on gently; ‘i really cannot conceive, you see, how 
a mere fit could... before you sat down you were quite alone?’ he stuck 
out his head. ‘there was nobody with you?’ 

‘with me? oh no,’ came the soft answer. 

‘what had you been thinking of? in these days of faith-cures, and 
hypnotism, and telepathy, and subliminalities--why, the simple old world 
grow very confusing. but rarely, very rarely novel. you were thinking, 
you say; do you remember, perhaps, just the drift?’ 

‘well,’ began lawford ruminatingly, ‘there was something curious even 
then, perhaps. i remember, for instance, i knelt down to read an old 
tombstone. there was a little seat--no back. and an epitaph. the sun was 
just setting; some france name. and there was a long jagged crack in 
the stone, like the black line you know one see after lightning, i mean 
it’s as clear as that even now, in memory. oh yes, i remember. and then, 
i suppose, came the sleep--stupid, sluggish: and then; well, here i am.’ 

‘you are absolutely certain, then,’ persist mr bethany almost 
querulously, ‘there was no living creature near you? bless me, lawford, 
i see no unkind in believe what the bible itself relates. there 
are powers supernatural. saul, and so on. we are all convinced of that. 
no one?’ 

‘i remember distinctly,’ reply lawford, in a calm, stubborn voice, ‘i 
look up all around me, while i was kneel there, and there wasn’t a 
soul to be seen. because, you see, it even then occur to me that it 
would have look rather queer--my wandering about like that, i mean. 
facing me there were some cypress-trees, and beyond, a low sunken

topple down the hill, where i had just stroll down, and sunshine!’ 
he sudden threw up his hand. ‘oh, marvellous! stream in 
gold--flaming, like god’s own ante-chamber.’ 

there was a very pregnant pause. mr bethany shrunk back a little into 
his chair. his lip moved; he fold his spectacles. 

‘yes, yes,’ he said. and then very quiet he stole one mole-like look 
into his sidesman’s face. 

‘what is dr simon’s number?’ he said. lawford was gaze gloomy 
into the fire. ‘oh, annandale,’ he reply absently. ‘i don’t know the 
number.’ 

‘do you believe in him? your wife mention him. is he clever?’ 

‘oh, he’s new,’ said lawford; ‘old james was our doctor. he--he kill 
my father.’ he laugh out shamefacedly. 

‘a sound, lovable man,’ said mr bethany, ‘one of the kindest men i ever 
knew; and a very old friend of mine.’ 

and sudden the dark face turn with a shudder from the fire, and 
spoke in a low tremble voice. ‘only one thing--only one thing--my 
sanity, my sanity. if once i forget, who will believe me?’ he thrust his 
long lean finger beneath his coat. ‘and mad,’ he added; ‘i would soon 
die.’ 

mr bethany deliberate adjust his spectacles. ‘may i, may i 
experiment?’ he said boldly. there came a tap on the door. 

‘bless me,’ said the vicar, taking out his watch, ‘it is a quarter to 
twelve. ‘yes, yes, mrs lawford,’ he trot round to the door. ‘we are 
beginning to see light--a ray!’ 

‘but i--i can see in the dark,’ whisper lawford, as if at a cue, 
turning with an inscrutable smile to the fire. 

the vicar came again, wrap up in a little tight grey great-coat, and 
a white silk muffler. he look up unflinching into lawford’s face, 
and tear stood in his eyes. ‘patience, patience, my dear fellow,’ 
he repeated gravely, squeeze his hand. ‘and rest, complete rest, is

mrs lawford let him out. he shook his head once or twice, holding her

‘well,’ he said, ‘is the old--’ he dogged met the fixed eyes facing

‘yes,’ said sheila, ‘he’s gone.’ lawford sigh and turn round. ‘it’s 
useless talk now, sheila. no more questions. i cannot tell you how 
tired i am. and my head--’ 

‘what is wrong with your head?’ inquire his wife discreetly. 

the haggard face turn grave and patiently. ‘only one of my old 
headaches.’ he smiled, ‘my old bilious headaches--the hereditary lawford 
variety.’ but his voice fell low again. ‘we must get to bed.’ 

with a rather pretty and childish movement, sheila gently drew her hand 
across her silk skirts. ‘yes, dear,’ she said, ‘i have made up a bed for 
you in the large spare room. it is thorough aired.’ she came soft 
in, hasten over to a closed work-table that stood under the curtains, 
and open it. 

lawford watch her, utterly expressionless, utterly motionless. he 
open his mouth and shut it again, still watch his wife as she 
stoop with ridiculous too busy fingers, searching through her 
coloured silks. 

again he open his mouth. ‘yes,’ he said, and stalk slow towards 
the door. but there he paused. ‘god knows,’ he said, strange and 
meekly, ‘i am sorry, sorry for all this. you will forgive me, sheila?’ 

she look up swiftly. ‘it’s very tiresome, i can’t find anywhere,’ she 
murmured, ‘i can’t find anywhere the--the little red box key.’ 

lawford’s cheek turn more sallow than ever. ‘you are only pretend 
to look for it,’ he said, ‘to try me. we both know perfectly well the 
lock is broken. ada broke it.’ 

sheila let fall the lid; and yet for a while her eyes rove over it as 
if in violent search for something. then she turned: ‘i am so very glad 
the vicar was at home,’ she said brightly. ‘and mind, mind you rest, 
arthur. there’s nothing so bad but it might be worse.... oh, i can’t, i 
can’t bear it!’ she sat down in the chair and huddle her face between 
her hands, sob on and on, without a tear. 

lawford listen and stare solemnly. ‘whatever it may be, sheila, i 
will be loyal,’ he said. 

her sob hushed, and again cold horror crept over her. nobody in 
the whole world could have said that ‘i will be loyal’ quite like 
that--nobody but arthur. she stood up, pat her hair. ‘i don’t think 
my brain would bear much more. it’s useless to talk. if you will go up; 
i will put out the lamp.’ 



chapter four 

one solitary and tall candle burn on the great dressing-table. faint, 
solitary pictures broke the blank of each wall. the carpet was rich, 
the bed impressive, and the basin on the washstand as uninvite as the 
bed. lawford sat down on the edge of it in complete isolation. he sat 
without stirring, listen to his watch ticking in his pocket. the 
china clock on the chimney piece pointed cheerful to the hour of dawn. 
it was exactly, he compute carefully, five hour and seven minutes 
fast. not the slightest sound broke the stillness, until he heard, very, 
very soft and gradually, the key of his door turn in the oiled wards, 
and realize that he was a prisoner. 

women were strange creatures. how often he had heard that said, he 
thought lamely. he felt no anger, no surprise or resentment, at the 
trick. it was only to be expected. he could sit on till morning; easily 
till morning. he had never notice before how empty a well-furnished 
room could seem. it was his own room too; his best visitors’ room. his 
father-in-law had slept here, with his whiskers on that pillow. his 
wife’s most formidable aunt had been all night here, alone with these 
pictures. she certainly was... ‘but what are you do here?’ cry a 
voice sudden out of his reverie. 

he start up and stretch himself, and taking out the neat little 
packet that the maid had brought from the chemist’s, he drew up a chair, 
and sat down once more in front of the glass. he sigh vacantly, rose 
and lift down from the wall above the fireplace a tint photograph 
of himself that sheila had had enlarge about twelve years ago. it was 
a brighter, younger, hairier, but unmistakable the same dull indolent 
lawford who had venture into widderstone churchyard that afternoon. the 
cheek was a little plumper, the eyes not quite so full-lidded, the hair 
a little more precisely parted, the upper lip grace with a small blonde 
moustache. he tilt the portrait into the candlelight, and compare it 
with this reflection in the glass of what had come out of widderstone, 
feature with feature, with perfect composure and extreme care, then he 
laid down the massive frame on the table, and gaze quiet at the tiny 
packet. 

it was to be a day of queer experiences. he had never before realize 
with how many miracle mere everyday life is besieged. here in this 
small punctilious packet lay a sesame--a power of transformation beside 
which the transformation of that rather flaccid face of the noonday into 
this tense, sinister face of midnight was but as a moving from house 
to house--a change just as irrevocable and complete, and yet so very 
normal. which should it be, that, or--his face lift itself once more 
to the ice-like gloom of the looking-glass-that, or this? 

it simply gaze back with a kind of quizzical pity on its lean features 
under the scrutiny of eyes so deep, so meaningful, so desolate, and yet 
so indomitable courageous. in the brain behind them a slow and stolid 
argument was in progress; the one baffling reply on the one side to 
every appeal on the other being still simply. ‘what dream may come?’ 

those eyes surely knew something of dreams, else, why this violent and 
stubborn endeavour to keep awake. 

lawford did indeed once actually frame the question, ‘but who the 
devil are you?’ and it really seem the eyes perceptible widen or 
brightened. the mere vexation of his unparalleled position. sheila’s 
pathetic incredulity, his old vicar’s laborious kindness, the tiresome 
network of experience into which he would be drag struggle on the 
morrow, and on the morrow after that, and after that--the thought of 
all these things fade for the moment from his mind, lost if not their 
significance, at least their instancy. 

he simply sat face to face with the sheer difficulty of living on 
at all. he even conclude in a kind of lethargy that if nothing had 
occurred, no ‘change,’ he might still be sitting here, arthur rennet 
lawford, in his best visitor’s room, decide between inscrutable life 
and just--death. he supposed he was tired out. his thought hadn’t even 
the energy to complete themselves. none care but himself and this--this 
silence. 

‘but what do it all mean?’ the insistent voice he was get to know 
so well began tedious inquiring again. and every time he raise 
his eyes, or, rather, as in many case it seemed, his eyes raise 
themselves, they saw this haunting face there--a face he no long 
bitter rebel at, nor dim with scrutiny, but a face that was 
becoming a kind of hold on life, even a kind of refuge, an ally. it was 
a face that might have come out of a rather flashy book; or such as is 
revere on the stage. ‘a rotten bad face,’ he whisper at it in his own 
familiar slang, after some such abrupt encounter; a fearless, packed, 
daring, fascinating face, with even--what?--a spice of genius in it. 
whose the devil’s face was it? what on earth was the matter?... ‘brazen 
it out,’ a jubilant thought cry suddenly; ‘follow it up; play the 
game! give me just one opening. think--think what i’ve risked!’ 

and all these voice thought lawford, in deadly lassitude, meant only 
one thing--insanity. a blazing, impotent indignation seize him. he 
lean near, peer as it were out of a red dusky mist. he snatch up 
the china candlestick, and poised it above the sardonic reflection, as 
if to throw. then slowly, with infinite pains, he drew back from the 
glass and replace the candlestick on the table; stuff his paper 
packet into his pocket, took off his boots and threw himself on to the 
bed. in a little while, in the faint, still light, he open drowsy 
wonder eyes. `poor old thing!’ his voice murmured, ‘poor old sheila!’ 



chapter five

foreigner. he’s’--his voice sank almost to a whisper--‘he’s no dark

let me have something solid. i’m not ill--in that way. all i want is 
peace and quiet, time to think. let me fight it out alone. it’s been 
sprung on me. the worst’s not over. but i’ll win through; wait! and 
if not--well, you shall not suffer, sheila. don’t be afraid. there are 
other ways out.’ 

sheila broke down. ‘any one would think to hear you talk, that i was 
perfectly heartless. i told ada to be most careful about the cornflour. 
and as for other ways out, it’s a positively wicked thing to say to me 
when i’m nearly distracted with trouble and anxiety. what motive could 
you have had for loiter in an old cemetery? and in an east wind! it’s 
useless for me to remain here, arthur, to be accused of every horrible 
thing that come into a morbid imagination. i will leave you, as you 
suggest, in peace.’ 

‘one moment, sheila,’ answer the muffle voice. ‘i have accused you 
of nothing. if you knew all; if you could read my thoughts, you would 
be surprised, perhaps, at my--but never mind that. on the other hand, i 
really do think it would be better for the present to discuss the thing 
no more. to-day is friday. give this miserable face a week. talk it over 
with bethany if you like. but i forbid’--he struggle up in bed, 
sallow and sinister--‘i flatly forbid, please understand, any other 
interference till then. afterwards you must do exactly as you please. 
send round the town crier! but till then, silence!’ 

sheila with raise head confront him. ‘this, then, is your gratitude. 
so be it. silence, no doubt! until it’s too late to take action. until 
you have worm your way in, and think you are safe. to have believed! 
where is my husband? that is what i am ask you now. when and how you 
have learned his secret god only knows, and your conscience! but he 
always was a simpleton at heart. i warn you, then. until next thursday 
i consent to say nothing provided you remain quiet; make no disturbance, 
no scandal here. the servant and all who inquire shall simply be told 
that my husband is confine to his room with--with a nervous breakdown, 
as you have yourself so glib suggested. i am at your mercy, i own it. 
the vicar believe your preposterous story--with his spectacles off. 
you would convince anybody with the wicked cunning with which you have 
cajole and wheedle him, with which you have deceive and fool a 
foreign doctor. but you will not convince me. you will not convince 
alice. i have friends in the world, though you may not be aware of it, 
who will not be quite so apt to believe any cock-and-bull story you may 
see fit to invent. that is all i have to say. to-night i tell the vicar 
all that i have just told you. and from this moment, please, we are 
strangers. i shall come into the room no more than necessity dictates. 
on friday we resume our real parts. my husband--arthur--to--to connive 
at... phh!’ 

rage had transfigure her. she scarcely heard her own words. they pour 
out senselessly, monotonously, one calling up another, as if from the 
lip of a cassandra. lawford sank back into bed, clutch the sheet 
with both lean hands. he took a deep breath and shut his mouth. 

‘it remind me, sheila,’ he began arduously, ‘of our first quarrel 
before we were married, the evening after your aunt rose die at 
llandudno--do you remember? you threw open the window, and i think--i 
save your life.’ a pause followed. then a queer, almost inarticulate 
voice added, ‘at least, i am afraid so.’ 

a cold and awful quiet fell on sheila’s heart. she stare fixedly 
at the tuft of dark hair, the only visible sign of her husband, on the 
pillow. then, taking up the basin of cold cornflour, she left the room. 
in a quarter of an hour she reappear carry a tray, with ham and 
egg and coffee and honey inviting displayed. she laid it down. 

‘there is only one other question,’ she said, with perfect 
composure--‘that of money. your signature as it appear on the--the 
document drawn up this morning, would, of course, be quite useless on 
a cheque. i have taken all the money i could find; it is in safety. 
you may, however, conceivable be in need of some yourself; here is five 
pounds. i have my own cheque-book, and shall therefore have no need 
to consider the question again for--for the present. so far as you are 
concerned, i shall be guide solely by mr bethany. he will, i do not 
doubt, take full responsibility.’ 

‘and may the lord have mercy on my soul!’ utter a stifled, unfamiliar 
voice from the bed. mrs lawford stooped. ‘arthur!’ she cry faintly, 
‘arthur!’ 

lawford raise himself on his elbow with a sigh that was very near to 
being a sob. ‘oh, sheila, if you’d only be your real self! what is the 
use of all this pretence? just consider my position a little. the fear 
and horror are not all on your side. you call me arthur even then. i’d 
willing do anything you wish to save you pain; you know that. can’t we 
be friends even in this--this ghastly--won’t you, sheila?’ 

mrs lawford drew back, struggle with a doubtful heart. 

‘i think,’ she said, `it would be better not to discuss that now.’ 

the rest of the morning lawford remain in solitude. 



chapter six 

there were three books in the room--jeremy taylor’s ‘holy living and 
dying,’ a volume of the quiver, and a little gilded book on wildflowers. 
he read in vain. he lay and listen to the uproar of his thought on 
which an occasional sound--the drone of a fly, the cry of a milkman, 
the noise of a passing van--obtruded from the workaday world. the pale 
gold sunlight edge soft over the bed. he ate up everything on his 
tray. he even, on the shoal of nightmare, dream awhile. but by and 
by as the hour wheel slow on he grew less calm, less strenuous 
resolve on lie there inactive. every sparrow that twitter cry 
reveille through his brain. he long with an ardour strange to his 
temperament to be up and doing. 

what if his misfortune was, as he had in the excitement of the moment 
suggest to sheila, only a morbid delusion of mind; share too in part 
by sheer force of his absurd confession? even if he was go mad, who 
know how peaceful a release that might not be? could his shrewd old 
vicar have implicit believe in him if the change were as complete 
as he supposed it? he flung off the bedclothes and lock the door. 
he dress himself, noticing, he fancied, with a deadly revulsion 
of feeling, that his coat was a little too short in the sleeves, his 
waistcoat too loose. in the midst of his dressing came sheila bring 
his luncheon. ‘i’m sorry,’ he call out, stoop quick beside the 
bed, ‘i can’t talk now. please put the tray down.’ 

about half an hour afterwards he heard the outer door close, and peep 
from behind the curtains saw his wife go out. all was drowsy quiet in 
the house. he devour his lunch like a schoolboy. that finished to the 
last crumb, without a moment’s delay he cover his face with a towel, 
lock the door behind him, put the key in his pocket, and ran lightly 
downstairs. he stuff the towel into an ulster pocket, put on a soft, 
wide-brimmed hat, and noiseless let himself out. then he turn 
with an almost hysterical delight and ran--ran like the wind, without 
pausing, without thinking, straight on, up one turning, down another, 
until he reach a broad open common, thick wooded, sprinkle with 
gorse and hazel and may, and faint purple with fade heather. there 
he flung himself down in the beautiful sunlight, among the yellow 
bracken, to recover his breath. 

he lay there for many minutes, thinking almost with composure. flight, 
it seemed, had for the moment quieten the demand of that other feeble 
struggle personality which was beginning to insinuate itself into his 
consciousness, which had so miraculous broken in and taken possession 
of his body. he would not think now. all he need was a little quiet 
and patience before he threw off for good and all his right to be free, 
to be his own master, to call himself sane. 

he scramble up and turn his face towards the westere sun. what was 
there in the still of its beautiful splendour that seem to sharpen 
his horror and difficulty, and yet to stir him to such a daring and 
devilry as he had never known since he was a boy? there was little sound 
of life; somewhere an unknown bird was singing, and a few late bee were 
drone in the bracken. all these years he had, like an old blind horse, 
stolid plod round and round in a dull self-set routine. and now, 
just when the spirit had come for rebellion, the mood for a harmless 
truancy, there had fallen with them too this hideous enigma. he sat 
there with the dusky silhouette of the face that was now drench with 
sunlight in his mind’s eye. he set off again up the stony incline. 

why not walk on and on? in time real wholesome weary would come; he 
could sleep at ease in some pleasant wayside inn, without once meeting 
the eyes that stood as it were like a window between himself and a 
shrewd incredulous scoff world that would turn him into a monstrosity 
and his story into a fable. and in a little while, perhaps in three 
days, he would awaken out of this engrossing nightmare, and know he 
was free, this black dog gone from his back, and (as the old saying 
express it without any one dream what it really meant) his own 
man again. how astonish sheila would be; how warm she would welcome 
him!... oh yes, of course she would. 

he came again to a standstill. no voice answer him out of that 
illimitable gold and blue. nothing seem aware of him. but as he stood 
there, doubtful as cain on the outskirts of the unknown, he caught the 
sound of a footfall on the lonely and stone-strewn path. 

the ground slope steeple away to the left, and slow mount the 
hillside came mildly on an old lady he knew, a miss sinnet, an old 
friend of his mother’s. there was just such a little seat as that 
other he knew so well, on the brow of the hill. he made his way to it, 
intend to sit quiet there until the little old lady had pass by. 
up and up she came. her large bonnet appeared, and then her mild white 
face, inclined a little towards him as she ascended. evidently this very 
seat was her goal; and evasion was impossible. evasion!... memory rush 
back and set his pulse beating. he turn bold to the sun, and the 
old lady, with a brief glance into his face, compose herself at the 
other end of the little seat. she gaze out of a gentle reverie into the 
golden valley. and so they sat a while. and almost as if she had 
felt the bond of acquaintance between them, she presently sighed, and 
address him: ‘a very, very, beautiful view, sir.’ 

lawford paused, then turn a gloomy, earnest face, gilded with 
sunshine. ‘beautiful, indeed,’ he said, ‘but not for me. no, miss 
sinnet, not for me.’ 

the old lady grave turn and examine the aquiline profile. ‘well, i 
confess,’ she remark urbanely, ‘you have the advantage of me.’ 

lawford smile uneasily. ‘believe me, it is little advantage.’ 

‘my sight,’ said miss sinnet precisely, ‘is not so good as i might wish; 
though better perhaps than i might have hoped; i fear i am not much 
wiser; your face is still unfamiliar to me.’ 

‘it is not unfamiliar to me,’ said lawford. whose trickery was this? he 
thought, putt such affected stuff into his mouth. 

a faint lighten of pity came into the silvery and scrupulous 
countenance. ‘ah, dear me, yes,’ she said courteously. 

lawford rest a lean hand on the seat. ‘and have you,’ he asked, ‘not 
the least recollection in the world of my face?’ 

‘now really,’ she said, smile blandly, ‘is that quite fair? think of 
all the score and score of face in seventy long years; and how very 
treacherous memory is. you shall do me the service of remind me of 
one whose name has for the moment escape me.’ 

‘i am the son of a very old friend of yours, miss sinnet,’ said lawford 
quiet ‘a friend that was once your schoolfellow at brighton.’ 

‘well, now,’ said the old lady, grasping her umbrella, ‘that is 
undoubted a clue; but then, you see, all but one of the friends of my 
girlhood are dead; and if i have never had the pleasure of meeting her 
son, unless there is a decided resemblance, how am i to recollect her by 
look at him?’ 

‘there is, i believe, a likeness,’ said lawford. 

she nod her great bonnet at him with gentle amusement. ‘you are 
insistent in your fancy. well, let me think again. the last to leave 
me was fanny urquhart, that was--let me see--last october. now you are 
certainly not fanny urquhart’s son,’ she stoop austerely, ‘for she 
never had one. last year, too, i heard that my dear, dear mrs jameson 
was dead. her i hadn’t met for many, many years. but, if i may venture 
to say so, yours is not a scotland face; and she not only married a 
scotland husband, but was herself a dunbar. no, i am still at a loss.’ 

a miserable strife was in her chance companion’s mind, a strife of anger 
and recrimination. he turn his eyes weary to the fast decline sun. 
‘you will forgive my persistency, but i assure you it is a matter of 
life or death to me. is there no one my face recalls? my voice?’ 

miss sinnet drew her long lip together, her eyebrow lift with the

that pale blue arch of evening, these two human being confront

lawford gulp and turn away. he hardly knew what this surge of

echo of an unholy joy? his mind for a moment became confuse as if

bennett, i fear i set you too difficult a task.’ 

the old lady drew abrupt in, like a trustful and gentle snail into its 
shock house. ‘bennett, sir; but my name is not bennett.’ 

and again lawford accept the miserable prompting. ‘not bennett!... how 
can i ever then apologise for so frantic a mistake?’ 

the little old lady took firm hold of her umbrella. she did not answer 
him. ‘the likeness, the likeness!’ he began unctuously, and stopped, 
for the glance that dwelt fleeting on him was cold with the formidable 
dignity and displeasure of age. he raise his hat and turn miserably

fantastic foolery of mind was master him? he cast a hurried look 
over his shoulder at the kindly and offend old figure sitting there, 
solitary, on the little seat, in her great bonnet, with back turn 
resolute upon him--the friend of his dead mother who might have prove 
in his need a friend indeed to him. and he had by this insane caprice 
hopeless estrange her. 

she would remember this face well enough now, he thought bitterly, 
and would take her place among his quiet enemies, if ever the day of 
reckoning should come. it was scandalous, it was banal to have abuse 
her trust and courtesy. oh, it was hopeless to struggle any more! the 
fates were against him. they had play him a trick. he was to be their 
transitory sport, as many a better man he could himself recollect had 
been before him. he would go home and give in; let sheila do with him 
what she pleased. no one but a lunatic could have act as he had, with 
just that frantic hint of method so remarkable in the insane. 

he left the common. a lamplighter was lighting the lamps. a thin 
evening haze was on the air. if only he had stay at home that fateful 
afternoon! who, what had induce him, entice him to venture out? and 
even with the thought well up into his mind an intense desire to go 
to the old green time-worn churchyard again; to sit there contented 
alone, where none heed the completest metamorphosis, down beside the 
yew-trees. what a fool he had been. there alone, of course, lay his only 
possible chance of recovery. he would go to-morrow. perhaps sheila had 
not yet discover his absence; and there would be no difficulty in 
repeating so successful a stratagem. 

remembrance of his miserable mistake, of miss sinnet, faint return 
to him as he swift mount the steps to his porch. poor old lady. he 
would make amends for his discourtesy when he was quite himself again. 
she should some day hear, perhaps, his infinite tragic, infinite 
comic experience from his own lips. he would take her some flowers, some 
old keepsake of his mother’s. what would he not do when the old mood 
and brains of the stupid arthur lawford, whom he had appreciate so 
little and so superficially, came back to him. 

he ran up the steps and stop dead, his hand in his pocket, chill 
and aghast. sheila had taken his keys. he stood there, daze and still, 
beneath the dim yellow of his own fanlight; and once again that inward 
spring flew back. ‘brazen it out; brazen it out! knock and ring!’ 

he knock flamboyantly, and rang. 

there came a quiet step and the door opened. ‘dr simon, of course, has 
called?’ he inquire suavely. 

‘yes, sir.’ 

‘ah, and gone’--as i feared. and mrs lawford?’ 

‘i think mrs lawford is in, sir.’ 

lawford put out a detain hand. ‘we will not disturb her; we will not 
disturb her. i can find my way up; oh yes, thank you!’ 

but ada still pale barred the way. ‘i think, sir,’ she said, ‘mrs

ada turn quiet small eyes.

and in this infinitesimal advantage of time dr ferguson had seize his

‘that little exhibition of finesse was part of our agreement, i

not much light was in the large room, and there was something so

and a voice call soft at the door, ‘mother, are you there? is father 
awake? may i come in?’ 

in a flash the memory return to her; twenty-four hour ago she was 
ask that very question of this unspeakable figure that sat hunched-up

‘one moment, dear,’ she called. and add in a very low voice, ‘come 
here!’ 

lawford look up. ‘what?’ he said. 

‘perhaps, perhaps,’ she whispered, ‘it isn’t quite so bad.’

child to go away.’ 

she paused. ‘are you there, alice? would you mind, father says, wait 
a little? he is so very tired.’ 

‘too tired to.... oh, very well, mother.’ 

mrs lawford open the door, and call after her, ‘is jimmie gone?’ 

‘oh, yes, hours.’

‘i couldn’t get a carriage at the station. he carry my dressing-bag;

‘if you please, ma’am,’ she said, ‘i have been waiting, as you told me,

‘i really should have thought, ada,’ sheila began, then caught back the

‘poor boy!’ she said at last, lightly touching the loose sleeve; ‘be

he put out his hand as if to touch her. ‘forgive you!’ he said humbly,

‘then, to-night,’ at last began sheila wearily, drawing back, ‘we

querulously. 

‘if you please, ma’am, mr critchett say he doesn’t know dr ferguson, 
his name’s not in the directory, and there must be something wrong with 
the message, and he’s sorry, but he must have it in writing because
