what shall i do, if you please?’ 

still look at her husband. sheila listen quiet to the end, and 
then, as if in inarticulate disdain, she deliberate shrug her 
shoulders, and went out to play her part unaided. 



chapter seven 

her husband turn weary once more, and drawing up a chair sat down in 
front of the cold grate. he realise that sheila thought him as much 
of a fool now as she had for the moment thought him an impostor, or 
something worse, the night before. that was at least something gained. 
he realised, too, in a vague way that the exuberance of mind that had 
practically invent dr ferguson, and outrage miss sinnet, had quite 
sudden flicker out. it was astonishing, he thought, with gaze 
fixed innocent on the black coals, that he should ever have done such 
things. he detest that kind of ‘rot’; that jaunty theatrical pose so 
many men pride their jackdaw brains on. 

and he sat quite still, like a cat at a cranny, listening, as it 
were, for the faintest remotest stir that might hint at any return of 
this--activity. it was the first really sane moment he had had since 
the ‘change.’ whatever it was that had happen at widderstone was now 
distinct weaken in effect. why, now, perhaps? he stole a thievish 
look over his shoulder at the glass, and cautious drew finger and 
thumb down that beak nose. then he really quiet smiled, a smile he 
felt this abominable facial caricature was quite unused to, the superior 
lawford smile of guileless contempt for the fanatical, the fantastic, 
and the bizarre: he wouldn’t have sat with his feet on the fender before 
a burnt-out fire. 

and the animosity of that ‘he,’ utter only just under his breath, 
surprise even himself. it actually did seem as if there were a chance; 
if only he kept cool and collected. if the whole mind of a man was bent 
on being one thing, surely no power on earth, certainly not on earth, 
could for long compel him to look another, any more (followed the 
resplendent thought) than vice versa. 

that, in fact, was the trick that had been in fitful fashion play him 
since yesterday. obviously, and apart altogether from his promise to 
sheila, the best possible thing he could do would be to walk quiet 
over to widderstone to-morrow and like a child that has lost a penny, 
just make the attempt to reverse the process: look at the graves, read 
the inscription on the weather-beaten stones, compose himself once more 
to sleep on the little seat. 

magic, witchcraft, possession, and all that--well, mr bethany might 
prefer to take it on the authority of the bible if it was his duty. but 
it was at least mainly old testament stuff, like polygamy, joshua, and 
the ‘unclean beasts.’ the ‘unclean beasts.’ it was simply, as simon had 
said, mainly an affair of the nerves, like indian jugglery. he had heard 
of dozen of such cases, or similar cases. and it was hardly likely 
that case even remotely like his own would be much brag about, or 
advertised. all those mysterious ‘disappearances,’ too, which one 
read about so repeatedly? what of them? even now, he felt (and glance 
swift behind him at the fancy), it would be better to think as soft 
as possible, not to hope too openly, certainly not to triumph in the 
least degree, just in case of--well--listeners. 

he would wrap up too. and he wouldn’t tell sheila of the project till 
he had come safe back. what an excellent joke it would be to confess 
meek to his escapade, and to be scolded, and then sudden to reveal 
himself. he sat back and gaze with an almost malignant animosity at the 
face in the portrait, comely and plump. 

an inarticulate, unfathomable depression roll back on him, like a 
mist out of the sea. he hasty undressed, put watch and door-key and 
critchett’s powder under his pillow, paused, vacant ruminated, and 
then replace the powder in his waistcoat pocket, said his prayers, and 
got shiver to bed. he did not feel hurt at sheila’s leave him like 
this. so long as she really believe in him. and now--alice was home. he 
listened, try not to shiver, for her voice; and sometimes heard, he 
fancied, the clear note. it was this beastly influenza that made him 
feel so cold and lifeless. but all would soon come right--that is, if 
only that face, luminous against the floating dark within, would not 
appear the instant he closed his eyes. 

but legion of dream are influenza’s allies. he fell into a chill doze, 
heard voice innumerable, and one above the rest, shouting them down, 
until there fell a lull. and another, as it were, from afar said quite 
clearly and distinctly, ‘but surely, my dear, you have heard the story 
of the poor old charwoman who talk greece in her delirium? a little 
school france need not alarm us.’ and lawford open his eyes again on 
mr bethany standing at his bed. 

‘tt, tt! there, i’ve been and wake him. and yet they say men make such 
excellent nurse in time of war. but you see, lawford, what did i tell 
you? wasn’t i now an infallible prophet? your wife has been give me a 
most glowing account. quite your old self, she tell me, except for just 
this--this touch of facial paralysis. and i think, do you know’ (the 
kind old creature stoop over the bed, but still, lawford notice 
bitterly, still without his spectacles)--‘yes, i really think there is 
a decided improvement. not quite so--drawn. we must make haste slowly. 
wedderburn, you know, believe profound in simon; he pull his 
wife through a dangerous confinement. and here’s pill and tonic and 
liniments--a whole chemist’s shop. oh, we are get on swimmingly.’ 

flamelight was flicker in the candle dusk. lawford turn his head 
and saw sheila’s coiled, beautiful hair in the firelight. 

‘you haven’t told alice?’ he asked. 

‘my dear good man,’ said mr bethany, ‘of course we haven’t. you shall 
tell her yourself on monday. what an incredible tradition it will be! 
but you mustn’t worry; you mustn’t even think. and no more of these 
jaunts, eh? that ferguson business--that was too bad. what are we go 
to do with the fellow now we have create him? he will come home to 
roost--mark my words. and as likely as not down the vicarage chimney. 
i wouldn’t have believe it of you, my dear fellow.’ he beamed, but 
looked, none the less, very lean and fagged and depressed. 

‘how did the wedding go off?’ lawford manage to think of inquiring. 

‘oh, a1,’ said mr bethany. ‘i’ve just been describe it to alice--the 
bride, her bridegroom, mother, aunts, cake, presents, finery, blushes, 
tears, and everything that was hers. we’ve been in fits, haven’t we, mrs 
lawford? and alice say i’m a worth in a clerical collar--didn’t she? 
and that it’s only art that has kept me out of an apron. now look here; 
quiet, quiet, quiet; no excitement, no pranks. what is there to worry 
about, pray? and now little dorrit’s down with influenza too. and craik 
and i will have double work to do. well, well; good-bye, my dear. god 
bless you, lawford. i can’t tell you how relieved, how unspeakable 
relieved i am to find you so much--so much better. feed him up, my other 
dear; body and mind and soul and spirit. and there go the bell. i must 
have a biscuit. i’ve swallow nothing but a cupid in plaster of paris 
since breakfast. goodnight; we shall miss you both--both.’ 

but when sheila returned, her husband was sunk again into a quiet sleep, 
from which not even the many question she fret to put to him seem 
weighty enough to warrant his disturbance. 

so when lawford again open his eyes he found himself lie wide awake, 
clear and refreshed, and eager to get up. but upon the air lay the still 
hush of early morning. he try in vain to catch back sleep again. 
a distant shred of dream still float in his mind, like a cloud 
at evening. he rarely dreamed, but certainly something immensely 
interesting had but a moment ago elude him. he sat up and look at 
the clear red cinders and their maze of grottoes. he got out of bed and 
peep through the blinds. to the east and opposite to him garden and 
an apple-orchard lay, and there in strange liquid tranquill hung the 
morning star, and rose, rifling into the dusk of night, the first grey 
of dawn. the street beneath its autumn leaves was vacant, charmed, 
deserted. 

hardly since childhood had lawford seen the dawn unless over his winter 
breakfast-table. very much like a child now he stood gaze out of his 
bow-window--the child whom time’s busy robins had long ago cover over 
with the leaves of numberless hours. a vague exultation fume up into 
his brain. still on the border of sleep, he unlock the great wardrobe 
and took out an old fade purple and crimson dressing-gown that had 
belong to his grandfather, the chief glory of every christmas charade. 
he pull the cowl-like hood over his head and strode majestic over 
to the looking-glass. 

he look in there a moment on the strange face, like a child dismay 
at its own excitement, and a fit of sob that was half uncontrollable 
laughter swept over him. he threw off the hood and turn once more to 
the window. consciousness had flood back indeed. what would sheila 
have said to see him there? the unearthly beauty and stillness, and 
man’s small labours, garden and wall and roof-tree idle and smokeless in 
the light of daybreak--there seem to be some half-told secret between 
them. what had life done with him to leave a reality so clouded? he 
put on his slippers, and, gently opening the door, crept with extreme 
caution up the stairs. at a long, narrow landing window he confront 
a panorama of starry night-gardens, slope orchards; and beyond them 
fields, hills, orion, the dogs, in the clear and cloudless darkness. 

‘my god, how beautiful!’ a voice whispered. and a cock crow misty 
afar. he stood staring like a child into the wintry bright of a 
pastry-cook’s. then once more he crept stealthy on. he stoop and 
listen at a closed door, until he fancy that above the beating of 
his own heart he could hear the breathing of the sleeper within. then, 
taking firm hold of the handle with both hands, he slow noiseless 
turn it, and peep in on alice. 

the moon was long past her faint shining here. the blind was down. and 
yet it was not pitch dark. he stood with eyes fixed, waiting. then he 
edge soft forward and knelt down beside the bed. he could hear her 
breathing now: long, low, quiet, unhastening--the miracle of life. he 
could just dim discern the dark of her hair against the pillow. 
some long-sealed spring of tender seem to rise in his heart with a 
grief and an ache he had never known before. here at least he could find 
a little peace, a brief pause, however futile and stupid all his hope 
of the night had been. he leant his head on his hand on the counterpane 
and refuse to think. he felt a quick tremor, a startle movement, and 
knew that eyes wide open with fear were strive to pierce the gloom 
between them. 

‘there, there, dearest,’ he said in a low whisper, ‘it’s only me, only 
me.’ he stroke the narrow hand and gaze into the shadowiness. her 
finger lay quiet and passive in his, with that strange sense of 
immaterial that sleep bring to the body. 

‘you, you!’ she answer with a deep sigh. ‘oh, dearest, how you 
frightened me. what is wrong? why have you come? are you worse, dearest, 
dearest?’ 

he kiss her hand. ‘no, alice, not worse. i couldn’t sleep, that was 
all.’ 

‘oh, and i came so utterly miserable to bed because you would not see 
me. and mother would tell me only so very little. i didn’t even know 
you had been ill.’ she pressed his hand between her own. ‘but this, you 
know, is very, very naughty--you will catch cold, you bad thing. what 
would mother say?’ 

‘i think we mustn’t tell her, dear. i couldn’t help it; i felt much i 
want to see you. i have been rather miserable.’ 

‘why?’ she said, stroke his hand from wrist to fingertip with one 
soft finger. ‘you mustn’t be miserable. you and me have never done such 
a thing before; have we? was it that wretched old flu?’ 

it was too dark in the little fragrant room even to see her face so 
close to his own. and yet he feared. ‘dr simon,’ she went on softly, 
‘said it was. but isn’t your voice a little hoarse, and it sound so 
melancholy in the dark. and oh’--she squeeze his wrist--‘you have grown 
so thin! you do frighten me. whatever should i do if you were really 
ill? and it was so odd, dear. when first i woke i seem to be still 
strain my eyes in a dream, at such a curious, haunting face--not very 
nice. i am glad, i am glad you were here.’ 

‘what was the dream-face like?’ came the mutter question. 

‘dark and sharp, and rather dwelling eyes; you know those long face one 
see in dreams: like a hawk, like a conjuror’s.’ 

like a conjuror’s!--it was the first unguarded and ungarbled criticism. 
‘perhaps, dear, if you find my voice different, and my hand shrunk up, 
you will find my face changed, too--like a conjuror’s.... what then?’ 

she laugh gaily and tenderly. ‘you silly silly; i should love you more 
than ever. your hand are icy cold. i can’t warm them nohow.’ 

lawford held tight his daughter’s hand. ‘you do love me, alice? you 
would not turn against me, whatever happened? ah, you shall see, you 
shall see.’ a sudden burning hope sprang up in him. surely when all was 
well again, these last few hour would not have been spent in vain. 
like the shadow of death they had been, against whose dark the green 
familiar earth seem beautiful as the plain of paradise. had he but 
realize before how much he love her--what years of life had been 
waste in leave it all unsaid! he came back from his reverie to find 
his hand wet with her tears. he stroke her hair, and touched gently her 
eyelid without speaking. 

‘you will let me come in to-morrow?’ she pleaded; ‘you won’t keep me 
out?’ 

‘ah, but, dear, you must remember your mother. she get so anxious, and 
every word the doctor say is law. how would you like me to come again 
like this, perhaps?--like santa claus?’ 

‘you know how i love have you,’ she said, and stopped. ‘but--but...’ 
he lean closer. ‘yes, yes, come,’ she said, clutch his hand and 
hiding her eyes; ‘it is only my dream--that horrible, dwelling face in 
the dream; it frightened me so.’ 

lawford rose very slow from his knees. he could feel in the dark his 
brow drawn down; there came a low, sullen beating on his ear; he saw 
his face as it were in dim outline against the dark. rage and rebellion 
surge up in him; even his love could be turn to bitterness. well, two 
could play at any game! alice sprang up in bed and caught his sleeve. 
‘dearest, dearest, you must not be angry with me now!’ 

he flung himself down beside the bed. anger, resentment die away. ‘you 
are all i have left,’ he said. 

he stole back, as he had come, in the clear dawn to his bedroom. 

it was not five yet. he put a few more coal on his fire and blew out 
the night-light, and lay down. but it was impossible to rest, to remain 
inactive. he would go down and search for that first volume of quain. 
hallucination, influenza, insanity--why, sheila must have purposely 
mislaid it. a rather formidable figure he looked, descend the stairs 
in the grey dusk of daybreak. the breakfast-room was at the back of 
the house. he tilt the blind, and a faint light flow in from the 
change colours of the sky. he open the glass door of the little 
bookcase to the right of the window, and ran eye and finger over the few 
row of books. but as he stood there with his back to the room, just as 
the shadow of a bird’s wing float across the moonlight of a pool, he 
became sudden conscious that something, somebody had pass across the 
doorway, and in passing had look in on him. 

he stood motionless, listening; but no sound broke the morning 
slumbrousness, except the faraway warble of a thrush in the first 
light. so sudden and transitory had been the experience that it seem 
now to be illusory; yet it had so caught him up, it had with so furtive 
and sinister a quiet broken in on his solitude, that for a moment he 
dare not move. a cold, indefinite sensation stole over him that he was 
being watched; that some dim, evil presence was behind him bide its 
time, patient and stealthy, with eyes fixed unmoving on him where 
he stood. but, watch and wait as silent as he might, only the day 
broaden at the window, and at last a narrow ray of sunlight stole 
tremble up into the dusky bowl of the sky. 

at any rate quain was found, with all the ill of life, from a to i; and 
lawford turn back to his bondage with the book under his arm. 



chapter eight 

the sabbath, pale with september sunshine, and monotonous with chime 
bells, had pass languid away. dr simon had come and gone, optimistic 
and urbane, yet with a faint inward dissatisfaction over a patient 
behind whose taciturn a hint of mockery and subterfuge seem to 
lurk. even mrs lawford had appear to share her husband’s reticence. 
but dr simon had happen on other case in his experience where tact 
was require rather than skill, and time than medicine. 

the voice and footsteps, even the frou-frou of worship go to 
church, the voice and footstep of worship return from church, 
had float up to the patient’s open window. sunlight had drawn across 
his room in one pale beam, and vanished. a few caller had called. 
hothouse flowers, waxen and pale, had been left with message of 
sympathy. even dr critchett had respectful and discreet made 
inquiry on his way home from chapel. 

lawford had spent most of his time in pace to and fro in his soft 
slippers. the very monotony had ease his mind. now and again he had 
lain motionless, with his face to the ceiling. he had doze and had 
awakened, cold and torpid with dream. he had hardly been aware of 
the process, but every hour had done something, it seemed, towards 
clarify his point of view. a consciousness had begun to stir in him 
that was neither that of the old, easy lawford, whom he had never been 
fully aware of before, nor of this strange ghostly intelligence that 
haunt the hawklike, restless face, and pluck so insistent at his 
distracted nerves. he had begun in a vague fashion to be aware of them 
both, could in a fashion discriminate between them, almost as if there 
really were two spirits in stubborn conflict within him. it would, of 
course, wear him down in time. there could be only one end to such a 
struggle--the end. 

all day he had long for freedom, on and on, with craving for the open 
sky, for solitude, for green silence, beyond these maddening walls. this 
heedful silken coming and going, these sunday voices, this reiterant 
yelp of a single peevish bell--would they never cease? and above all, 
betwixt dread and an almost physical greed, he hunger for night. he 
sat down with elbow on knee and head on his hands, thinking of night, 
its secrecy, its immeasurable solitude. 

his eyelid twitched; the fire before him had for an instant gone black 
out. he seem to see slow-gesturing branches, grass stoop beneath 
a grey and wind-swept sky. he start up; and the remembrance of the 
morning return to him--the glassy light, the change rays, the 
beam gilt upon the useless books. now, at last, at the windows; 
afternoon had begun to wane. and when sheila brought up his tea, as if 
chance had heard his cry, she enter in hat and stole. she put down the 
tray, and pause at the glass, look across it out of the window. 

‘alice say you are to eat every one of those delicious sandwiches, and 
especially the tiny omelette. you have scarcely touched anything to-day, 
arthur. i am a poor one to preach, i am afraid; but you know what that 
will mean--a worse breakdown still. you really must try to think of--of 
us all.’ 

‘are you go to church?’ he ask in a low voice. 

‘not, of course, if you would prefer not. but dr simon advise me most 
particularly to go out at least once a day. we must remember, this is 
not the beginning of your illness. long-continued anxiety, i suppose, 
do tell on one in time. anyhow, he said that i look worried and 
run-down. i am worried. let us both try for each other’s sakes, or even 
if only for alice’s, to--to do all we can. i must not harass you; but is 
there any--do you see the slightest change of any kind?’ 

‘you always look pretty, sheila; to-night you look prettier: that is the 
only change, i think.’ 

mrs lawford’s attitude intensify in its stillness. ‘now, speak 
quite frankly, what is it in you suggest these remark at such a time? 
that’s what baffle me. it seem so childish, so needlessly blind.’ 

‘i am very sorry, sheila, to be so childish. but i’m not, say what 
you like, blind. you are pretty: i’d repeat it if i was burning at the 
stake.’ 

sheila lower her eyes soft on to the rich-toned picture in the 
glass. ‘supposing,’ she said, watch her lip move, ‘supposing--of 
course, i know you are get better and all that--but supposing you 
don’t change back as mr bethany thinks, what will you do? honestly, 
arthur, when i think over it calmly, the whole tragedy come back on me 
with such a force it sweep me off my feet; i am for the moment scarcely 
my own mistress. what would you do?’ 

‘i think, sheila,’ reply a low, infinite weary voice, ‘i think i 
should marry again.’ it was the same wavering, faint ironic voice 
that had slightly discompose dr simon that same morning. 

‘“marry again”!’ exclaim incredulous the full lip in the 
looking-glass. ‘who?’ 

‘you, dear!’ 

sheila turn soft round, conscious in a most humiliate manner that 
she had ever so little flushed. 

her husband was pour out his tea, unaware, apparently, of her change 
of position. she watch him curiously. in spite of all her reason, of 
her absolute certainty, she wonder even again for a moment if this 
really could be arthur. and for the first time she realise the power 
and mastery of that eager and far too hungry face. her mind seem to 
pause, flutter in air, like a bird in the wind. she hasten rather 
unsteadi to the door. 

‘will you want anything more, do you think, for an hour?’ she asked. 

her husband look up over his little table. ‘is alice go with you?’ 

‘oh yes; poor child, she looks so pale and miserable. we are go to 
mrs sherwin’s, and then on to church. you will lock your door?’ 

‘yes, i will lock my door.’ 

‘and i do hope arthur--nothing rash!’ 

a change, that seem almost the effect of actual shadow, came over his 
face. ‘i wish you could stay with me,’ he said slowly. ‘i don’t think 
you have any idea what--what i go through.’ 

it was as if a child had ask on the verge of terror for a candle in 
the dark. but an hour’s terror is better than a lifetime of timidity. 
sheila sighed. 

‘i think,’ she said, ‘i too might say that. but there; give way will 
do nothing for either of us. i shall be gone only for an hour, or two 
at the most. and i told mr bethany i should have to come out before the 
sermon: it’s only mr craik.’ 

‘but why mrs sherwin? she’d worm a secret out of one’s grave.’ 

‘it’s useless to discuss that, arthur; you have always consistent 
dislike my friends. it’s scarcely likely that you would find any 
improvement in them now.’ 

‘oh, well--’ he began. but the door was already closed. 

‘sheila!’ he call in a burst of anger. 

‘well, arthur?’ 

‘you have taken my latchkey.’ 

sheila came hasty in again. ‘your latchkey?’ 

‘i am go out.’ 

‘“going out!”--you will not be so mad, so criminal; and after your 
promise!’ 

he stood up. ‘it is useless to argue. if i do not go out, i shall 
certainly go mad. as for criminal--why, that’s a woman’s word. who on 
earth is to know me?’ 

‘it is of no consequence, then, that the servant are already gossip 
about this impossible dr ferguson; that you are certain to be seen 
either go or returning; that alice is bound to discover that you are 
well enough to go out, and yet not even enough to say good-night to your 
own daughter--oh, it’s monstrous, it’s a frantic, a heartless thing to 
do!’ her voice vague suggest tears. 

lawford eye her cold and stubbornly--thinking of the empty room 
he would leave await his return, its lamp burning, its fire-flames 
shining. it was almost a physical discomfort, this longing unspeakable 
for the twilight, the green secrecy and the silence of the graves. ‘keep 
them out of the way,’ he said in a low voice; ‘it will be dark when i 
come in.’ his harden face lit up. ‘it’s useless to attempt to dissuade 
me.’ 

‘why must you always be hurt me? why do you seem to delight in try 
to estrange me?’ husband and wife face each other across the clear-lit 
room. he did not answer. 

‘for the last time,’ she said in a quiet, hard voice, ‘i ask you not to 
go.’ 

he shrug his shoulders. ‘ask me not to come back,’ he said; ‘that’s 
near your hope.’ he turn his face to the fire. without moving 
he heard her go out, return, pause, and go out again. and when he 
deliberate wheel round in his chair the little key lay conspicuous 
there on the counterpane. 



chapter nine 

the last light of sunset lay in the west; and a sullen wrack of cloud 
was mount into the windless sky when lawford enter the country 
graveyard again by its dark weather-worn lych-gate. the old stone church 
with its square tower stood amid trees, its eastern window faint aglow 
with crimson and purple. he could hear a steady, rather nasal voice 
through its open lattices. but the stoop stone and the cypress 
were out of sight of its porch. he would not be seen down there. he 
pause a moment, however; his hat was drawn down over his eyes; he was 
shivering. far over the harvest field show a grow pallor in 
the solitary seat beneath the cypresses. he stood hesitating, gaze 
steady and yet half vacant at the motionless figure, and in a while 
a face was lift in his direction, and undisconcerted eyes calm 
survey him. 

‘i am afraid,’ call lawford rather nervously--‘i hope i am not 
intruding?’ 

‘not at all, not at all,’ said the stranger. ‘i have no privilege here; 
at least as yet.’ 

lawford again hesitated, then slow advanced. ‘it’s astonishing quiet 
and beautiful,’ he said. 

the stranger turn his head to glance over the fields. ‘yes, it is, 
very,’ he replied. there was the faintest accent, a little drawl of 
unfriend in the remark. 

‘you often sit here?’ lawford persisted. 

the stranger raise his eyebrows. ‘oh yes, often.’ he smiled. ‘it is 
my own modest fashion of attend divine service. the congregation is 
rapt.’ 

‘my visits,’ said lawford, ‘have been very few--in fact, so far as i 
know, i have only once been here before.’ 

‘i envy you the novelty.’ there was again the same faint unmistakable 
antagonism in voice and attitude; and yet so deep was the relief in 
talk to a fellow creature who hadn’t the least suspicion of anything 
unusual in his appearance that lawford was extremely disinclined to turn 
back. he made another effort--for conversation with stranger had always 
been a difficulty to him--and advanced towards the seat. ‘you mustn’t 
please let me intrude upon you,’ he said, ‘but really i am very 
interested in this queer old place. perhaps you would tell me something 
of its history?’ he sat down. his companion move slow to the other 
side of the broken gravestone. 

‘to tell you the truth,’ he replied, pick his way as it were from 
word to word, ‘it’s “history,” as people call it, do not interest me 
in the least. after all, it’s not when a thing is, but what it is, that 
much matters. what this is’--he glanced, with head bent, across the 
shadowy stones, ‘is pretty evident. of course, age has its charms.’ 

‘and is this very old?’ 

‘oh yes, it’s old right enough, as things go; but even age, perhaps, 
is mainly an affair of the imagination. there’s a tombstone near that 
little old hawthorn, and there are two other side by side under the 
wall, still even legible late seventeenth century. that’s pretty 
good weathering.’ he smile faintly. ‘of course, the church itself is 
century older, drench with age. but she’s still sleep-walking while 
these old tombstone dream. glow-worms and cricket are not such bad 
bedfellows.’ 

‘what interested me most, i think,’ said lawford haltingly, ‘was this.’ 
he pointed with his stick to the grave at his feet. 

‘ah, yes, sabathier’s,’ said the stranger; ‘i know his peculiar history 
almost by heart.’ 

lawford found himself staring with unusual concentration into the 
rather long and pale face. ‘not, i suppose,’ he resume faintly--‘not, i 
suppose, beyond what’s there.’ 

his companion leant his hand on the old stoop tombstone. ‘well, you 
know, there’s a good deal there’--he stoop over--‘if you read between 
the lines. even if you don’t.’ 

‘a suicide,’ said lawford, under his breath. 

‘yes, a suicide; that’s why our christian countrymen have bury him 
outside of the fold. dead or alive, they try to keep the wolf out.’ 

‘is this, then, unconsecrated ground?’ said lawford. 

‘haven’t you noticed,’ drawl the other, ‘how green the grass grow 
down here, and how very sharp are poor old sabathier’s thorns? besides, 
he was a stranger, and they--kept him out.’ 

‘but, surely,’ said lawford, ‘was it so entire a matter of choice--the 
law of the church? if he did kill himself, he did.’ 

the stranger turn with a little shrug. ‘i don’t suppose it’s a matter 
of much consequence to him. i fancy i was his only friend. may i 
venture to ask why you are interested in the poor old thing?’ 

lawford’s mind was as calm and shallow as a millpond. ‘oh, a rather 
unusual thing happen to me here,’ he said. ‘you say you often come?’ 

‘often,’ said the stranger rather curtly. 

‘has anything--ever--occurred?’ 

‘“occurred?”’ he raise his eyebrows. ‘i wish it had. i come here 
simply, as i have said, because it’s quiet; because i prefer the company 
of those who never answer me back, and who do not so much as condescend 
to pay me the least attention.’ he smile and turn his face towards 
the quiet fields. 

lawford, after a long pause, lift his eyes. ‘do you think,’ he said 
softly, ‘it is possible one ever could?’ 

‘“one ever could?”’ 

‘answer back?’ 

there was a low rot wall of stone encompass sabathier’s grave; 
on this the stranger sat down. he glance up rather curious at his 
companion. ‘seldom the time and the place and the revenant altogether. 
the thought has occur to others,’ he venture to add. 

‘of course, of course,’ said lawford eagerly. ‘but it is an absolutely 
new one to me. i don’t mean that i have never had such an idea, just in 
one’s own superficial way; but’--he pause and glance swift into the 
fast-thickening twilight--‘i wonder: are they, do you think, really, all 
quite dead?’ 

‘call and see!’ taunt the stranger softly. 

‘ah, yes, i know,’ said lawford. ‘but i believe in the resurrection of 
the body; that is what we say; and supposing, when a man dies--supposing 
it was most frightfully against one’s will; that one hate the awful 
inaction that death brings, shut a poor devil up like a child 
kick against the door in a dark cupboard; one might surely one 
might--just quietly, you know, try to get out? wouldn’t you?’ he added. 

‘and, surely,’ he found himself beginning gently to argue again, 
‘surely, what about, say, him?’ he nod towards the old and broken 
grave that lay between them. 

‘what, sabathier?’ the other echoed, lay his hand upon the stone. 

and a sheer enormous abyss of silence seem to follow the unanswerable 
question. 

‘he was a stranger; it say so. good god!’ said lawford, ‘how he must 
have want to get home! he kill himself, poor wretch, think of the 
fret and fever he must have been in--just before. imagine it.’ 

‘but it might, you know,’ suggest the other with a smile--‘might have 
been sheer indifference.’ 

‘“nicholas sabathier, stranger to this parish”--no, no,’ said lawford, 
his heart beating as if it would choke him, ‘i don’t fancy it was 
indifference.’ 

it was almost too dark now to distinguish the stranger’s features but 
there seem a faint suggestion of irony in his voice. ‘and how do 
you suppose your angry naughty child would set about it? it’s narrow 
quarters; how would he begin?’ 

lawford sat quite still. ‘you say--i hope i am not detain you--you 
say you have come here, sat here often, on this very seat; have you ever 
had--have you ever fallen asleep here?’ 

‘why do you ask?’ inquire the other curiously. 

‘i was only wondering,’ said lawford. he was cold and shivering. he felt 
instinctive it was madness to sit on here in the thin gliding mist 
that had gather in swathe above the grass, milk-pale in the rising 
moon. the stranger turn away from him. 

‘“for in that sleep of death what dream may come must give us pause,”’ 
he said slowly, with a little satirical catch on the last word. ‘what 
did you dream?’ 

lawford glance helpless about him. the moon cast lean grey beam 
of light between the cypresses. but to his wide and wandering eyes it 
seem that a radiance other than hers haunt these mound and leaning 
stones. ‘have you ever notice it?’ he said, putt out his hand 
towards his unknown companion; ‘this stone is cracked from head to 
foot?... but there’--he rose stiff and chilled--‘i am afraid i have 
bore you with my company. you came here for solitude, and i have been 
try to convince you that we are surround with witnesses. you will 
forgive my intrusion?’ there was a kind of old-fashioned courtesy in his 
manner that he himself was dim aware of. he held out his hand. 

‘i hope you will think nothing of the kind,’ said the other earnestly; 
‘how could it be in any sense an intrusion? it’s the old story of 
bluebeard. and i confess i too should very much like a peep into his 
cupboard. who wouldn’t? but there, it’s merely a matter of time, i 
suppose.’ he paused, and together they slow ascend the path already 
glimmer with a heavy dew. at the porch they pause once more. and now 
it was the stranger that held out his hand. 

‘perhaps,’ he said, ‘you will give me the pleasure of some day 
continue our talk. as for our friend below, it so happen that i have 
manage to pick up a little more of his history than the sexton seem to 
have heard of--if you would care some time or other to share it. i live 
only at the foot of the hill, not half a mile distant. perhaps you could 
spare the time now?’ 

lawford took out his watch, ‘you are really very kind,’ he said. ‘but, 
perhaps--well, whatever that history may be, i think you would agree 
that mine is even--but, there, i’ve talk too much about myself 
already. perhaps to-morrow?’ 

‘why, to-morrow, then,’ said his companion. ‘it’s a flat wooden house, 
on the left-hand side. come at any time of the evening’; he pause again 
and smiled--‘the third house after the rectory, which is marked up on 
the gate. my name is herbert--herbert herbert to be precise.’ 

lawford took out his pocket-book and a card. ‘mine,’ he said, hand 
it grave to his companion. ‘is lawford--at least...’ it was really the 
first time that either had seen the other’s face at close quarters and 
clear-lit; and on lawford’s a moon almost at the full shone 
dazzlingly. he saw an expression--dismay, incredulity, overwhelming 
astonishment--start sudden into the dark, rather indifferent eyes. 

‘what is it?’ he cried, hasty stoop close. 

‘why,’ said the other, laugh and turning away, ‘i think the moon must 
have bewitch me too.’ 



chapter ten 

lawford listen awhile before opening his door. he heard voice in the 
dining-room. a light shone faint between the blind of his bedroom. he 
very gently let himself in, and unheard, unseen, mount the stairs. he 
sat down in front of the fire, tired out and bitter cold in spite 
of his long walk home. but his mind was weary even than his body. he 
try in vain to catch up the thread of his thoughts. he only knew for 
certain that so far as his first hope and motive had gone his errand 
had prove entire futile. ‘how could i possibly fall asleep with that 
fellow talk there?’ he had said to himself angrily; yet knew in his 
heart that their talk had driven every other idea out of his mind. he 
had not yet even glance into the glass. his every thought was vain 
wandering round and round the one curious hint that had drift in, but 
which he had not yet been able to put into words. 

supposing, though, that he had really fallen into a deep sleep, with 
none to watch or spy--what then? however ridiculous that idea, it was 
not more ridiculous, more incredible than the actual fact. if he had 
remain there, he might, it was just possible that he would by now, 
have actually awaken just his own familiar every-day self again. and 
the thought of that--though he hardly realise its full import--actually 
did send him on tip-toe for a glance that more or less effectually set 
the question at rest. and there look out at him, it seemed, the 
same dark sallow face that had so much appall him only two nights 
ago--expressionless, cadaverous, with shadowy hollow beneath the 
glittering eyes. and even as he watch it, its lips, of their own 
volition, drew together and question him--‘whose?’ 

he was not to be given much leisure, however, for fantastic reverie 
like this. as he lean his head on his hands, gladly conscious that he 
could not possibly bear this incessant strain for long, sheila open 
the door. he start up. 

‘i wish you would knock,’ he said angrily; ‘you talk of quiet; you tell 
me to rest, and think; and here you come creep and spy on me as 
if i was a child in a nursery. i refuse to be watch and guarded and 
peep on like this.’ he knew that his hand were trembling, that he 
could not keep his eyes fixed, that his voice was nearly inarticulate. 

sheila drew in her lips. ‘i have merely come to tell you, arthur, that 
mr bethany has brought mr danton in to supper. he agree with me it 
really would be advisable to take such a very old and prudent and 
practical friend into our confidence. you do nothing i ask of you. i 
simply cannot bear the burden of this incessant anxiety. look, now, what 
your night walk has done for you! you look positively at death’s door.’ 

‘what--what an instinct you have for the right word,’ said lawford 
softly. ‘and danton, of all people in the world! it was surely rather a 
curious, a thoughtless choice. has he had supper?’ 

‘why do you ask?’ 

‘he won’t believe: too--bloated.’ 

‘i think,’ said sheila indignantly, ‘it is hardly fair to speak of a 
very old and a very true friend of mine in such--well, vulgar terms as 
that. besides, arthur, as for believing--without in the least desire 
to hurt your feelings--i must candidly warn you, some people won’t.’ 

‘come along,’ said lawford, with a faint gust of laughter; ‘let’s see.’ 

they went quick downstairs, sheila with less dignity, perhaps, than 
she had been surprise into since she had left a slim girlhood 
behind. she swept into the gaze of the two gentlemen standing together 
on the hearthrug; and so was caught, as it were, between a rain of 
conflict glances, for her husband had follow instantly, and stood 
now behind her, stoop a little, and with something between contempt 
and defiance confront an old fat friend, whom that one brief 
challenging instant had congeal into a condition of passive and 
immovable hostility. 

mr danton compose his chin in his collar, and deliberate 
turn himself towards his companion. his small eyes wandered, and 
instantaneous met and rest on those of mrs lawford. 

‘arthur thought he would prefer to come down and see you himself.’ 

‘you take such formidable risks, lawford,’ said mr bethany in a dry, 
difficult voice. 

‘am i really to believe,’ danton began huskily. ‘i am sure, bethany, 
you will--my dear mrs lawford!’ said he, stirring vaguely, glancing 
restlessly. 

‘it was not my wish, vicar, to come at all,’ said a voice from the 
doorway. ‘to tell you the truth, i am too tired to care a jot either 
way. and’--he lift a long arm--‘i must positively refuse to produce 
the least, the remotest proof that i am not, so far as i am personally 
aware, even the man in the moon. danton at heart was always an 
incorrigible sceptic. aren’t you, t. d.? you pride your dear old brawn 
on it in secret?’ 

‘i really--’ began danton in a rich still voice. 

‘oh, but you know you are,’ drawl on the slightly hesitate 
long-drawn syllables; ‘it’s your parochial metier. firm, unctuous, 
subtle, scepticism; and to that end your body flourishes. you were born 
fat; you became fat; and fat, my dear danton, has been deliberate 
thrust on you--in layers! lampreys! you’ll perish of surfeit some day, 
of sheer dantonism. and fat, postmortem, danton. oh, what a basting’s 
there!’ 

mr bethany, with a convulsive effort, woke. he turn swift on mrs 
lawford. ‘why, why, could you not have seen?’ he cried. 

‘it’s no good, vicar. she’s all sheer laodicean. blow hot, blow cold. 
north, south, east, west--to have a weathercock for a wife is to marry 
the wind. there’s nothing to be got from poor sheila but.... 

‘lawford!’ the little man’s voice was as sharp as the crack of a whip; 
‘i forbid it. do you hear me? i forbid it. some self-command; my dear 
good fellow, remember, remember it’s only the will, the will that keeps 
us breathing.’ 

lawford peer as if out of a gathering dusk, that thicken and 
flicker with shadow before his eyes. ‘what’s he mean, then,’ he

peeping--what’s he mean, i say?’ there was a moment’s silence. then with 
lift brow and wide eyes that to every one of his three witness 
left an indelible memory of clear and wolfish light within their glassy 
pupils, he turn heavily, and climb back to his solitude. 

‘i suppose,’ began danton, with an obvious effort to disentangle himself 
from the humiliate of the moment, ‘i suppose he was--wandering?’ 

‘bless me, yes,’ said mr bethany cordially--‘fever. we all know what 
that means.’ 

‘yes,’ said danton, taking refuge in mrs lawford’s white and intent 
gaze. 

‘just think, think, danton--the awful, incessant strain of such an 
ordeal. think for an instant what such a thing means!’ 

danton insert a plump, white finger between collar and chin. ‘oh yes. 
but--eh?--needlessly abusive? i never said i disbelieve him.’ 

‘do you?’ said mrs lawford’s voice. 

he poised himself, as if it were, on the monolithic stability of his 
legs. ‘eh?’ he said. 

mr bethany sat down at the table. ‘i rather fear some such temporary 
breakdown as this, danton. i think i foresaw it. and now, just while we 
are all three alone here together in friendly conclave, wouldn’t it be 
as well, don’t you think, to confront ourselves with the difficulties? 
i know--we all know, that that poor half-demented creature is arthur 
lawford. this morning he was as sane, as lucid as i hope i am now. an 
awful calamity has sudden fallen upon him--this change. i own frankly 
at the first sheer shock it stagger me as i think for the moment it 
has stagger you. but when i had seen the poor fellow face to face, 
heard him talk, and watch him there upstairs in the silence stir and 
awake and come up again to his trouble out of his sleep. i had no more 
doubt in my own mind and heart that he was he than i have in my mind 
that i--am i. we do in some mysterious way, you’ll own at once, grow so 
accustomed, so inured, if you like, to each other’s face (masks 
though they be) that we hardly realise we see them when we are speak 
together. and yet the slightest, the most infinitesimal change is 
instantly apparent.’ 

‘oh yes, vicar; but you see--’ 

mr bethany raise a small lean hand: ‘one moment, please. i have heard 
lawford’s own account. conscious or unconscious, he has been through 
some terrific strain, some such awful conflict with the unseen powers 
that we--thank god!--have only read about, and never perhaps, until 
death is upon us, shall witness for ourselves. what more likely, more 
inevitable than that such a thing should leave its scar, its cloud, its 
mask shadow?--call it what you will. a smile can turn a face we dread 
into a face we’d die for. some experience, which would be nothing but 
a hideous cruelty and outrage to ask too close about--one, perhaps, 
which he could, even if he would, poor fellow, give no account of--has 
put him temporary at the world’s mercy. they made him a nine days’ 
wonder, a byword. and that, my dear danton, is just where we come in. 
we know the man himself; and it is to be our privilege to act as a 
buffer-state, to be intermediary between him and the rest of this 
deadly, craving, sheepish world--for the time being; oh yes, just for 
the time being. other and keen and more knowledgeable mind than mine 
or yours will some day bring him back to us again. we don’t attempt to 
explain; we can’t. we simply believe.’ 

but danton merely continue to stare, as if into the quiet of an 
aquarium. 

‘my dear good danton,’ persist mr bethany with cherub patience, ‘how 
old are you?’ 

‘i don’t see quite...’ smile danton with recover ease, and 
rapid mobilise forces. ‘excuse the confidence, mrs lawford, i’m 
forty-three.’ 

‘good,’ said mr bethany; ‘and i’m seventy-one, and this child here’--he 
pointed an accuse finger at sheila--is youth perpetual. so,’ he 
brisk brightened, ‘say, between us we’re six score all told. are 
we--can we, deliberately, with this mere pinch of years at our command 
out of the wheeling million that have gone--can we say, “this is 
impossible,” to any single phenomenon? can we?’ 

‘no, we can’t, of course,’ said danton formidably. ‘not finally. that’s 
all very well, but’--he paused, and nodded, nod his round head 
upward as if towards the inaudible overhead, ‘i suppose he can’t hear?’ 

mr bethany rose cheerfully. ‘all right, danton; i am afraid you are 
exactly what the poor fellow in his delirium solemn asseverated. 
and, jesting apart, it is in delirium that we tell our sheer, plain, 
unadulterated truth: you’re a nicely cover sceptic. personally, i 
refuse to discuss the matter. mere dull, stubborn prejudice; bigotry, if 
you like. i will only remark just this--that mrs lawford and i, in our 
inmost hearts, know. you, my dear danton, forgive the freedom, merely 
incredulous grope. faith versus reason--that prehistoric armageddon. 
some day, and a day not far distant either, lawford will come back 
to us. this--this shutter will be taken down as abrupt as by some 
inconceivable drowsy heedless of common nature it has been put 
up. he’ll win through; and of his own sheer will and courage. but now, 
because i ask it, and this poor child here entreat it, you will say 
nothing to a living soul about the matter, say, till friday? what 
step-by-step creature we are, to be sure! i say friday because it 
will be exactly a week then. and what’s a week?--to nature scarcely 
the unfold of a rose. but still, friday be it. then, if nothing has 
occurred, we will, we shall have to call a friendly gathering, we shall 
be compel to have a friendly consultation.’ 

‘i’m not, i hope, a brute, bethany,’ said danton apologetically; ‘but, 
honestly, speak for myself, simply as a man of the world, it’s a 
big risk to be taking on--what shall we call it?--on mere intuition. 
personally, and even in a court of law--though heaven forbid it ever 
reach that stage--personally, i could swear that the fellow that stood 
abuse me there, in that revolting fashion, was not lawford. it would 
be easy even to believe in him, if there were not that--that glaze, 
that shocking simulation of the man himself, the very man. but then, i 
am a sceptic; i own it. and ‘pon my word, mrs lawford, there’s plenty of 
room for sceptic in a world like this.’ 

‘very well,’ said mr bethany crisply, ‘that’s settled, then. with your 
permission, my dear,’ he added, turning untarnishable clear childlike 
eyes on sheila, ‘i will take all risks--even to the foot of the gibbet: 
accessory, danton, after the fact.’ and so direct and cloudless was his 
gaze that sheila try in vain to evade it and to catch a glimpse 
of danton’s small agate-like eyes, now completely under mastery, and 
await confident the meeting with her own. 

‘of course,’ she said, ‘i am entire in your hands, dear mr bethany.’ 



chapter eleven 

lawford slept far into the cloudy monday morning, to wake steep in 
sleep, lethargic, and fretful haunt by inconclusive remembrance 
of the night before. when sheila, with obvious and capacious composure, 
brought him his breakfast tray, he watch her face for some time 
without speaking. 

‘sheila,’ he began, as she was about to leave the room again. 

she paused, smiling. 

‘did anything happen last night? would you mind telling me, sheila? who 
was it was here?’ 

her lid the least bit narrowed. ‘certainly, arthur; mr danton was 
here.’ 

‘then it was not a dream?’ 

‘oh no,’ said sheila. 

‘what did i say? what did he say? it was hopeless, anyhow.’ 

‘i don’t quite understand what you mean by “hopeless,” arthur. and must 
i answer the other questions?’ 

lawford drew his hand over his face, like a tired child. ‘he 
didn’t--believe?’ 

‘no, dear,’ said sheila softly. 

‘and you, sheila?’ came the subdued voice. 

sheila cross slow to the window. ‘well, quite honestly, arthur, i 
was not very much surprised. whatever we are agree about on the whole, 
you were scarcely yourself last night.’ 

lawford shut his eyes, and re-opened them full on his wife’s calm 
scrutiny, who had in that moment turn in the light of the one drawn 
blind to face him again. 

‘who is? always?’ 

‘no,’ said sheila; ‘but--it was at least unfortunate. we can’t, i 
suppose, rely on dr bethany alone.’ 

lawford crouch over his food. ‘will he blab?’ 

‘blab! mr danton is a gentleman, arthur.’ 

lawford roll his eyes as if in temporary vertigo. ‘yes,’ he said. and 
sheila once more prepared to make a reposeful exit. 

‘i don’t think i can see simon this morning.’ 

‘oh. who, then?’ 

‘i mean i would prefer to be left alone.’ 

‘believe me, i had no intention to intrude.’ and this time the door 
really closed. 

‘he is in a quiet, soothe sleep,’ said sheila a few minutes later. 

‘nothing could be better,’ said dr simon; and lawford, to his 
inexpressible relief, heard the fevered throb of the doctor’s car 
reverse, and turn over and shut his eyes, dull and exhaust in 
the still unfriend of the vacant room. his spirits had sunk, 
he thought, to their lowest ebb. he scarcely heed the fragment of 
dreams--clear, green landscapes, amazing gleam of peace, the 
sudden broken voices, the rustling and calling shadowy of 
subconsciousness--in this quiet sunlight of reality. the cloud had 
broken, or had been withdrawn like a veil from the october skies. one 
thought alone was his refuge; one face alone haunt him with its 
peace; one remembrance soothe him--alice. through all his scattered 
and purposeless argument he strove to remember her voice, the

in the afternoon he got up and dress himself. he could not bring

‘i couldn’t rest,’ he told sheila, when she presently came in on one 
of her quiet, cautious, heedful visits; ‘and one tire of reading even 
quain in bed.’ 

‘have you found anything?’ she inquire politely. 

‘oh yes,’ said lawford wearily; ‘i have discover that infinite 
worse things are infinite commoner. but that there’s nothing quite so 
picturesque.’ 

‘tell me,’ said sheila, with refreshing naivete. ‘how do it feel? do 
it even in the slightest degree affect your mind?’ 

he turn his back and look up at his broad gilt portrait for

one’s nerves--that fellow danton--when one’s overtired. you have’--his

‘me? oh dear, no! i never was the least bit observant; you know

‘she mopes, dear, rather. she cannot, of course, quite understand why

move sheila, perhaps, more than any other of the congest occurrence

but lawford, quite unmindful of the shock, continue in a kind of

measure for freedom that until yesterday he had not even dream exist

had forgotten flitters. had flit forgotten him? would he bark at the

alice nodded. her whole face was in a moment flood with red. it ebb

‘certainly, certainly, by no means,’ he began, listen vague to the

for alice stood gaze stirless on and on into this infinite 
strange, infinite familiar shadowy, phantasmal face. ‘oh yes,’ she

‘i fear, my dear...it cannot be. he long to see you. but with his mind, 
you know, in this state, it might--?’ 

‘but mother never told me,’ broke in the girl desperately, ‘there was

lawford scan swift the little square beloved and memory room that

‘but who--who “can walk and talk in the night?”’ inquire a low stealthy 
voice out of the quiet behind her. 

alice turn swiftly. her mother was standing at a little distance, with

volatile and eau de cologne.... 

it was yet clear daylight when lawford appear beneath the portico of

he found himself walking with an affectation of effrontery, and smile 
with a faint contempt on all alike, as if to keep himself from slinking, 
and the wolf out of his eyes. he felt restless, and watchful, and

a peculiar aura in lawford’s presence, a shadow of a something in his 
demeanour that prove him alien. 

none the less green widderstone kept calling him, much as a bell in the 
imagination toll on and on, the echo of reality. if the worst should

at the foot of the hill he pass by the green and white rectory, and

it stood square and high and dark in a small amphitheatre of verdure.

‘ah, yes, come in, mr lawford,’ he drawled; ‘i was beginning to be

lawford laid hat and walking-stick on an oak bench, and follow his

‘please sit down; i shan’t be a moment; i am not sure that my sister is

the oven, so we won’t wait. i hope you haven’t been very much bored.’

‘my sister’s favorite occupation; she sit for hour and hours, with not

what’s in a name?’ laugh herbert. ‘but it really is a queer show-up of

big hands; and then just like some quiet, groping, nocturnal creature, 
he begin his precious search--shelves, drawers that are not here, 
cupboard gone years ago, quest and nose no end, and quite

imperfect simulacrum, to the outward illusion of a passing likeness

dwelt for a moment with a dark, quiet scrutiny on his visitor. 

‘you say a passing likeness; do you mean that?’ 

herbert smile indulgently. ‘if one can mean what is purely a 
speculation. i am only try to look at the thing dispassionately, you 
see. we are so much the slave of mere repetition. here is life--yours 
and mine--a kind of plenum in vacuo. it is only when we begin to play 
the eavesdropper; when something go askew; when one of the sentry on 
the frontier of the unexpected shout a hoarse “qui vive?”--it is only 
then we begin to question; to prick our aldermen and pinch the calves 
of our kings. why, who is there can answer to anybody’s but his own 
satisfaction just that one fundamental question--are we the prisoners, 
the slaves, the inheritors, the creatures, or the creator of our 
bodies? fallen angel or horrific dust? as for identity or likeness 
or personality, we have only our neighbours’ nod for them, and just a 
fade memory. no, the old fairy tale knew better; and witchcraft’s 
witchcraft to the end of the chapter. honestly, and just of course on 
that one theory, lawford, i can’t help thinking that sabathier’s raid 
only just so far succeed as to leave his impression in the wax. 
it doesn’t, of course, follow that it will necessarily end there. it 
might--it may be even now just gradual fade away. it may, you know, 
need driving out--with whip and scorpions. it might, perhaps, work in.’ 

lawford sat cold and still. ‘it’s no good, no good,’ he said, ‘i don’t 
understand; i can’t follow you. i was always stupid, always bigoted and 
cocksure. these things have never seem anything but old women’s tale 
to me. and now i must pay for it. and this nichola sabathier; you say 
he was a blackguard?’ 

‘well,’ said herbert with a faint smile, ‘that depend on your 
definition of the word. he wasn’t a flunkey, a fool, or a prig, if 
that’s what you mean. he wasn’t perhaps on mrs grundy’s visiting 
list. he wasn’t exactly gregarious. and yet in a sense that kind of 
temperament is so rare that sappho, nelson, and shelley share it. to 
the stodgy, suety world of course it’s little else than sheer moonshine, 
midsummer madness. naturally, in its own charming and stodgy way 
the world kept flicker cold water in his direction. naturally it 
hissed.... i shall find the book. you shall have the book; oh yes.’ 

‘there’s only one more question,’ said lawford in a dull, slow voice, 
stoop and covering his face with his hands. ‘i know it’s impossible 
for you to realise--but to me time seem like that water there, to be 
heap up about me. i wait, just as one waits when the conductor of an 
orchestra lift his hand and in a moment the whole surge of brass and 
wood, cymbal and drum will crash out--and sweep me under. i can’t tell 
you herbert, how it all is, with just these grope stirring of that 
mole in my mind’s dark. you say it may be this face, working in! god 
knows. i find it easy to speak to you--this cold, clear sense, you know. 
the other feel too much, or are afraid, or--let me think--yes, i 
was go to ask you a question. but no one can answer it.’ he peer 
darkly, with white face sudden reveal between his hands. ‘what 
remains now? where do i come in? what is there left for me to do?’ 

and at that moment there sounded, even above the monotonous roar of 
the water beyond the window--there fell the sound of a light footfall 
approach along the corridor. 

‘listen,’ said herbert; ‘here’s my sister coming; we’ll ask her.’ 




chapter thirteen 

the door opened. lawford rose, and into the further ray of the 
candlelight enter a rather slim figure in a light summer gown. 

‘just home?’ said herbert. 

‘we’ve been for a walk--’ 

‘my sister always forget everything,’ said herbert, turning to lawford; 
‘even tea-time. this is mr lawford, grisel. we’ve been argue no end. 
and we want you to give a decision. it’s just this: supposing if by some 
impossible trick you had come in now, not the charming familiar sister 
you are, but shorter, fatter, fair and round-faced, quite different, 
physically, you know--what would you do?’ 

‘what nonsense you talk, herbert!’ 

‘yes, but supposing: a complete transmogrification--by some unimaginable 
ingress or enchantment, by nibble a bunch of roses, or whatever you 
like to call it?’ 

‘only physically?’ 

‘well, yes, actually; but potentially, why--that’s another matter.’ 

the dark eyes pass slow from her brother’s face and rest grave 
on their visitor’s. 

‘is he making fun of me?’ 

lawford almost imperceptible shook his head. 

‘but what a question! and i’ve had no tea.’ she drew her glove slow 
through her hand. ‘the thing, of course, isn’t possible, i know. but 
shouldn’t i go mad, don’t you think?’ 

lawford gaze quiet back into the clear, grave, deliberate eyes. 
‘suppose, suppose, just for the sake of argument--not,’ he suggested. 

she turn her head and reflected, glancing from one to the other of the 
pure, steady candle-flames. 

‘and what was your answer?’ she said, look over her shoulder at her

‘my dear child, you know what my answer are like!’

lawford took a deep breath, gaze mutely, forlornly, into the lovely

‘you’ll come again?’ herbert was saying, with a hint of anxiety, even of 
apology in his voice.

france book. it was i who--who mislaid it.’ 

the set, stricken face listen unmoved. 

‘you are ill. come back! i am afraid you are very ill.’ 

‘it’s not that, not that,’ lawford muttered; ‘don’t leave me; i am 
alone. don’t question me,’ he said strangely, look down into her

he swept a lean arm towards the unseen churchyard. ‘i am afraid.’ 

the cold hand clasp his closer. ‘hush, don’t speak! come back; come 
back. i am with you, a friend, you see; come back.’ 

lawford clutch her hand as a blind man in sudden peril might clutch

‘oh, but it’s must,’ he said; ‘i must go on. you see--why, everything 
depend on struggle through: the future! but if you only knew--there!’ 
again his arm swept out, and the lean terrify face turn shudder

gradual lightens. how thankful i am i came.’ 

she had turn and they were steady ascend as if push their way,

standing in clear-cut silhouette against the liquid dusk of the sky.

fear fade out and vanished, as the passed-away cloud of a storm that 
leave a purer, serene sky. 

they stop and stood together on the brow of the little hill, and

forgiveness--and a stranger!’ 

‘please don’t say that--unless--unless--a “pilgrim” too. i think, 
surely, you must own we did have the best of it that time. yes--and i

the clear grey eyes show not the faintest perturbation. ‘but i do; i

they pause in the dark face to face, the silence of the sky,

triumph.

windless air turn slow the cockle leaf. 

few of them were alike except for the dingy of the print and the

chapter fourteen 

sheila, calm, alert, reserved, was sitting at the open window when he 
awoke again. his breakfast tray stood on a little table beside the bed. 
he raise himself on his elbow and look at his wife. the morning
