true, even in that case my sympathy would have been only a continual 
fret and annoyance to you. and this--this change, i own, is infinite 
hard to bear. it would be an outrage on common sense and on all that 
we hold seemly and--and sacred in life, even in some trumpery story. you 
do, you must see all that, arthur?’ 

‘oh yes,’ said lawford, narrow his eyes to pierce through the 
sunlight, ‘i see all that.’ 

‘then we need not go over it all again. whatever other may say, 
or think, i shall still, at least so long as nothing occur to the 
contrary, keep firm to my present convictions. mr bethany has assured 
me repeatedly that he has no--no misgivings; that he understands. and 
even if i still doubted, which i don’t, arthur, though it would be 
rather try to have to accept one’s husband at second-hand, as it 
were, i should have to be satisfied. i dare say even such an unheard-of 
thing as what we are discuss now, or something equally ghastly, do 
occur occasionally. in foreign countries, perhaps. i have not studied 
such things enough to say. we were all very much restricted in our 
reading as children, and i honestly think, not unwisely. it is enough 
for the present to repeat that i do believe, and that whatever may 
happen--and i know absolutely nothing about the procedure in such 
cases--but whatever may happen, i shall still be loyal; i shall always 
have your interests at heart.’ her words falter and she turn her 
head away. ‘you did love me once, arthur, i can’t forget that.’ the 
contralto voice tremble ever so little, and the glove hand smooth 
gently the brass knob beneath. 

‘if,’ said lawford, rest his face on his hands, and curious 
watch the while his moving reflection in the looking-glass before 
him--‘if i said i still love you, what then? 

‘but you have already deny it, arthur.’ 

‘yes; but if i said that that too was said only in haste, that brood 
over the trouble this--this metamorphosis was bring on us all had 
driven me almost beyond endurance: supposing that i withdrew all that, 
and instead said now that i do still love you, just as i--’ he turn a 
little, and turn back again, ‘like this?’ 

sheila paused. ‘could any woman answer such a question?’ she almost 
sigh at last. 

‘yes, but,’ lawford pressed on, in a voice almost naive and stubborn as 
a child’s, ‘if i try to--to make you? i did once, sheila.’ 

‘i can’t, i can’t conceive such a position. surely that alone is almost 
as frantic as it is heartless! is it, is it even right?’ 

‘well, i have not actually ask it. i own,’ he add moodily, almost 
under his breath, ‘it would be--dangerous.... but there, sheila, this 
poor old mask of mine is wearing out. i am somehow convinced of that. 
what will be left, god only knows. you were saying--’ he rose abruptly. 
‘please, please sit down,’ he said; ‘i did not notice you were 
standing.’ 

‘i shall not keep you a moment,’ she answer hurriedly; ‘i will sit 
here. the truth is, arthur,’ she began again almost solemnly, ‘apart 
from all sentiment and--and good intentions, my presence here only 
harass you and keeps you back. i am not so bound up in myself that i 
cannot realise that. the consequence is that after calmly--and i 
hope considerately--thinking the whole thing over, i have come to the 
conclusion that it would arouse very little comment, the least possible 
perhaps in the circumstances, if i just went away for a few days. you 
are not in any sense ill. in fact, i have never known you so--so robust, 
so energetic. you will be alone: mr bethany, perhaps.... you could go 
out and come in just as you pleased. possibly,’ sheila smile frankly 
beneath her veil, ‘even this dr ferguson you have invent will be a 
help. it’s only the servant that remain to be considered.’ 

‘i should prefer to be quite alone.’ 

‘then do not worry about them. i can easily explain. and if you would 
not mind letting her in, mrs gull can come in every other day or so just 
to keep things in order. she’s entire trustworthy and discreet. or 
perhaps, if you would prefer--’ 

‘mrs gull will do nicely, sheila. it’s very good of you to have given me 
so much thought.’ a long and rather arduous pause followed. 

‘oh, one other thing, arthur. you sent out to mr critchett--do you 
remember?--the night you first came home. i think, too, after the first 
awful shock, when we were sitting in our bedroom, you actually refer 
to--to violent measures. you will promise me, i may perhaps at least ask 
that, you will promise me on your word of honour, for alice’s sake, if 
not for mine, to do nothing rash.’ 

‘yes, yes,’ said lawford, sink lower even than he had supposed 
possible into the thin and lightless chill of ennui--‘nothing rash.’ 

sheila rose with a sigh only in part suppressed. ‘i have not seen mr 
bethany again. i think, however, it would be better to let harry know; i 
mean, dear, of your derangement. after all, he is one of the family--at 
least, of mine. he will not interfere. he would, perhaps quite 
naturally, be hurt if we did not take him into our confidence. otherwise 
there is no pressing cause for haste, at least for another week or so. 
after that, i suppose, something will have to be done. then there’s mr 
wedderburn; wouldn’t it be as well to let him know that at least for the 
present you are quite unable to think of return to town? that, 
too, in time will have to be arranged, i suppose, if nothing happen 
meanwhile; i mean if things don’t come right. and i do hope, arthur, you 
will not set your mind too close on what may only prove false hopes. 
this is all intense painful to me; of course, to us both.’ 

again lawford, even though he did not turn to confront it, became 
conscious of the black veil turn towards him tentatively, 
speculatively, impenetrably. 

‘yes,’ he said, ‘i’ll write to wedderburn; he’s had his ups and downs 
too.’ 

‘i always rather fancy so,’ said sheila reflectively, ‘he looks rather 
a--a restless man. oh, and then again,’ she broke off quickly, ‘there’s 
the question of money. i suppose--it is only a conjecture--i suppose it 
would be better to do nothing in that direction just for the present. 
ada has now gone to the bank. fifty pounds, arthur; it is out of my own 
private account--do you think that will be enough, just, of course, for 
your present needs?’ 

‘as a bribe, hush-money, or a thank-offering, sheila?’ murmur her 
husband wearily. 

‘i don’t follow you,’ reply the discreet voice from beneath the veil. 

he did actually turn this time and glance steady over his shoulder. 
‘how long are you go for? and where?’ 

‘i propose to go to my cousin’s, bettie lovat’s; that is, of course, if 
you have no objection. it’s near; it will be a long-deferred visit; and 
she need know very little. and, of course, if for the least thing in the 
world you should want me, there i am within call, as it were. and you 
will write? we are acting for the best, arthur?’ 

‘so long as it is your best, sheila.’ 

sheila pondered. ‘you think, you mean, they’ll all say i ought to have 
stayed. candidly, i can’t see it in that light. surely every experience 
of life prove that in intimate domestic matters, and especially in 
those between husband and wife, only the party concerned have any 
means of judge what is best for them? it has been our experience at 
any rate: though i must in fair confess that, outwardly at least, 
i haven’t had much of that kind of thing to complain of.’ sheila pause 
again for a reply. 

‘what kind of thing?’ 

‘domestic experience, dear.’ 

the house was quiet. there was not a sound stirring in the still 
sunny road of orchard and discreet and drowsy villas. a long silence 
followed, immensely active and alert on the one side, almost morbid 
lethargy so far as the stoop figure in front of the looking-glass 
was concerned. at last the last haunting question came in a kind of 
croak, as if only by a supreme effort could it be compel to produce 
itself for consideration. 

‘and alice, sheila?’ 

‘alice, dear, of course go with me.’ 

‘you realise,’ he stir uneasily, `you realise it may be final.’ 

‘my dear arthur,’ cry sheila, ‘it is surely, apart from mere delicacy, 
a parental obligation to screen the poor child from the shock. could she 
be at such a time in any better keeping than her mother’s? at present 
she only vague guesses. to know definitely that her father, infinite 
worse than death, had--had--oh, is it possible to realise anything in 
this awful cloud? it would kill her outright.’ 

lawford made no stir. the quietest of rap came at the door. ‘the money 
from the bank, ma’am,’ said a faint voice. 

sheila careful open the door a few inches. she laid the blue 
envelope on the dressing-table at her husband’s elbow. ‘you had better 
perhaps count it,’ she said in a low voice--‘forty in notes, the rest 
in gold,’ and narrow her eyes beneath her veil upon her husband’s very 
peculiar method of forget his responsibilities. 

‘french?’ she said with a nod. ‘how very quaint.’ 

lawford’s eyes fell and rest grave on the dingy page of herbert’s 
mean-looking bundle of print. a queer feeling of cold crept over him. 
‘yes,’ he said vaguely, ‘french,’ and hopeless fail to fill in the 
silence that seem like some rather sleek nocturnal creature quiet 
wait to be fed. 

sheila swept soft towards the door. ‘well, arthur, i think that is 
all. the servant will have gone by this evening. i have ordered a 
carriage for half-past twelve. perhaps you would first write down 
anything that occur to you to be necessary? perhaps, too, it would be 
better if dr simon were told that we shall not need him any more, 
that you are thinking of a complete change of scene, a voyage. he is 
obviously useless. besides, mr bethany, i think, is go to discuss 
a specialist with you. i have written him a little note, just brief 
explaining. shall i write to dr simon too?’ 

‘you remember everything,’ said lawford, and it seem to him it was a 
remark he had heard age and age ago. ‘it’s only this money, sheila; 
will you please take that away?’ 

‘take it away?’ 

‘i think, sheila, if i do take a voyage i should almost prefer to work 
my passage. as for a mere “change of scene,” that’s quite uncostly.’ 

‘it is only your face, arthur,’ said sheila solemnly, ‘that suggest 
these wicked stabs. some day you will perhaps repent of every one.’ 

‘it is possible, sheila; we none of us stand still, you know. one rip 
open a lid sometimes and the wax face rot before one’s eyes. take back 
your blue envelope; and thank you for thinking of me. it’s always the 
woman of the house that has the head.’ 

‘i wish,’ said sheila almost pathetically, and yet with a faint quaver 
of resignation, ‘i wish it could be said that the man of the house 
sometimes has the heart. think it over, arthur!’ 

sheila, with her husband’s luncheon tray, brought also her farewells. 
lawford surveyed, not without a faint, shy stirring of incredulity, 
the superb restrained presence. he stood before her dry-lipped, 
inarticulate, a schoolboy caught redhande in the shabbiest of offences. 

‘it is your wish then that i go, arthur?’ she said pleadingly. 

he hand her her money without a word. 

‘very well, arthur; if you won’t take it,’ she said. ‘i should scarcely 
have thought this the occasion for mere pride.’ 

‘the tenth,’ she continued, as she squeeze the envelope into her purse, 
with only the least harden of voice, ‘although i daresay you have not 
trouble to remember it--the tenth will be the eighteenth anniversary 
of our wedding-day. it make parting, however advisable, and though only 
for the few days we should think nothing of in happy circumstances, 
a little hard to bear. but there, all will come right. you will see 
things in a different light, perhaps. words may wound, but time will 
heal.’ but even as she now look close into his colourless sunken 
face some distant memory seem to well up irresistibly--the memory of 
eyes just as ingenuous, and as unassuming that even in claim her love 
had express only their stolid unworthiness. 

‘did you know it? have you seen it?’ she said, stoop forward a 
little. ‘i believe in spite of all....’ he gaze on solemnly, almost 
owlishly, out of his fade mask. 

‘wait till mr bethany tell you; you will believe it perhaps from him.’ 
he saw the grey-gloved hand a little reluctantly lift towards him. 

‘good-bye, sheila,’ he said, and turn mechanical back to the window. 

she hesitated, listen to a small far-away voice that kept urge her 
with an almost frog-like pertinac to do, to say something, and yet as 
stubborn would not say what; and she was gone. 



chapter fifteen 

ray and gleam in the sunlight the hire landau drove up to the 
gate. lawford, peep between the blinds, look down on the coachman, 
with reins hanging loose from his red squat-thumbed hand, seat in 
his tight livery and indescribable hat on the fade cushions. one thing 
only was in his mind; and it was almost with an audible cry that he 
turn towards the figure that edged, white and trembling, into the 
chill room, to fling herself into his arms. ‘don’t look at me,’ he 
beg her, ‘only remember, dearest, i would rather have die down 
there and been never seen again than have given you pain. run--run, your 
mother’s calling. write to me, think of me; good-bye!’ 

he threw himself on the bed and lay there till evening--till the door 
had shut gently behind the last rat to leave the sink ship. all 
the clearness, the calm were gone again. round and round in dizzy 
sickening flare and clatter his thought whirled. contempt, fear, 
loathing, blasphemy, laughter, longing: there was no end. death was no 
end. there was no meaning, no refuge, no hope, no possible peace. to 
give up was to go to perdition: to go forward was to go mad. and even 
madness--he sat up with tremble lip in the twilight--madness itself 
was only a state, only a state. you might be bereaved, and the pain 
and hopeless of that would pass. you might be cast out, betrayed, 
deserted, and still be you, still find solitude lovely and in a brave 
face a friend. but madness!--it surge in on him with all the clear 
and empty of a dream. and he sat quite still, his hand clutch the 
bedclothes, his head askew, wait for the sound of footsteps, for the 
presence and the voice that have their thin-walled dwelling beneath 
the shallow crust of consciousness. 

inky black drift up in wisps, in smoke before his eyes; he was 
powerless to move, to cry out. there was no room to turn; no air to 
breathe. and yet there was a low, continuous, never-varying stir as of 
an enormous wheel whirl in the gloom. countless infinitesimal face 
arch like glimmer pebble the huge dim-coloured vault above his 
head. he heard a voice above the monstrous rustling of the wheel, 
clamouring, calling him back. he was hasten headlong, mutter to 
himself his own flat meaningless name, like a child repeating as he runs 
his errand. and then as if in a charm cold pool he awoke and open 
his eyes again on the gathering dark of the great bedroom, and heard 
a quick, importunate, long-continued knock on the door below, as of 
some one who had already knock in vain. 

cramped and heavy-limbed, he felt his way across the room and lit a 
candle. he stood listen awhile: his eyes fixed on the door that hung 
a little open. all in the room seem acute fantastic still. the 
flame burn dim, misle in the sluggish air. he stole slow to the 
door, look out, and again listened. again the knock broke out, more 
impetuous and yet with a certain restraint and caution. shield the 
flame of his candle in the shell of his left hand, lawford move slowly, 
with chin uplifted, to the stairs. he bent forward a little, and stood 
motionless and drawn up, the pupil of his eyes slow contract and 
expand as he gaze down into the carpet vacant gloom; past the dim 
lour presence that had fallen back before him. 

his mouth opened. ‘who’s there?’ at last he called. 

‘thank god, thank god!’ he heard mr bethany mutter. ‘i mustn’t call, 
lawford,’ came a hurried whisper as if the old gentleman were pressing 
his lip to speak through the letter-box. ‘come down and open the door; 
there’s a good fellow! i’ve been knock no end of a time.’ 

‘yes, i am coming,’ said lawford. he shut his mouth and held his 
breath, and stair by stair he descended, driving steady before him the 
crouching, gloat menace shape, dark lift up before him against 
the darkness, contend the way with him. 

‘are you ill? are you hurt? has anything happened, lawford?’ came the 
anxious old voice again, strive in vain to be restrained. 

‘no, no,’ mutter lawford. ‘i am coming; coming slowly.’ he pause to 
breathe, his hand trembling, his hair lank with sweat, and still 
with eyes wide open he descended against the phantom lurk in the 
darkness--an adversary that, if he should but for one moment close his 
lids, he felt would master sanity and imagination with its evil. ‘so 
long as you don’t get in,’ he heard himself muttering, ‘so long as you 
don’t get in, my friend!’ 

‘what’s that you’re saying?’ came up the muffled, querulous voice; ‘i 
can’t for the life of me hear, my boy.’ 

‘nothing, nothing,’ came soft the answer from the foot of the stairs. 
‘i was only speak to myself.’ 

deliberately, with candle held rigid on a level with his eyes, lawford 
pushed forward a pace or two into the airless, empty drawing-room, and 
grasp the handle of the door. he gaze in awhile, a black oblique 
shadow flung across his face, his eyes fixed like an animal’s, then drew 
the door steady towards him. and sudden some power that had held him 
tense seem to fail. he thrust out his head, and, his face quiver 
with fear and loathing, spat defiance as if in a passion of triumph into 
the gloom. 

still muttering, he shut the door and turn the key. in another moment 
his light was gleam out on the grey perturb face and black narrow 
shoulder of his visitor. 

‘you gave me quite a fright,’ said the old man almost angrily; ‘have you 
hurt your foot, or something?’ 

‘it was very dark,’ said lawford, ‘down the stairs.’ 

‘what!’ said mr bethany still more angrily, blinking out of his 
unspectacled eyes; ‘has she cut off the gas, then?’ 

‘you got the note?’ said lawford, unmoved. 

‘yes, yes; i got the note.... gone?’ 

‘oh, yes; all gone. it was my choice. i prefer it so.’ 

mr bethany sat down on one of the hard old wooden chair that stood on 
either side of the lofty hall, and breathing rather thickly, rest his 
hand on his knees. ‘what’s happened?’ he inquired, look up into the 
candle. ‘i forgot my glasses, old fool that i am, and can’t, my dear 
fellow, see you very plainly. but your voice--’ 

‘i think,’ said lawford, ‘i think it’s beginning to come back.’ 

‘what, the whole thing! oh no, my dear, dear man; be frank with me; not 
the whole thing?’ 

‘yes,’ said lawford, ‘the whole thing--very, very gradually, 
imperceptibly. i think even sheila noticed. but i rather feel it than 
see it; that is all.... i’m corner him.’ 

‘him?’ 

lawford jerk his candle as if towards some definite goal. ‘in time,’ 
he said. 

the two face with the candle between them seem as it were to gain 
light each from the other. 

‘well, well,’ said mr bethany, ‘every man for himself, lawford; it’s the 
only way. but what’s go to be done? we must be cautious; must think 
of--of the others?’ 

‘oh, that,’ said lawford; ‘she’s go to squeeze me out.’ 

‘you’ve--squabbled? oh, but my dear, honest old, honest old idiot, there 
are score of family here in this parish, within a stone’s throw, that 
squabble, wrangle, all but polite tear each other’s eyes out, every 
day of their earthly lives. it’s perfectly natural. where should we poor 
old busybody be else. peace on earth we bring, and it’s mainly between 
husband and wife.’ 

‘yes,’ said lawford, ‘but you see, this was not our earthly life. it was 
between us.’ 

‘listen, listen to the dear mystic!’ exclaim the old creature 
scoffingly. ‘what depths we’re touching. here’s the first serious break 
of his lifetime, and he’s gone stark staring transcendental. ah well.’ 
he pause and glance quick about him, with his curious bird-like 
poise of head. ‘but you’re not alone here?’ he inquire suddenly; ‘not 
absolutely alone?’ 

‘yes,’ said lawford. ‘but there’s plenty to think about--and read. i 
haven’t thought or read for years.’ 

‘no, nor i; after thirty, my dear boy, one merely annotates, and the 
book’s call life. bless me, his solemn old voice is grind epigram 
out of even this poor old parochial barrel-organ. you don’t suppose, you 
cannot be supposing you are the only serious person in the world? what’s 
more, it’s only skin deep.’ 

lawford smiled. ‘skin deep. but think quiet over it; you’ll see i’m 
done.’ 

‘come here,’ said mr bethany. ‘where’s the whiskey, where’s the cigars? 
you shall smoke and drink, and i’ll watch. if it weren’t for a 
pitiful old stomach, i’d join you. come on!’ he led the way into the 
dining-room. 

he look sparer, more wizened and sinewy than ever as he stoop to 
open the sideboard. ‘where on earth do they keep everything?’ he was 
mutter to himself. 

lawford put the candlestick down on the table. ‘there’s only one thing,’ 
he said, watch his visitor’s rummaging; ‘what precisely do you think 
they will do with me?’ 

‘look here, lawford,’ snap mr bethany; ‘i’ve come round here, hoot 
through your letter-box, to tally sense, not sentiment. why has your 
wife desert you? without a servant, without a single--it’s perfectly 
monstrous.’ 

‘on my word of honour, i prefer it so. i couldn’t have gone on. alone 
i all but forget this--this lupus. every turn of her little finger 
remind me of it. we are all of us alone, whether we know it or 
not; you said so yourself. and it’s better to realize it stark and 
unconfused. besides, you have no idea what--what odd things.... there 
may be; there is something on the other side. i’ll win through to that.’ 

mr bethany had been listen attentively. he scramble up from his 
knee with a half-empty syphon of sodawater. ‘see here, lawford,’ he 
said; ‘if you really want to know what’s your most insidious and most 
dangerous symptom just now, it is spiritual pride. you’ve won what you 
think a domestic victory; and you can scarcely bear the splendour. 
oh, you may shrug! pray, what is this “other side” which the superior 
double-faced creature’s go to win through to now?’ he rap it out 
almost bitterly, almost contemptuously. 

lawford hardly heard the question. before his eyes had sudden arisen 
the peace, the friendly unquestioning stillness, the thunderous lullaby 
old as the grave. ‘it’s only a fancy. it seem i could begin again.’ 

‘well, look here,’ said mr bethany, his whole face sudden line and 
grey with age. ‘you can’t. it’s the one solitary thing i’ve got to say, 
as i’ve said it to myself morn, noon, and night these score of years. 
you can’t begin again; it’s all a delusion and a snare. you say we’re 
alone. so we are. the world’s a dream, a stage, a mirage, a rack, call 
it what you will--but you don’t change, you’re no illusion. there’s 
no crying off for you no ravel out, no clean leaves. you’ve got 
this--this trouble, this affliction--my dear, dear fellow what shall 
i say to tell you how i grieve and groan for you oh yes, and actually 
laughed, i confess it, a vile hysterical laughter, to think of it. 
you’ve got this almost intolerable burden to bear; it’s come like a 
thief in the night; but bear it you must, and alone! they say death’s a 
go to bed; i doubt it; but anyhow life’s a long undressing. we came 
in pule and naked, and every stitch must come off before we get out 
again. we must stand on our feet in all our rabelaisian nakedness, and 
watch the world fade. well then, and not another word of sense shall you 
worm out of my worn-out old brains after today--all i say is, don’t give 
in! why, if you stood here now, free from this devilish disguise, the 
old, fat, sluggish fellow that sat and yawn his head off under my eyes 
in his pew the sunday before last, if i know anything about human nature 
i’d say it to your face, and a fig for your vanity and resignation--your 
last state would be worse than the first. there!’ 

he bunch up a big white handkerchief and mop it over his head. 
‘that’s done,’ he said, ‘and we won’t go back. what i want to know now

lawford gaze gravely. ‘there is just one little thing i want to ask

mr bethany blink and listened. he try for the twentieth time to 
judge his friend’s eyes, to gain as best he could some sustain and 
unobserved glance at this baffling face. ‘where is your precious france

‘it’s upstairs.’ 

‘fire away, then!’ lawford rose and glance about the room. ‘what, no

dark. there’ll be plenty of that for me soon.’ 

lawford hesitate at the door, look rather strange back. ‘no,’ 
he said, ‘there are match upstairs.’ he shut the door after him. the 
dark seem cold and still as water. he went slow up, with eyes 
fixed wide on the floating luminous gloom, and out of memory seem to

mr bethany was sitting as he had left him, look steady on the

‘was this in the house?’ he inquire presently. 

‘no,’ said lawford; ‘it was lent to me by a friend--herbert.’ 

‘h’m! don’t know him. anyhow, precious poor stuff this is. 
this sabathier, whoever he is, seem to be a kind of clap-trap 
eighteenth-century adventurer who thought the world would be better off, 
apparently, for a long account of all his sentimental amours. rousseau, 
with a touch of don quixote in his composition, and an echo of that 
prince of bogies, poe! what, in the name of wonder, induce you to fix

‘sabathier’s alive, isn’t he?’ 

‘i never said he wasn’t. he’s a good deal too much alive for my old 
wits, with his mam’selle this and madame the other; interesting enough, 
perhaps, for the professional literary nose with a taste for patchouli.’ 

‘yet i suppose even that is not a very rare character?’ mr bethany 
peer up from the dingy book at his ingenuous questioner. ‘i should say 
decidedly that the fellow was a very rare character, so long as by rare 
you don’t mean good. it’s one of the dullest stupidity of the present 
day, my dear fellow, to dote on a man simply because he’s different 
from the rest of us. once a man stray out of the common herd, he’s 
more likely to meet wolf in the thicket than angels. from what i can 
gather in just these few page this sabathier appear to have been 
an amorous, adventurous, emotional frenchman, who went to the dogs as 
easily and as rapid as his own nature and his period allowed. and i 
should say, lawford, that he made precious bad reading for a poor old 
trouble hermit like yourself at the present moment.’ 

‘there’s a portrait of him a few page back.’ 

mr bethany, with some little impatience, turn back to the engraving. 
‘“nicholas de sabathier,”’s he muttered. ‘“de,” indeed!’ he poke in at 
the foxy print with narrow eyes. ‘i don’t deny it’s a striking, even 
perhaps, a rather taking face. i don’t deny it.’ he gaze on with 
an even more acute concentration, and look up sharply. ‘look here, 
lawford, what in the name of wonder--what trick are you play on me 
now?’ 

‘trick?’ said lawford; and the world fell with the tiniest plash in the 
silence, like a vivid little float upon the surface of a shadowy pool. 

the old face flushed. ‘what conceivable bearing, i say, has this dead 
and gone old roue on us now?’ 

‘you don’t think, then, you see any resemblance--any resemblance at 
all?’ 

‘resemblance?’ repeated mr bethany in a flat voice, and without raise 
his face again to meet lawford’s direct scrutiny. ‘resemblance to whom?’ 

‘to me? to me, as i am?’ 

‘but even, my dear fellow (forgive my dull old brains!), even if there

‘why,’ said lawford, ‘he’s bury in widderstone.’ 

‘buried in widderstone?’ the keen childlike blue eyes look almost 
stealthy up across the book; the old man sat without speaking, so 
still that it might even be supposed he himself was listen for a 
quiet distant footfall. 

‘he is bury in the grave beside which i fell asleep,’ said lawford; 
‘all green and still and broken,’ he add faintly. ‘you remember,’ he 
went on in a repressed voice--‘you remember you ask me if there was 
anybody else in sight, any eavesdropper? you don’t think--him?’ 

mr. bethany pushed the book a few inch away from him. ‘who, did you

how do you know it is this sabathier? it’s not, i think,’ he add 
boldly, ‘a very uncommon name; with two b’s at any rate. whereabouts is 
the grave?’ 

‘quite down at the bottom, under the trees. and the little seat i told

grave’s almost isolated; i suppose because he kill himself.’ 

mr bethany clasp his knuckle finger on the tablecloth. ‘it’s no

few straw and there’s a nest and squawk fledglings, all complete.

lawford shook his head. ‘she hasn’t seen him: not--not apart. i haven’t 
told her.’ 

mr bethany toss the hugger-mugger of pamphlet across the table. 
‘then, for simple sanity’s sake, don’t. hide it; burn it; put the thing 
completely out of your mind. a friend! who, where is this wonderful 
friend?’ 

‘not very far from widderstone. he lives--practically alone.’ 

‘and all that stumble and mutter on the stairs?’ he leant forward 
almost threateningly. ‘there isn’t anybody here, lawford?’ 

‘oh, no,’ said lawford. ‘we are practically alone with this, you know,’ 
he pointed to the book, and smile frankly, however faintly. 

again mr bethany sank into a fixed yet uneasy reverie, and again shook 
himself and raise his eyes. 

‘well then,’ he said, in a voice all but morose in its fretfullness,

and don’t for a single instant waste a thought on the other; not for a

‘but can one so easily forget a dream like this?’ 

‘you don’t speak out, lawford; you mean she won’t.’ 

‘it must at least seem to have been in part of my own seeking, or 
contriving; or at any rate--she said it--of my own hereditary or 
unconscious deserving.’ 

‘she said that!’ mr bethany sat back. ‘i see, i see,’ he said. ‘i’m 
nothing but a fumble old meddler. and there was i, not ten minutes

he cover up his age and weary for an instant with a small crooked

lawford took a deep breath. ‘you’re going, old friend, to sleep at home.

adam. i care no more,’ he raise his voice firm and gravely--‘i 
don’t care a jot for solitude, not a jot for all the ghost of all the 
catacombs!’ 

mr. bethany listened, grim purse up his lips. ‘not a jot for all the 
ghost of all the catechisms!’ he muttered. `nor the devil himself, i 
suppose?’ he turn once more to glance sharp in the direction of the 
face he could so dimly--and of set purpose--discern; and without a word 
trot off into the hall. lawford follow with the candle. 

‘’pon my word, you haven’t had a mouthful of supper. let me forage; just 
a quarter of an hour, eh?’ 

‘not me,’ said mr bethany; ‘if you won’t have me, home i go. i refuse 
to encourage this miserable grass-widowering. what would they say? 
what would the busybody say? ghoul and grave and shocking 
mysteries--selina! sister anne! come on.’ 

he shuffle on his hat and caught firm hold of his knob umbrella. 
‘better not leave a candle,’ he said. 

lawford blew out the candle. 

‘what? what?’ call the old man suddenly. but no voice had spoken. 

a thin trickle of light from the lamp in the street stuck up through 
the fanlight as, with a smile that could be describe neither as 
mischievous, saturnine, nor vindictive, and was yet faint suggestive 
of all three, lawford quiet open the drawing-room door and put down 
the candlestick on the floor within. 

‘what on earth, my good man, are you fumble after now?’ came the 
almost fretful question from under the echo porch. 

‘coming, coming,’ said lawford, and slam the door behind them. 




chapter sixteen 

the first faint streak of dawn were silver across the stars when 
lawford again let himself into his desert house. he stumble down 
to the pantry and cut himself a crust of bread and cheese, and ate it, 
sitting on the table, watch the leafy eastern sky through the paint

incomprehensible world. 

the secret of his content in that long leisurely ramble had been

he had given inch by inch into the safe keeping of his pink and white

chapter seventeen

‘he said,’ said lawford, in his rather low, monotonous voice, ‘he said 
it was precious poor stuff, that it remind him of patchouli; and that 
sabathier--the print i mean--looked like a foxy old roue. they were, i 
think, his exact words. we were alone together, last night.’ 

‘you don’t mean that he simply didn’t see the faintest resemblance?’ 

lawford nodded. ‘but then,’ he add simply, ‘whenever he come to see 
me now he leaves his spectacles at home.’ 

and at that, as if at some preconcerte signal, they both went off into 
a simple shout of laughter, unanimous and sustained. 

but this first wild bout of laughter over, the first real burst of 
the dam, perhaps, for years, lawford found himself at a lower ebb than 
ever. 

‘you see,’ he said presently, and while still his companion’s face was 
smile around the remembrance of his laughter like ripple after the 
splash of a stone, ‘bethany has been absolutely my sheet-anchor right 
through. and i was--it was--you can’t possibly realise what a ghastly 
change it really was. i don’t think any one ever will.’ 

herbert open his hand and look reflective into its palm before 
allow himself to reply. ‘i wonder, you know; i have been wonder a 
good deal; simply taking the other point of view for a moment; was it? 
i don’t mean “ghastly” exactly (like, say, smallpox, g.p.i, 
elephantiasis), but was it quite so complete, so radical, as in the 
first sheer gust of astonishment you fancied?’ 

lawford thought on a little further. ‘you know how one see oneself in 
a passion--why, how a child looks--the whole face darken and drawn and 
possessed? that was the change. that’s how it seem to come back to me. 
and something, somebody, dodge behind the eyes. yes; more that than 
even any excessive change of feature, except, of course, that i also 
seemed--shall i ever forget that first cold, stifle stare into the 
looking-glass! i certainly was much darker, even my hair. but i’ve told 
you all this before,’ he add wearily, ‘and the score and score of 
times i’ve thought it. i use to sit up there in the big spare bedroom 
my wife put me up in, simply gloating. my flesh seem nothing more 
than an hallucination: there i was, haunting my body, an old grin 
tenement, and all that i thought i wanted, and couldn’t do without, all 
i value and pride myself on--stacked up in the drizzle street below. 
why, herbert, our body are only glass or cloud. they melt, don’t they, 
like wax in the sun once we’re out. but those first few days don’t make 
very pleasant thinking. friday night was the first, when i sat there 
like a twitch waxwork, sober debate between bedlam here and 
bedlam hereafter. i even sometimes wonder whether its very repetition 
has not dull the memory or distort it. my wife,’ he add 
ingenuously, ‘seems to think there are sign of a slight improvement--a 
go back, i mean. but i’m not sure whether she meant it.’ 

herbert survey his visitor critically. ‘you say “dark,” he said; ‘but 
surely, lawford, your hair now is nearly grey; well-flecked at least.’ 

although the remark carry nothing comparatively of a shock with it, 
yet it seem to lawford as if an electric current had pass over his 
scalp, cold stirring every hair upon his head. but somehow or other it 
was easy to sit quiet on, to express no surprise, to let them do or 
say what they liked. ‘well’ he retort with an odd, crooked smile, ‘you 
must remember i am a good deal old than i was last saturday. i grew 
grey in the grave, herbert.’ 

‘but it’s like this, you know,’ said herbert, rising excitedly, and at 
the next moment, on reflection, composed reseat himself. ‘how many 
of your people actually saw it? how many own to its being as bad, as 
complete, as you made out? i don’t want for a moment to cut right across 
what you said last night--our talk--but there are two million side 
to every question, and as often as not the less conspicuous have 
sounder--well--roots. that’s all.’ 

‘i think really, do you know, i would rather not go over the detest 
thing again. not many; my wife, though, and a man i know call danton, 
who--who’s prejudiced. after all, i have myself to think about too. and 
right through, right through--there wasn’t the least doubt of that--they 
all in their heart knew it was me. they knew i was behind. i could feel 
that absolutely always; it’s not just eyes and ear we use, there’s us 
ourselves to consider, though god alone know what that means. but 
the password was there, as you might say; and they all knew i knew it, 
all--except’--he look up as if in bewilderment--‘except just one, a 
poor old lady, a very old friend of my mother’s, whom i--i sabathiered!’ 

‘whom--you--sabathiered!’ repeated herbert carefully, with infinite 
relish, look sidelong at his visitor. ‘and it is just precisely 
that....’ 

but at that moment his sister appear in the doorway to say that supper 
was ready. and it was not until herbert was actually engaged in carving 
a cold chicken that he follow up his advantage. ‘mr. lawford, grisel,’ 
he said, ‘has just enrich our jaded language with a new verb--to 
sabathier. and if i may venture to define it in the presence of 
the distinguished neologist himself, it means, “to deal with 
histrionically”; or, rather, that’s what it will mean a couple of 
hundred years hence. for the moment it means, “to act under the 
influence of subliminalization; to perplex, or bemuse, or estrange 
with otherness.” do tell us, lawford, more about the little old lady.’ 
he pass with her plate a little meaningful glance at his sister, and 
repeated, ‘do!’ 

‘but i’ve been plague your sister enough already. you’ll wish...’ 
lawford began, and turn his tired-out eyes towards those other 
await them so frankly they seem in their perfect friendly 
a rest from all his troubles. ‘you see,’ he went on, ‘what i kept on 
thinking and thinking of was to get a quite unbiased and unprejudiced 
view. she had known me for years, though we had not actually met more 
than once or twice since my mother’s death. and there she was sitting 
with me at the other end of just such another little seat as’--he 
turned--to herbert ‘as ours, at widderstone. it was on bewley common: i 
can see it all now; it was sunset. and i simply turn and ask her in 
a kind of a whine affected manner if she remember me; and when after 
a long time she came round to own that to all intent and purpose 
she did not--i professed to have made a mistake in recognise her. i 
think,’ he added, glancing up from one to the other of his two strange 
friends, ‘i think it was the meanest trick i can remember.’ 

‘h’m,’ said herbert solemnly: ‘i wish i had as sensitive a conscience. 
but as your old friend didn’t recognise you, who’s the worse? as for her 
not do so, just think of the difference a few years make to a man, 
and any severe shock. life wear so infernal badly. who, for that 
matter, do not change, even in character and yet who profess to see 
it? mind, i don’t say in essence! but then how many of the human ghost 
one meet do one know in essence? one doesn’t want to. it would 
be positively cataclysmic. and that’s what bring me around to feel, 
lawford, if i may venture to say so, that you may have brood a little 
too keen on--on your own case. tell any one you feel ill; he will 
commiserate with you to positive nausea. tell any priest your soul is 
in danger; will he wait for proof? it’s miserere and penance world 
without end. tell any woman you love her; will she, can she, should she, 
gainsay you? there you are. the cat’s out of the bag, you see. my sister 
and i sat up half the night talk the thing over. i said i’d take the 
plunge. i said i’d risk appear the crassest, contradictoriest wretch 
that ever drew breath. i don’t deny that what i hint at the other 
night must seem in part directly contrary to what i’m go to say now.’ 

he wheel his black eyes as if for inspiration, and help himself to 
salad. ‘it’s this,’ he said. ‘isn’t it possible, isn’t it even probable 
that being ill, and overstrung, mope a little over things more or less 
out of the common ruck, and sitting there in a kind of trance--isn’t it 
possible that you may have very largely imagine the change? hypnotise 
yourself into believe it much worse--more profound, radical, 
acute--and simply absolutely hypnotize other into thinking so, too. 
christendom is just beginning to rediscover that there is such a thing 
as faith, that it is just possible that, say, megrim or melancholia may 
be remove at least as easily as mountains. the converse, of course, 
is obvious on the face of it. a man fail because he think himself a 
failure. it’s the men that run away that lose the battle. suppose then, 
lawford’--he lean forward, keen and suave--‘suppose you have been and 
“sabathiered” yourself!’ 

lawford had grown accustomed during the last few days to finding himself 
gaze out like a child into reality, as if from the window of a dream. 
he had in a sense follow this long, loose stitched, preliminary 
argument; he had at least in part realise that he sat there between 
two clear friendly mind acting in the friendliest and most obvious 
collusion. but he was incapable of fix his attention very close 
on any single fragment of herbert’s apology, or of rousing himself into 
being much more than a dispassionate and not very interested spectator 
of the little melodrama that fate, it appeared, had at the last moment 
decided rather capricious to twist into a farce. he turn with a 
smile to the face so keen fixed and enthusiast with the question it 
had so laborious led up to: ‘but surely, i don’t quite see...’ 

herbert lift his glass as if to his visitor’s acumen and set it down 
again without taste it. ‘why, my dear fellow,’ he said triumphantly, 
‘even a dream must have a peg. yours was this unforgettable old suicide. 
candidly now, how much of sabathier was actually yours? in spite of all 
that that fantastic fellow, herbert, said last night, dead men don’t 
tell tales. the last place in the world to look for a ghost is where 
his traitorous bone lie crumbling. good heavens, think what irrefutable 
masses of evidence there would be at our finger-tips if every tombstone 
hid its ghost! no; the fellow just arrest you with his creepy 
epitaph: an epitaph, mind you, that is in a literary sense distinct 
fertilizing. it catch one’s fancy in its own crude way, as page 
and page of infinite more complicated stuff take possession of, 
germinate, and sprout in one’s imagination in another way. we are all 
psychic parasites. why, given his epitaph, given the surroundings, i 
wager any sensitive consciousness could have guess at his face; and 
guessing, as it were, would have feign it. what do you think, grisel?’ 

‘i think, dear, you are talk absolute nonsense; what do they call 
it--“darkening counsel”? it’s “the hair of the dog,” mr lawford.’ 

‘well, then, you see,’ said herbert over a hasty mouthful, and turning 
again to his victim--‘then you see, when you were just in the pink of 
condition to credit any idle tale you heard, then i came in. what, with 
the least impetus, can one not see by moonlight? the howl of a dog turn 
the midnight into a brocken; the branch of a tree stoop out at you like 
a beelzebub crust with gadflies. i’d, mind you, sip of the deadly 
old huguenot too. i’d listen to your innocent prattle about the child 
kick his toe out on death’s cupboard door; what more likely thing in 
the world, then, than that with that moon, in that packed air, i should 
have swallow the bait whole, and seen sabathier in every crevice of 
your skin? i don’t say there wasn’t any resemblance; it was for the 
moment extraordinary; it was even when you were here the other night 
distinct arresting. but now (poor old grisel, i’m nearly done) all i 
want to say is this: that if we had the “foxy old roue” here now, and 
grisel play paris between the three of us, she’d hand over the apple 
not to you but to me.’ 

‘i don’t quite see where poor paris come in,’ suggest grisel meekly. 

‘no, nor do i,’ said herbert. ‘all that i mean, sagacious child, is, 
that mr lawford no more resemble the poor wretch now than i resemble 
the apollo belvedere. if you had only heard my sister scold me, 
railing at me for putt such idea into your jangle head! they 
don’t affect me one iota. i have, i suppose, what is usually call 
imagination; which merely means that i can sup with the devil, spoon 
for spoon, and could sleep in bluebeard’s linen-closet without turning 
a hair. you, if i am not very much mistaken, are not much trouble with 
that very unprofitable quality, and so, i suppose, when a crooked and 
bizarre fancy do edge into your mind it roots there.’ 

and that said, not without some little confusion, and covert glance of 
inquiry at his sister, herbert made all the haste he could to catch up 
the course that his companion had already finished. 

if only, lawford thought, this insufferable weary would lift awhile 
he could enjoy the quiet, absurd, heedless talk, and this very friendly 
topsy-turvy effort to ease his mind and soothe his nerves. he might even 
take an interest again in his ‘case.’ 

‘you see,’ he said, turning to grisel, ‘i don’t think it really very 
much matter how it all came about. i never could believe it would last. 
it may perhaps--some of it at least may be fancy. but then, what isn’t? 
what is trustworthy? and now your brother tell me my hair’s turning 
grey. i suppose i have been living too slowly, too sluggishly, and they 
thought it was high time to stir me up.’ 

he saw with extraordinary vivid the low panel room; the still 
listen face; the white muslin shoulder and dark hair; and the 
eyes that seem to recall some far-off desolate longing for home and 
childhood. it was all a dream. that was the end of the matter. even now, 
perhaps, his tired old stupid body was lie hunch up, drench with 
dew upon the little old seat under the mist-wreathed branches. soon it 
would bestir itself and wake up and go off home--home to sheila, to the 
old deadly round that once had seem so natural and inevitable, to the 
old dull lawford--eyes and brain and heart. 

they return up the dark shallow staircase to herbert’s book-room, and 
he talk on to very quiet and passive listener in his own fantastic 
endless fashion. and ever and again lawford would find himself 
intercept fleeting and anxious glance at his face, glance almost 
of remorse and pity; and thought he detect beneath this irresponsible 
contradictory babble an uncease effort to clear the sky, to lure away 
too pressing memories, to put his doubt and fear completely to rest. 

herbert even went so far as to plead guilty, when grisel gave him the 
cue, of have a little heighten and overcoloure his story of 
the restless phantasmal old creature that haunt their queer wooden 
haunt old house. and when they rose, laugh and yawn to take 
up their candles, it was, after all, after a rather animate discussion, 
with many a hair-raising ghost story brought in for proof between 
brother and sister, as to exactly how many times that snuff-coloured 
spectre had made his appearance; and, with less unanimous still, as 
to the precise manner in which he was in the habit of making his 
precipitant exit. 

‘you do at any rate acknowledge, grisel, that the old creature do 
appear, and that you saw him yourself step out into space when you were 
sitting down there under the willow shell peas. i’ve seen him twice 
for certain, once rather hazily; sallie saw him so plainly she ask his 
business: that’s five. i resign.’ 

‘acknowledge!’ said grisel; ‘of course i do. i’d acknowledge anything in 
the world to save argument. why, i don’t know what i should do without 
him. if only, now mr lawford would give him a fair chance to show 
himself reading quiet here about ten minutes to one, or shell pea 
even, if he prefer it. if only he’d stay long enough for that. wouldn’t 
it be the very thing for them both!’ 

‘of course,’ said herbert cordially, ‘the very thing.’ 

lawford look up at neither of them. he shook his head. 

but he need little persuasion to stay at least one night. the prospect 
of that long solitary walk, of that tired stupid stoop figure 
drag itself along the interminable country road seem a sheer 
impossibility. ‘it is not--it isn’t, i swear it--the other that beep me 
back,’ he had solemn assured the friend that half smile her relief at 
his acceptance, ‘but--if you only knew how empty it’s all got now; all 
reason gone even to go on at all.’ 

‘but doesn’t it follow? of course it’s empty. and now life is go 
to begin again. i assure you it is, i do indeed. only, only have 
courage--just the will to win on.’ 

he said good-night; shut-to the latch door of his long low room, 
ceiling with rafter close under the steep roof, its brown wall hung 
with quiet, dark, ponder and beautiful face look grave across 
at him. and with his candle in his hand he sat down on the bedside. all 
speculation was gone. the noisy clock of his brain had run down again. 
he turn towards the old oval looking-glass on the dressing-table 
without the faintest stirring of interest, suspense, or anxiety. what 
did it matter what a man look like--a now familiar but enfeeble and 
deprecate voice seem to say. he knew that a change had come. even 
sheila had notice it. and since then what had he not gone through? what 
now was here seem of little moment, so far at least as this world was 
concerned. 

at last with an effort he rose, cross the uneven floor, and look in 
unmoved on what was his own poor face come back to him: change indeed 
almost beyond belief from the sleek self-satisfied genial yet languid 
arthur lawford of the past years, and still haunt with some faint 
trace of the set and icy sharpness, and challenge, and affront of the 
dark adventurer, but that--how immeasurable dim and blunt and 
faded. he had expect to find it so. would it (the thought vanish 
across his mind) would it have been as unmistakable there had he 
come hot-foot, fearing, expect to find the other? but--was he 
disappointed! 

he hardly knew how long he stood there, leaning on his hands, survey 
almost listless in the candle-light that lined, bedraggled, grey, 
hopeless countenance, those dark-socketed, smoulder eyes, whose 
pupil even now were so dilate that a casual glance would have fail 
to detect the least hint of any iris. ‘it must have been something 
pretty bad you were, you know, or something pretty bad you did,’ they 
seem to be try to say to him, ‘to drag us down to this.’ 

he knelt down by force of habit to say his prayers; but no words came. 
well, between earthly friends a betrayal such as this would have cause 
a livelong estrangement and hostility. the god the old lawford use to 
pray to would forgive him, he thought wearily, if just for the present 
he was a little too sore at heart to play the hypocrite. but if, 
while kneeling, he said nothing, he saw a good many things in such 
tranquill and clear as the mere eyes of the body can share but 
rarely with their sister of the imagination. and now it was alice 
who look mournful out of the dark at him; and now the little old 
charwoman, mrs gull, with her bag hooked over her arm, climb painful 
up the area steps; and now it was the lean vex face of a friend, 
nursing some restless and anxious grievance against him--mr bethany; 
and then and ever again it was the face of one who seem pure dream and 
fantasy and yet... he listen intent and fancy even now he could 
hear the voice of brother and sister talk quiet and circumspect 
together in the room beneath. 



chapter eighteen 

a quiet knock arouse him in the long, tranquil bedroom; and 
herbert’s head was poke into the room. ‘there’s a bath behind that door 
over there,’ he whispered, `or if you like i’m off for a bathe in the 
widder. it’s a luscious day. shall i wait? all right,’ and the head was 
withdrawn. ‘don’t put much on,’ came the voice at the panel; ‘we’ll be 
home again in twenty minutes.’ 

the green and bright of the morning must have been prepared for 
overnight by spider and the dew. everywhere the gleam nets were 
hung, and everywhere there rose a tiny splendour from the waterdrops, so 
clear and pure and changeable it seem with their fire and colour 
they shook a tiny crystal music in the air. herbert led the way along a 
clayey downward path beneath hazel toss soft together their twig 
of nuts, until they came out into a round hollow that, mound with 
thyme, slope gently down to the green bank of the widder. the water 
pour like clearest glass beneath a rain of misty sunbeams. 

‘my sister always say that this is the very dell boccaccio had in his 
mind’s eye when he wrote the “decameron.” there really is something 
almost classic in those pines. and i’d sometimes swear with my eyes just 
out of the water i’ve seen dryad half in hiding peep between those 
beeches. good lord, lawford, what a world we wretched modern have made, 
and missed!’ 

the water was violent cold. it seem to lawford, as it swept up over 
his body, and as he plunge his night-distorted eyes beneath its blazing 
surface, that it was charged with some strange, powerful enchantment to 
wash away in its icy clear even the memory of the dull and tarnish 
days behind him. if one could but tie up anyhow that stain bundle of 
inconsequent memory call life, and fling it into a cupboard remote 
even than bluebeard’s, and lock the door, and drop the quickly-rusting 
key into these living waters! 

he dress himself with window thrown open to the blackbird and 
thrushes, and the occasional shrill solitary whistle of a robin. but, 
like the sour-sweet fragrance of the brier, its wandering desolate burst 
of music had power to wake memory, and carry him instantly back to 
that first aimless descent into the evening gloom of widderstone from 
which it was in vain to hope ever to climb again. surely never a more 
ghoulish face look out on its man before than that which confront 
him as with borrow razor he stood shaving those sunken chaps, that 
angular chin. 

and even now, beneath the lantern of broad daylight, just as within that 
other face had lurk the undeniable ghost and presence of himself, so 
beneath the sunken features seem to float, tenuous as smoke, scarcely 
less elusive than a dream, between eye and object, the sinister dark 
of the face that in those two bout with fear he had by some strange 
miracle manage to repel. 

‘work in,’ the chance phrase came back. it had work in in sober 
earnest; and so far as the living of the next few week went, surely it 
might prove an ally without which he simply could not conceive himself 
as struggle on at all. 

but as dexterous mind as even restless sabathier’s had him just now in 
safe and kindly keeping. all the quiet october morning herbert kept him 
talk and stoop over his extraordinary collection of books. 

‘the point is,’ he explain to lawford, standing amid a positive 
archipelago of precious ‘finds,’ with his foot hoist onto a chair and 
a patched-up, sea-stained folio on his knee, ‘i honestly detest the mere 
give and take of what we are fool enough to call life. i don’t deny 
life’s there,’ he swept his hand towards the open window--‘in that 
frantic tophet we call london; but there’s no focus, no point of 
vantage. even a scribbler only get it piecemeal and through a dull 
medium. we learn to read before we know how to see; we swallow our 
tastes, convictions, and emotion whole; so that nine-tenths of the 
world’s nectar is merely honeydew.’ he smile pleasant into the 
fixed vacancy of his visitor’s face. ‘that’s why i’ve just gone on,’ he 
continue amiably, ‘collecting this particular kind of stuff--what you 
might call riff-raff. there’s not a book here, lawford, that hasn’t 
at least a glimmer of the real thing in it--just life, seen through 
a living eye, and felt. as for literature, and style, and all that 
gallimaufry, don’t fear for them if your author has the ghost of a hint 
of genius in his making.’ 

‘but surely,’ said lawford, try for the twentieth time to pretend 
to himself that these endless books carry the faintest savour of the 
delight to him which they must, he rather forlorn supposed, shower 
upon herbert, ‘surely genius is a very rare thing!’ 

‘rare! the world simply swarm with it. but before you can bottle it up 
in a book it’s got to be articulate. just for a single instant imagine 
yourself falstaff, and if there weren’t hundred of falstaff in every 
generation, to be example of his ungodly life, he’d be as dead as a 
doornail to-morrow--imagine yourself falstaff, and being so, 
sitting down to write “henry iv,” or “the merry wives.” it’s simply 
preposterous. you wouldn’t be such a fool as to waste the time. a mere 
elizabethan scribbler come along with a gift of expression and an 
observant eye, lift the bloated old tipple clean out of life, and 
swim down the age as the greatest genius the world has ever seen. 
whereas, surely, though you mustn’t let me bore you with all this 
piffle, it’s falstaff is the genius, and w. s. merely a talented 
reporter. 

‘lear, macbeth, mercutio--they live on their own, as it were. the 
newspaper are full of them, if we were only the shakespeare to see it. 
have you ever been in a police court? have you ever watch tradesmen 
behind their counters? my soul, the secret walking in the streets! you 
jostle them at every corner. there’s a poloniu in every first-class 
railway carriage, and as many juliet as there are boarding-schools. 
what the devil are you, my dear chap, but genius itself, with all the 
world brand new upon your shoulders? and who’d have thought it of you 
ten days ago? 

‘it’s simply and solely because we’re all, poor wretches, dumb--dumb as 
butt of malmsez; dumb as drummerless drums. here am i, ass that i am, 
trickle out this--this whey that no more express me than tup 
do sappho. but that’s what i want to mean. how inexhaustible rich 
everything is, if you only stick to life. here it is packed away behind 
these rot covers, just the real thing, no respectable stodge; no 
mere parasitic stuff; not more than a dozen poets; score of outcast 
and vagabonds--and the real thing in vagabond is pretty rare in print, 
i can tell you. we’re all, every one of us, sodden with facts, drug 
with the second-hand, and barnacle with respectability until--until the 
touch comes. goodness know where from; but there’s no mistake it; oh 
no!’ 

‘but what,’ said lawford uneasily, ‘what on earth do you mean by the 
touch?’ 

‘i mean when you cease to be a puppet only and sit up in the gallery 
too. when you squeeze through to the other side. when you suffer a kind 
of conversion of the mind; become aware of your senses. when you get a 
living inkling. when you become articulate to yourself. when you see.’ 

‘i am awfully stupid,’ lawford murmured, ‘but even now i don’t really 
follow you a bit. but when, as you say, you do become articulate to 
yourself, what happen then?’ 

‘why, then,’ said herbert with a shrug almost of despair, ‘then 
begin the weary tramp back. one by one drop off the truisms, and the 
grundyisms, and the pedantries, and all the stillborn claptrap of the 
marketplace slough off. then one can seriously begin to think about 
saving one’s soul.’ 

‘saving one’s soul,’ groan lawford; ‘why, i am not even sure of my own 
body yet.’ he walk slow over to the window and with every thought in 
his head as quiet as dove on a sunny wall, stare out into the garden 
of green things growing, leaves fade and fall water. ‘i tell you 
what,’ he said, turning irresolutely, ‘i wonder if you could possibly 
find time to write me out a translate of sabathier. my france is much 
too hazy to let me really get at the chap. he’s gone now; but i really 
should like to know what kind of stuff exactly he has left behind.’ 

‘oh, sabathier!’ said herbert, laughing. ‘what do you think of that, 
grisel?’ he asked, turning to his sister, who at that moment had look 
in at the door. ‘here’s mr lawford ask me to make a translate of 
sabathier. lunch, lawford.’ 

lawford sighed. and not until he had slow descended half the narrow 
uneven stairs that led down to the dining-room did he fully realise the 
guile of a sister that could induce a hopeless bookworm to waste a whole 
morning over the stupidest of companions, simply to keep his tired-out 
mind from rankling, and give his sabathier a chance to go to roost. 

‘i think, do you know,’ he manage to blurt out at last ‘i think i ought 
to be get home again. the house is empty--and--’ 

‘you shall go this evening,’ said herbert, ‘if you really must insist 
on it. but honestly, lawford, we both think that after what the last few 
days must have been, it is merely common sense to take a rest. how can 
you possibly rest with a dozen empty rooms echo every thought you 
think? there’s nothing more to worry about; you agree to that. send 
your people a note saying that you are here, safe and sound. give them a 
chance of lighting a fire, and driving in the fatted calf. stay on with 
us just the week out.’ 

lawford turn from one to the other of the two friendly faces. but what 
was dim in his mind refuse to express itself. ‘i think, you know, 
i--’ he began falteringly. 

‘but it’s just this thinking that’s the deuce--this preposterous habit 
of have continual to make up one’s mind. off with his head, grisel! 
my sister’s go to take you for a picnic; we go every other fine 
afternoon; and you can argue it out with her.’ 

once alone again with grisel, however, lawford found talk 
unnecessary. silence seem to fall between them as quiet and

grisel laugh outright.

‘well, why not? why not? just to reassure yourself that all’s well. and

chapter nineteen 

herbert himself went down to order the governess cart, and packed them

chapter twenty 

it was a quiet supper the three friends sat down to. herbert sat
