impossible dream? her face with its strange smile, her eyes with their 
still pity and rapt courage had taken hope away. ‘here’s not your rest,’ 
cry one insistent voice; ‘she is the mystery that haunt day and 
night, past all the change of the restless hours. chance has given you 
back eyes to see, a heart that can be broken. chance and the stirring 
of a long-gone life have torn down the veil age spin so thick and fast. 
pride and ambition; what dull fool men are! effort and duty, what dull 
fool men are!’ he listen on and on to these phantom pleadings and to 
the rather coarse old lawford conscience grunt them merciless down, 
too weary even to try to rest. 

rook at dawn came sweeping beneath the turquoise of the sky. he saw 
their sharp-beaked head turn this way, that way, as they float on 
outspread wings across the misty world. except for the hoarse roar of 
the water under the huge thin-leafed trees, not a sound was stirring. 
‘one thing,’ he seem to hear himself mutter as he turn with a shiver 
from the morning air, ‘it won’t be for long. you can, at least, poor 
devil, wait the last act out.’ if in this foolish hustle mob of the 
world, hire anywhere and anywhen for the one poor dubious wage of a 
penny--if it was only his own small dull part to carry a mock spear, 
and shout huzza with the rest--there was nothing for it, he grunt 
obstinate to himself, shout he would with the loudest. 

he threw himself on to the bed with eyes so weary with want of sleep 
it seem they had lost their livelong skill in finding it. not the echo 
of triumph nor even a sigh of relief stir the torpor of his mind. he 
knew vague that what had been the misery and madness of the last few 
days was gone. but the thought had no power to move him now. sheila’s 
good sense, and mr bethany’s stubborn loyalty were alike old story 
that had lost their savour and meaning. gone, too, was the need for that 
portentous family gathering that had sat so often in his fancy during 
these last few days around his dining-room table, discuss with futile 
decorum the problem of how to hush him up, to muffle him down. half 
dreaming, half awake, he saw the familiar door slow open and, like the 
timely hero in a melodrama, his own figure appear before the stricken 
and astonish company. his eyes open half-fearfully, and glance up 
in the morning twilight. their perplexity gave place to a quiet, almost 
vacant smile; the lid slow closed again, and at last the lean hand 
twitch awhile in sleep. 

next morning he spent rummage among the old books, dip listless 
here and there as the tasteless fancy took him, while herbert sat 
writing with serene face and lift eyebrow at his open window. but the 
unfamiliar long s’s, the close type, and the spelling of the musty old 
books weary eye and mind. what he read, too, however far-fetched, 
or lively, or sententious, or gross, seem either to be of the same 
texture as what had become his everyday experience, and so baffle him 
with its nearness, or else was only the meaningless rambling of an idle 
pen. and this, he thought to himself, look covert up at the spruce 
clear-cut profile at the window, this is what herbert had call life. 

‘am i interrupt you, herbert; are you very busy?’ he ask at last, 
taking refuge on a chair in a far corner of the room. 

‘bless me, no; not a bit--not a bit,’ said herbert amiably, lay down 
his pen. ‘i’m afraid the old leatherjacket have been bore you. it’s 
a habit this beastly reading; this gorge and glint and fever all at 
second-hand--purely a bad habit, like morphia, like laudanum. but once 
in, you know there’s no recovery anyhow, i’m neck-deep, and to struggle 
would be simply to drown.’ 

‘i was only go to say how sorry i am for have left sabathier at 
home.’ 

‘my dear fellow--’ began herbert reassuringly. 

‘it was only because i want so very much to have your translation. i 
get muddle up with other things grope through the dictionary.’ 

herbert survey him critically. ‘what exactly is your interest now, 
lawford? you don’t mean that my old “theory” has left any sting now?’ 

‘no sting; oh no. i was only curious. but you yourself still think it 
really, don’t you?’ 

herbert turn for a moment to the open window. 

‘i was simply try then to find something to fit the fact as you 
experienced them. but now that the fact have gone--and they have, 
haven’t they?--exit, of course, my theory!’ 

‘i see,’ was the cryptic answer. ‘and yet, herbert,’ lawford solemn 
began again, ‘it has change me; even in my way of thinking. when i shut 
my eyes now--i only discover it by chance--i see immediately face 
quite strange to me; or places, sometimes throng with people; and once 
an old well with some one sitting in the shadow. i can’t tell you how 
clearly, and yet it is all altogether different from a dream. even when 
i sit with my eyes open, i am conscious, as it were, of a kind of faint, 
colourless mirage. in the old days--i mean before widderstone, what 
i saw was only what i’d seen already. nothing came uncall for, 
unexplained. this make the old life seem so blank; i did not know what 
extraordinarily real things i was do without. and whether for that 
reason or another, i can’t quite make out what in fact i did want then, 
and was always fret and strive for. i can see no wisdom or purpose 
in anything now but to get to one’s journey’s end as quick and brave 
as one can. and even then, even if we do call life a journey, and death 
the inn we shall reach at last in the evening when it’s over; that, too, 
i feel will be only as brief a stopping-place as any other inn would 
be. our experience here is so scanty and shallow--nothing more than the 
moment of the continual present. surely that must go on, even if one 
do call it eternity. and so we shall all have to begin again. probably 
sabathier himself.... but there, what on earth are we, herbert, when all 
is said? who is it has--has done all this for us--what kind of self? 
and to what possible end? is it that the clockwork has been wound up and 
must still jolt on a while with jar wheels? will it never run down, 
do you think?’ 

herbert smile faintly, but made no answer. 

‘you see,’ continue lawford, in the same quiet, dispassionate 
undertone, ‘i wouldn’t mind if it was only myself. but there are so many 
of us, so many selves, i mean; and they all seem to have a voice in the 
matter. what is the reality to this infernal dream?’ 

‘the reality is, lawford, that you are fret your life out over this 
rotten illusion. be guide by me just this once. we’ll go, all three 
of us, a good ten-mile walk to-day, and thorough tire you out. and 
to-night you shall sleep here--a really sound, refreshing sleep. then 
to-morrow, whole and hale, back you shall go; honestly. it’s only 
professional strong men should ask questions. babe like you and me must 
keep to slops.’ 

so, though lawford made no answer, it was agreed. before noon the three 
of them had set out on their walk across the fields. and after rambling 
on just as caprice took them, past redden blackberry bush and 
copse of hazel, and flaming beech, they sat down to spread out their 
meal on the slope of a hill, overlook quiet plough field and 
graze cattle. herbert stretch himself with his back to the earth, 
and his placid face to the pale vacant sky, while lawford, even more 
dispirit after his walk, wander up to the crest of the hill. 

at the foot of the hill, upon the other side, lay a farm and its 
out-buildings, and a pool of water beneath a group of elms. it was 
vacant in the sunlight, and the water vivid green with a scum of weed. 
and about half a mile beyond stood a cluster of cottage and an old 
tower church. he gaze idle down, listen vague to the wail of 
a curlew flit anxious to and fro above the broken solitude of 
its green hill. and it seem as if a thin and dark cloud began to be 
quiet withdrawn from over his eyes. hill and wail cry and barn and 
water fade out. and he was staring as if in an endless still at an 
open window against which the sun was beating in a bristle torrent of 
gold, while out of the garden beyond came the voice of some evening bird 
singing with such an unspeakable ecstasy of grief it seem it must 
be perch upon the confines of another world. the light gather to a 
radiance almost intolerable, driving back with its rain beam some 
memory, forlorn, remorseless, remote. his body stood dark and senseless, 
rock in the air on the hillside as if bereft of its spirit. then his 
hand were drawn over his eyes. he turn unsteadi and made his way, 
as if through a thick, drizzle haze, slow back. 

‘what is that--there?’ he said almost menacingly, standing with 
bloodshot eyes look down upon herbert. 

‘“that!”--what?’ said herbert, glancing up startle from his book. ‘why, 
what’s wrong, lawford?’ 

‘that,’ said lawford sullenly, yet with a faint mournful cadence in 
his voice; ‘those field and that old empty farm--that village over 
there? why did you bring me here?’ 

grisel had not stirred. ‘the village...’ 

‘ssh!’ she said, catching her brother’s sleeve; ‘that’s detcham, yes, 
detcham.’ 

lawford turn wide vacant eyes on her. he shook his head and shuddered. 
‘no, no; not detcham. i know it; i know it; but it has gone out of my 
mind. not detcham; i’ve been there before; don’t look at me. horrible, 
horrible. it take me back--i can’t think. i stood there, trying, 
trying; it’s all in a blur. don’t ask me--a dream.’ 

grisel lean forward and touched his hand. ‘don’t think; don’t even 
try. why should you? we can’t; we mustn’t go back.’ 

lawford, still gaze fixedly, turn again a darken face towards 
the steep of the hill. ‘i think, you know,’ he said, stoop and 
whispering, ‘he would know--the window and the sun and the singing. and 
oh, of course it was too late. you understand--too late. and once... you 
can’t go back; oh no. you won’t leave me? you see, if you go, it would 
only be all. i could not be quite so alone. but detcham--detcham? 
perhaps you will not trust me--tell me? that was not the name.’ 
he shudder violent and turn dog-like beseech eyes. 
‘to-morrow--yes, to-morrow,’ he said, ‘i will promise anything if you 
will not leave me now. once--’ but again the thread running so faint 
through that inextricable maze of memory elude him. ‘so long as you 
won’t leave me now!’ he implore her. 

she was vain try to win back her composure, and could not answer 
him at once.... 

in the evening after supper grisel sat her guest down in front of a big 
wood fire in the old book-room, where, staring into the play flames, 
he could fall at peace into the almost motionless reverie which he 
seem merely to harass and weary himself by try to disperse. she 
open the little piano at the far end of the room and play on and 
on as fancy led--chopin and beethoven, a fugue from bach, and lovely 
forlorn old english airs, till the music seem not only a voice 
persuading, pondering, and lamenting, but gather about itself the 
hollow surge of the water and the darkness; wistful and clear, as the 
thought of a solitary child. ever and again a log burnt through its 
strength, and fall amid sparks, stirred, like a restless animal, the 
stillness; or herbert in his corner lift his head to glance towards 
his visitor, and to turn another page. at last the music, too, fell 
silent, and lawford stood up with his candle in his hand and eye with a 
strange fixity brother and sister. his glance wander slow round the 
quiet flame-lit room. 

‘you won’t,’ he said, stoop towards them as if in extreme confidence, 
‘you won’t much notice? they come and go. i try not to--to speak. it’s 
the only way through. it is not that i don’t know they’re only dreams. 
but if once the--the other thought there had been any tampering’--he 
tap his forehead meaningly--‘here: if once they thought that, it 
would, you know, be quite over then. how could i prove...?’ he turn 
cautious towards the door, and with laborious significance nod his 
head at them. 

herbert bent down and held out his long hand to the fire. ‘tampering, 
my dear chap: that’s what the lump said to the leaven.’ 

‘yes, yes,’ said lawford, putt out his hand, ‘but you know what 
i mean, herbert. anything i try to do then would be quite, quite 
hopeless. that would be poison the wells.’ 

they watch him out of the room, and listen till quite distinct in 
the still night-shaded house they heard his door gently close. then, as 
if by consent, they turn and look long and questioning into each 
other’s faces. 

‘then you are not afraid?’ herbert said quietly. 

grisel gaze steady on, and almost imperceptible shook her head. 

‘you mean?’ he question her; but still he had again to read her answer 
in her eyes. 

‘oh, very well, grisel,’ he said quietly, ‘you know best,’ and return 
once more to his writing. 

for an hour or two lawford slept heavily, so heavy that when a little 
after midnight he awoke, with his face towards the uncurtained window, 
though for many minutes he lay bright confront all orion, that from 
blazing helm to flaming dog at heel fill high the glimmer square, 
he could not lift or stir his cold and leaden limbs. he rose at last and 
threw off the burden of his bedclothes, and rest awhile, as if free 
from the heavy of an unrememberable nightmare. but so clear was his 
mind and so extraordinarily refresh he seem in body that sleep for 
many hour would not return again. and he spent almost all the remainder 
of the lagging dark pace soft to and fro; one face only before 
his eyes, the one sure thing, the one thing unattainable in a world of 
phantoms. 

herbert wait on in vain for his guest next morning, and after 
wandering up and down the mossy lawn at the back of the house, went off 
cheerful at last alone for his dip. when he return lawford was in 
his place at the breakfast-table. he sat on, moody and constrained, 
until even herbert’s haphazard talk trickle low. 

‘i fancy my sister is nursing a headache,’ he said at last, ‘but she’ll 
be down soon. and i’m afraid from the looks of you, lawford, your night 
was not particularly restful.’ he felt his way very heedfully. ‘perhaps 
we walk you a little too far yesterday. we are so use to tramp 
that--’ lawford kept thoughtful eyes fixed on the deprecate face. 

‘i see what it is, herbert--you are humour me again. i have 
been wrack my brains in vain to remember what exactly did happen 
yesterday. i feel as if it was all sunk ocean deep in sleep. i get so 
far--and then i’m done. it won’t give up a hint. but you really mustn’t 
think i’m an invalid, or--or in my second childhood. the truth is,’ he 
added, ‘it’s only my first, come back again. but now that i’ve got so 
far, now that i’m really better, i--’ he broke off rather vacantly, as 
if afraid of his own confidence. ‘i must be get on,’ he sum 
up with an effort, ‘and that’s the solemn fact. i keep on forget 
i’m--i’m a ratepayer!’ 

herbert sat round in his chair. ‘you see, lawford, the very term is 
little else than double-dutch to me. as a matter of fact grisel send 
all my hush-money to the horrible people that do the clean up, as 
it were. i can’t catch their drift. government to me is merely the 
spectacle of the clever, or the specious, manage the dull. it deal 
merely with the physical, and just the fringe of consciousness. i am not 
joking. i think i follow you. all i mean is that the obligations--mainly 
tepid, i take it--that are lure you back to the fold would be the 
very one that would scare me quickest off. the imagination, the appeal 
faded: we’re dead.’ 

lawford open his mouth; ‘temporarily tepid,’ he at last all but 
cough out. 

‘oh yes, of course,’ said herbert intelligently. ‘only temporarily. it’s 
this beastly gregarious that’s the devil. the very thought of it 
undo me--with an absolute shock of sheepishness. i sudden realise 
my human nakedness: that here we are, little better than naked animals, 
bleat behind our illusory wattle on the slope of--of infinity. 
and nakedness, after all, is a wholesome thing to realize only when one 
think too much of one’s clothes. i peer sometimes, feeble enough, out 
of my wool, and it seem to me that all these busybodies, all these 
fact-devourers, all this news-reading rabble, are nothing bright than 
very dull-witted children try to play an imaginative game, much too 
deep for their poor reasons. i don’t mean that your wanting to go home 
is anything gregarious, but i do think their insist on your coming 
back at once might be. and i know you won’t visit this stuff on me as 
anything more than just my “scum,” as grisel call the fine flower of my 
maiden meditations. all that i really want to say is that we should both 
be more than delight if you’d stay just as long as it will not be a 
bore for you to stay. stay till you’re heartily tired of us. go back 
now, if you must; tell them how much better you are. bolt off to a nerve 
specialist. he’ll say complete rest--change of scene, and all that. they 
all do. instinct via intellect. and why not take your rest here? we 
are such miserably dull company to one another it would be a greater 
pleasure to have you with us than i can say. i mean it from the very 
bottom of my heart. do!’ 

lawford listened. ‘i wish--,’ he began, and stop dead again. ‘anyhow, 
i’ll go back. i am afraid, herbert, i’ve been play truant. it was all 
very well while--to tell you the truth i can’t think quite straight yet. 
but it won’t last for ever. besides--well, anyhow, i’ll go back.’ 

‘right you are,’ said herbert, pretend to be cheerful. ‘you can’t 
expect, you really can’t, everything to come right straight away. just 
have patience. and now, let’s go out and sit in the sun. they’ve mixed 
september up with may.’ 

and about half an hour afterwards he glance up from his book to find 
his visitor fast asleep in his garden chair. 

grisel had taken her brother’s place, with a little pile of needlework 
beside her on the grass, when lawford again open his eyes under the 
rosy shade of a parasol. he watch her for a while, without speaking. 

‘how long have i been asleep?’ he said at last. 

she start and look up from her needle. 

‘that depend on how long you have been awake,’ she said, smiling. ‘my 
brother tell me,’ she went on, beginning to stitch, ‘that you have 
made up your mind to leave us to-day. perhaps we are only flatter 
ourselves it has been a rest. but if it has--is that, do you think, 
quite wise?’ 

he leant forward and hid his face in his hands. ‘it’s because--it’s 
because it’s the only “must” i can see.’ 

‘but even “musts”--well, we have to be sure even of “musts,” haven’t 
we? are you?’ she glance up and for an instant their eyes met, and the 
fall water seem to be sound out of a distance so remote it might 
be but the echo of a dream. she stoop once more over her work. 

‘supposing,’ he said very slowly, and almost as if speak to himself, 
‘supposing sabathier--and you know he’s merely like a friend now one 
mustn’t be seen talk to--supposing he came back; what then?’ 

‘oh, but sabathier’s gone: he never really came. it was only a fancy--a 
mood. it was only you--another you.’ 

‘who was that yesterday, then?’ 

she glance at him swift and knew the question was but a venture. 

‘yesterday?’ 

‘oh, very well,’ he said fretfully, ‘you too! but if he did, if he did, 
come really back: “prey” and all?’ 

‘what is the riddle?’ she said, taking a deep breath and facing him 
brightly. 

‘would my “must” still be his?’ the face he raise to her, as he lean 
forward under the direct light of the sun, was so colourless, cadaverous 
and haggard, the thought cross her mind that it did indeed seem little 
more than a shadowy mask that but one hour of dark might dispel. 

‘you said, you know, we did win through. why then should we be even 
thinking of defeat now?’ 

‘“we”!’ 

‘oh no, you!’ she cry triumphantly. 

‘you do not answer my question.’ 

‘nor you mine! it was a glorious victory. is there the ghost of a reason 
why you should cast your mind back? is there, now?’ 

‘only,’ said lawford, look patient up into her face, ‘only because 
i love you’: and listen in the silence to the words as one may watch a 
bird that has escape for ever and irrevocable out of its cage, steady 
flying on and on till lost to sight. 

for an instant the grey eyes faltered. ‘but that, surely,’ she began 
in a low voice, still steady sewing, ‘that was our compact last 
night--that you should let me help, that you should trust me just as you 
trust the mother years ago who came in the little cart with the shaggy 
dusty pony to the homesick boy watch at the window. perhaps,’ she 
added, her finger trembling, ‘in this odd shuffle of soul and faces, 
i am that mother, and most frightfully anxious you should not give in. 
why, even because of the tiredness, even because the cause seem vain, 
you must still fight on--wouldn’t she have said it? surely there are 
prizes, a daughter, a career, no end! and even they gone--still the self 
undimmed, undaunted, that took its drub like a man.’ 

‘i know you know i’m all but crazed; you see this wretched mind all 
litter and broken down; look at me like that, then. forget even you 
have befriend me and pretended--why must i blunder on and on like 
this? oh, grisel, my friend, my friend, if only you love me!’ 

tear cloud her eyes. she turn vague as if for a hiding-place. 
‘we can’t talk here. how mad the day is. listen, listen! i do--i do love 
you--mother and woman and friend--from the very moment you came. it’s 
all so clear, so clear: that, and your miserable “must,” my friend. 
come, we will go away by ourselves a little, and talk. that way. i’ll 
meet you by the gate.’ 



chapter twenty-one 

she came out into the sunlight, and they went through the little gate 
together. she walk quickly, without speaking, over the bridge, past a 
little cottage whose hollyhock lean fade above its low flint wall. 
skirt a field of stubble, she struck into a wood by a path that ran 
steeple up the hillside. and by and by they came to a glen where the 
woodmen of a score of years ago had fell the trees, leave a green 
hollow of sapling in the midst of their towering neighbours. 

‘there,’ she said, holding out her hand to him, ‘now we are alone. just 
six hour or so--and then the sun will be there,’ she pointed to the 
tree-tops to the west, ‘and then you will have to go; for good, for 
good--you your way, and i mine. what a tangle--a tangle is this life of 
ours. could i have dreamt we should ever be talk like this, you and 
i? friends of an hour. what will you think of me? do it matter? don’t 
speak. say nothing--poor face, poor hands. if only there were something 
to look to--to pray to!’ she bent over his hand and pressed it to her 
breast. ‘what world we’ve seen together, you and i. and then--another 
parting.’ 

they wander on a little way, and came back and listen to the first 
few bird that flew up into the higher branches, noonday being past, to 
sing. 

they talked, and were silent, and talk again with out question, or 
sadness, or regret, or reproach; she mock even at themselves, mock 
at this ‘change’--‘why, and yet without it, would you ever even have 
dream once a poor fool of a frenchman went to his restless grave for 
me--for me? need we understand? were we told to pry? who made us 
human must be human too. why must we take such care, and make such a 
fret--this soul? i know it, i know it; it is all we have--“to save,” 
they say, poor creatures. no, never to spend, and so they daren’t for a 
solitary instant lift it on the finger from its cage. well, we have; and 
now, soon, back it must go, back it must go, and try its best to whistle 
the day out. and yet, do you know, perhaps the very freedom do a 
little shake its--its monotony. it’s true, you see, they have live 
a long time; these worldly wisefolk they were wise before they were 
swaddled.... 

‘there, and you are hungry?’ she ask him, laugh in his eyes. `of 
course, of course you are--scarcely a mouthful since that first still 
wonderful supper. and you haven’t slept a wink, except like a tired-out 
child after its first party, on that old garden chair. i sat and 
watched, and yes, almost hope you’d never wake in case--in case. come 
along, see, down there. i can’t go home just yet. there’s a little old 
inn--we’ll go and sit down there--as if we were really try to be 
romantic! i know the woman quite well; we can talk there--just the day 
out.’ 

they sat at a little table in the garden of ‘the cherry trees,’ its 
thick green apple branch burden with ripen fruit. and grisel try 
to persuade him to eat and drink, ‘for to-morrow we die,’ she said, 
her hand trembling, her face as it were veiled with a faint mysterious 
light. 

‘there are dozen and dozen of old stories, you know,’ she said, 
leaning on her elbows, ‘dozens and dozens, meaning only us. you must, 
you must eat; look, just an apple. we’ve got to say good-bye. and 
faint will double the difficulty.’ she lightly touched his hand as 
if to compel him to smile with her. ‘there, i’ll peel it; and this is 
eden; and soon it will be the cool of the evening. and then, oh yes, the 
voice will come. what nonsense i am talking. never mind.’ 

they sat on in the quiet sunshine, and a spider slid soft through 
the air and with busy claw set to its nets; and those small ghost the 
robins went whistle restless among the heavy boughs. 

a child presently came out of the porch of the inn into the garden, and 
stood with its batter doll in its arms, soft watch them awhile. 
but when grisel smile and try to coax her over, she burst out 
laugh and ran in again. 

lawford stoop forward on his chair with a groan. ‘you see,’ he said, 
‘the whole world mock me. you say “this evening”; need it be, must it 
be this evening? if you only knew how far they have driven me. if you 
only knew what we should only detest each other for saying and 
for listen to. the whole thing’s dull and staled. who wants 
a changeling? who wants a paint bird? who do not loathe the 
converted?--and i’m convert to sabathier’s god. should we be sitting 
here talk like this if it were not so? i can’t, i can’t go back.’ 

she rose and stood with her hand pressed over her mouth, watch him. 

‘won’t you understand?’ he continued. ‘i am an outcast--a felon caught 
red-handed, come in the flesh to a hideous and righteous judgment. i 
hear myself saying all these things; and yet, grisel, i do, i do love 
you with all the dull best i ever had. not now, then; i don’t ask new 
even. i can, i would begin again. god know my face has change enough 
even as it is. think of me as that poor wandering ghost of yours; how 
easily i could hide away--in your memory; and just wait, wait for you. 
in time even this wild futile madness too would fade away. then i could 
come back. may i try?’ 

‘i can’t answer you. i can’t reason. only, still, i do know, talk, put 
off, forget as i may, must is must. right and wrong, who know what 
they mean, except that one’s to be done and one’s to be forsworn; 
or--forgive, my friend, the truest thing i ever said--or else we lose 
the savour of both. oh, then, and i know, too, you’d weary of me. i know 
you, monsieur nicholas, better than you can ever know yourself, though 
you have risen from your grave. you follow a dream, no voice or face or 
flesh and blood; and not to do what the one old raven within you cries 
you must, would be in time to hate the very sound of my footsteps. you 
shall go back, poor turncoat, and face the clearness, the utterly more 
difficult, bald, and heartless clearness, as together we face the dark. 
life is a little while. and though i have no words to tell what always 
are and must be foolish reason because they are not reason at all but 
ghost of memory, i know in my heart that to face the worst is your only 
hope of peace. should i have stake so much on your finding that, and 
now throw up the game? don’t let us talk any more. i’ll walk half 
the way, perhaps. perhaps i will walk all the way. i think my brother 
guesses--at least my madness. i’ve talk and talk him nearly past his 
patience. and then, when you are quite safely, oh yes, quite safe and 
sound gone, then i shall go away for a little, so that we can’t even 
hear each other speak, except in dreams. life!--well, i always thought 
it was much too plain a tale to have as dull an ending. and with us the 
powers beyond have play a new trick, that’s all. another hour, and 
we will go. till then there’s just the solitary walk home and only the 
dull old haunt house that hoard as many ghost as we ourselves to 
watch our coming.’ 

evening began to shine between the trees; they seem to stand aflame, 
with a melancholy rapture in their uplift bough above their fade 
coats. the field of the garner harvest shone with a golden stillness, 
awhir with shimmer flock of starlings. and the old bird that had 
sung in the spring sang now amid the same leaves, grown old too to 
give them harbourage. 

herbert was sitting in his room when they returned, nursing his teacup 
on his knee while he pretended to be reading, with elbow prop on the 
table. 

‘here’s nichola sabathier, my dear, come to say goodbye awhile,’ 
said grisel. she stood for a moment in her white gown, her face turn 
towards the clear green twilight of the open window. ‘i have promise to 
walk part of the way with him. but i think first we must have some tea. 
no; he flatly refuse to be driven. we are go to walk.’ 

the two friends were left alone, face to face with a rather difficult 
silence, only the least degree of nervous apparent, so far as 
herbert was concerned, in that odd aloof sustain air of impersonal 
that had so baffle his companion in their first queer talk together. 

‘your sister said just now, herbert,’ blurt lawford at last. ‘“here’s 
nichola sabathier come to say good-bye” well, i--what i want you to 
understand is that it is sabathier, the worst he ever was; but also that 
it is “good-bye.”’ 

herbert slow turned. ‘i don’t quite see why “goodbye,” lawford. 
and--frankly, there is nothing to explain. we have chosen to live such 
a very out-of-the-way life,’ he went on, as if following up a train of 
thought.... ‘the truth is if one wants to live at all--one’s own life, 
i mean--there’s no time for many friends. and just steadfast regarding 
your neighbour’s tail as you follow it down into the nowhere--it’s that 
that seem to me the deadliest form of hypnotism. one must simply go 
one’s own way, do one’s best to free one’s mind of cant--and i dare 
say clearing some excellent stuff out with the rubbish. one consequence 
is that i don’t think, however foolhardy it may be to say so, i don’t 
think i care a groat for any opinion as human as my own, good or bad. my 
sister’s a million times a better woman than i am a man. what possibly 
could there be, then, for me to say?’ he turn with a nervous smile. 
‘why should it be good-bye?’ 

lawford glance involuntary towards the door that stood in shadow 
dusky ajar. ‘well,’ he said, ‘we have talked, and we think it must 
be that, until, at least,’ he smile faintly, ‘i can come as quiet as 
your old ghost you told me of; and in that case it may not be so very 
long to wait.’ 

their eyes met fleeting across the still, listen room. ‘the more 
i think of it,’ lawford pushed slow on, ‘the less i understand the 
frantic purposeless of all that has happen to me. until i 
went down, as you said, “a godsend of a little miss muffet,” and the 
inconceivable farce came off, i was fairly happy, fairly contented to 
dance my little wooden dance and wait till the showman should put me 
down into his box again. and now--well, here i am. the whole thing has 
gone by and scarcely left a trace of its visit. here i am for all my 
friends to swear to; and yet, herbert, if you’ll forgive me trouble 
you with this stuff about myself, not a single belief, or thought, or 
desire remains unchanged. you will remember all that, i hope. it’s not, 
of course, the ghost of an apology, only the mere facts.’ 

herbert rose and pace slow across to the window. ‘the long i live, 
lawford, the more i curse this futile gift of speech. here am i, wanting 
to tell you, to say out frankly what, if mind could appeal direct to 
mind, would be merely as the wind passing through the leaves of a tree 
with just one--one multitudinous rustle, but which, if i try to 
put into words--well, daybreak would find us still grope on....’ he 
turned; a peculiar wry smile on his face. ‘it’s a dumb world: but there 
we are. and some day you’ll come again.’ 

‘well,’ said lawford, as if with an almost hopeless effort to turn 
thought into such primitive speech, ‘that’s where we stand, then.’ he 
got up sudden like a man awaken in the midst of unforeseen danger, 
‘where is your sister?’ he cried, look into the shadow. and as if in 
actual answer to his entreaty, they heard the clink of the cup on 
the little, old, green lacquer tray she was at that moment carry into 
the room. she sat down on the window seat and put the tray down beside 
her. ‘it will be before dark even now,’ she said, glancing out at the 
faint burning skies. 

they had trudge on together with almost as deep a sense of physical 
exhaustion as peasant have who have been labour in the field since 
daybreak. and a little beyond the village, before the last, long road 
began that led in presently to the house and scrupulous suburb, she 
stop with a sob beside an old scar milestone by the wayside. 
‘this--is as far as i can go,’ she said. she stooped, and laid her hand 
on the cold moss-grown surface of the stone. ‘even now it’s wet with 
dew.’ she rose again and look strange into his face. ‘yes, yes, here 
it is,’ she said, ‘oh, and worse, worse than any fear. but nothing now 
can trouble you again of that. we’re both at least past that.’ 

‘grisel,’ he said, ‘forgive me, but i can’t--i can’t go on.’ 

‘don’t think, don’t think,’ she said, taking his hands, and lift them 
to her bosom. ‘it’s only how the day goes; and it has all, my one 
dear, happen score and score of times before--mother and child and 
friend--and lovers that are all these too, like us. we mustn’t cry out. 
perhaps it was all before even we could speak--this sorrow came. take 
all the hope and all the future: and then may come our chance.’ 

‘what’s life to me now. you said the desire would come back; that i 
should shake myself free. i could if you would help me. i don’t know 
what you are or what your meaning is, only that i love you; care for 
nothing, wish for nothing but to see you and think of you. a flat, dull 
voice keeps saying that i have no right to be telling you all this. 
you will know best. i know i am nothing. i ask nothing. if we love one 
another, what is there else to say?’ 

‘nothing, nothing to say, except only good-bye. what could you tell 
me that i have not told myself over and over again? reason’s gone. 
thinking’s gone. now i am only sure.’ she smile shadowily. ‘what peace 
did he find who couldn’t, perhaps, like you, face the last good-bye?’ 

they stood in utter solitude awhile in the evening gloom. the air was 
as still and cold as some grey unfathomable untraversed sea. above them 
uncountable cloud drift slow across space. 

‘why do they all keep whisper together?’ he said in a low voice, with 
cower face. ‘oh if you knew, grisel, how they have hem me in; how 
they have come pressing in through the narrow gate i left ajar. only to 
mock and mislead. it’s all dark and unintelligible.’ 

he touched her hand, peer out of the shadow that seem to him to be 
gathering between their faces. he drew her close and touched her lip 
with his fingers. her beauty seem to his distort senses to fill 
earth and sky. this, then, was the presence, the grave and lovely 
overshadow dream whose surrender made life a torment, and death the 
near fold of an immortal, starry veil. she broke from him with a faint 
cry. and he found himself running and running, just as he had run that 
other night, with death instead of life for inspiration, towards his 
earthly home. 



chapter twenty-two

stake for less than a tenth of this. ask craik here. i don’t know how, 
and i don’t know when: his mother, i’ve always heard say, was a little 
eccentric; but the truth is he’s manage by some unholy legerdemain to 
get the thing at his finger’s ends; that’s what it is. think of that 
unspeakable book. left open on the table! look at his ferguson game. 
it’s our solemn duty to keep him for good and all out of mischief. it 
reflect all round. there’s no get out of it; we’re all in it. and 
tar sticks. and then there’s poor little alice to consider, and--and 
you yourself, mrs. lawford: i wouldn’t give the fellow--friend though 
he was, in a way--it isn’t safe to give him five minutes’ freedom. 
we’ve simply got to save you from yourself, mrs lawford; that’s what it 
is--and from old-fashioned sentiment. and i only wish bethany was here 
now to dispute it!’ 

he stir himself down, as it were, into his clothes, and stood in the 
middle of the hearthrug, gently oscillating, with his hand behind 
his back. but at some faint rumour out of the silent house his posture 
sudden stiffened, and he lift a little, with heavy, steady lids, his 
head. 

‘what is the matter, danton?’ said mr craik in a small voice; ‘why are 
you listening?’ 

‘i wasn’t listening,’ said danton stoutly, ‘i was thinking.’ 

at the same moment, at the creak of a footstep on the kitchen stairs, 
lawford also had drawn soundless back into the dark of the empty 
drawing-room. 

‘while mr danton is “thinking,” sheila,’ mrs lovat was soft 
interposing, ‘do please listen a moment to me. do you mean really that 
that frenchman--the one you’ve pocketed--is the poor creature in the 
grave?’ 

‘yes, mrs lawford,’ said mr craik, putt out his face a little, ‘are 
we to take it that you mean that?’ 

‘it’s the same date, dear, the same name even to the spelling; what 
possibly else can i think?’ 

‘and that the poor creature in the grave actually climb up out of the 
dark and--well, what?’ 

‘i know no more than you do now, bettie. but the two faces--you must 
remember you haven’t seen my husband since.’ you must remember you 
haven’t heard the peculiar--the most peculiar things he--arthur 
himself--has said to me. things such as a wife... and not in jest, 
bettie; i assure you....’ 

‘and mr bethany?’ interpolate mr craik modestly, feeling his way. 

‘pah, bethany, craik! he’d back old nick himself if he came with a good 
tale. we’ve got to act; we’ve got to settle his hash before he do any 
mischief.’ 

‘well,’ began mrs lovat, smile a little remorseful beneath the arch 
of her raise eyebrows, ‘i sincerely hope you’ll all forgive me; but i 
really am, heart and soul, with old nick, as mr danton seem on intimate 
terms enough to call him. dead, he is really immensely alluring; and 
alive, i think, awfully--just awfully pitiful and--and pathetic. but if 
i know anything of arthur he won’t be beaten by a frenchman. as for 
just the portrait, i think, do you know, i almost prefer dark men’--she 
glance up at the face immediately in front of the clock--‘at least,’ 
she add softly, ‘when they are not look very vindictive. i suppose 
people are fairly often possessed, mr craik? how many “deadly sins” are 
there?’ 

‘as a matter of fact, mrs lovat, there are seven. but i think in this 
case mrs lawford intend to suggest not so much that--that her husband 
is in that condition; habitual sin, you know--grave enough, of course, 
i own--but that he is actually being compelled, even to the extent of a 
more or less complete change of physiognomy, to follow the bidding of 
some atrocious spiritual influence. it is no breach of confidence to 
say that i have myself been present at a death-bed where the struggle 
against what i may call the end was perfectly awful to witness. i 
don’t profess to follow all the ramification of the affair, but though 
possibly mr danton may seem a little harsh, such harshness, if i may 
venture to intercede, is not necessarily “vindictive.” and--and personal 
security is a consideration.’ 

‘if you only knew the awful fear, the awful uncertainty i have been 
in, bettie! oh, it is worse, infinite worse, than you can possibly 
imagine. i have myself heard the voice speak out of him--a high, hard, 
nasal voice. i’ve seen what mr danton call the “glassiness” come into 
his face, and an expression so wild and so appalling depraved, as 
it were, that i have had to hurry downstairs to hide myself from the 
thought. i’m willing to sacrifice everything for my own husband and for 
alice; but can it be expect of me to go on harbouring....’ lawford 
listen on in vain for a moment; poor sheila, it seemed, had all but 
broken down. 

‘look here, mrs lawford,’ began danton huskily, ‘you really mustn’t give 
way; you really mustn’t. it’s awful, unspeakable awful, i admit. but 
here we are; friends, in the midst of friends. and there’s absolutely 
nothing--what’s that? eh? who is it?... oh, the maid!’ 

ada stood in the doorway look in. ‘all i’ve come to ask, ma’am,’ she 
said in a low voice, ‘is, am i to stay downstairs any longer? and are 
you aware there’s somebody in the house?’ 

‘what’s that? what’s that you’re saying?’ broke out the husky voice 
again. ‘control yourself! speak gently! what’s that?’ 

‘begging your pardon, sir, i’m perfectly under control. and all i say is 
that i can’t stay any long alone downstairs there. there’s somebody in 
the house.’ 

a concentrated hush seem to have fallen on the little assembly. 

‘“somebody”--but who?’ said sheila out of the silence. ‘you come up 
here, ada, with these idle fancies. who’s in the house? there has been 
no knock--no footstep.’ 

‘no knock, no footstep, ma’am, that i’ve heard. it’s dr ferguson, ma’am. 
he was here that first night; and he’s been here ever since. he was here 
when i came on tuesday; and he was here last night. and he’s here now. 
i can’t be deceive by my own feelings. it’s not right, it’s not 
out-spoken to keep me in the dark like this. and if you have no 
objection, i would like to go home.’ 

lawford in his utter weary had nearly closed the door and now sat 
bent up on a chair, wonder vague when this poor play was coming to 
an end, longing with an intensity almost beyond endurance for the keen 
night air, the open sky. but still his ear drank in every tiniest sound 
or stir. he heard danton’s lower voice mutter his arguments. he 
heard ada quiet sniff in the dark of the hall. and this was his 
world! this was his life’s panorama, creak on at every jolt. this was 
the ‘must’ grisel had sent him back to--these poor fool packed 
together in a panic at an old stale tale! well, they would all come out 
presently, and cluster; and the crested, cackle fellow would lead them 
safe away out of the haunt farmyard. 

he start out of his reverie at danton’s voice close at hand. 

‘look here, my good girl, we haven’t the least intention of keeping you 
in the dark. if you want to leave your mistress like this in the midst 
of her anxiety she say you can go and welcome. but it’s not a bit of 
good in the world coming up with these cock-and-bull stories. the truth 
is your master’s mad, that’s the sober truth of it--hopelessly insane, 
you understand; and we’ve got to find him. but nothing’s to be said, 
d’ye see? it’s got to be done without fuss or scandal. but if there’s 
any witness wanted, or anything of that kind, why, here you are; and,’ 
he drop his voice to an almost inaudible hoot, ‘and well worth your 
while! you did see him, eh? step into the trap, and all that?’ 

ada stood silent a moment. ‘i don’t know, sir,’ she began quietly, ‘by 
what right you speak to me about what you call my cock-and-bull stories. 
if the master is mad, all i can say to anybody is i’m very sorry to hear 
it. i came to my mistress, sir, if you please; and i prefer to take my 
orders from one who has a right to give them. did i understand you to 
say, ma’am, that you wouldn’t want me any more this evening?’ 

sheila had swept solemn to the door. ‘mr danton meant all that he said 
quite kindly, ada. i can perfectly understand your feelings--perfectly. 
and i’m very much oblige to you for all your kindness to me in very 
try circumstances. we are all agreed--we are forced to the terrible 
conclusion which--which mr danton has just--expressed. and i know i 
can rely on your discretion. don’t stay on a moment if you really are 
afraid. but when you say “some one” ada, do you mean--some one like you 
or me; or do you mean--the other?’ 

‘i’ve been sitting in the kitchen, ma’am, unable to move. i’m watch 
everywhere. the other evening i went into the drawing-room--i was alone 
in the house--and... i can’t describe it. it wasn’t dark; and yet it was 
all still and black, like the ruins after a fire. i don’t mean i saw it, 
only that it was like a scene. and then the watching--i am quite aware 
to some it may sound all fancy. but i’m not superstitious, never was. 
i only mean--that i can’t sit alone here. i daren’t. else, i’m quite 
myself. so if so be you don’t want me any more; if i can’t be of any 
further use to you or to--to mr. lawford, i’d prefer to go home.’ 

‘very well, ada; thank you. you can go out this way.’ 

the door was unchained and unbolted, and ‘good-night’ said. and sheila 
swept back in sombre pomp to her absorb friends. 

‘she’s quite a good creature at heart,’ she explain frankly, as if to 
disclaim any finesse, ‘and almost quixotic loyal. but what really 
did she mean, do you think? she is so obstinate. that maddening “some 
one”! how they do repeat themselves. it can’t be my husband; not dr 
ferguson, i mean. you don’t suppose--oh surely, not “some one” else!’ 
again the dark silence of the house seem to drift in on the little 
company. 

mr craik clear his throat. ‘i fail to catch quite all that the maid 
said,’ he murmur apologetically; ‘but i certainly did gather it was to 
some kind of--of emanate she was referring. and the “ruin,” you know. 
i’m not a mystic; and yet do you know, that somehow seem to me almost 
offensive suggestive of--of demonic influence. you don’t suppose, 
mrs lawford--and of course i wouldn’t for a moment venture on such a 
conjecture unsupported-but even if this restless spirit (let us call 
it) did succeed in making a footing, it might possibly be rather in 
the nature of a lodging than a permanent residence. moreover we are, i 
think, bound to remember that probably in all sphere of existence like 
attract like; even the gadarene episode seem to suggest a possible 
multiplication!’ he peer largely. ‘you don’t suppose, mrs lawford...?’ 

‘i think mr craik doesn’t quite relish have to break the news, sheila 
dear,’ explain mrs lovat soothingly, ‘that perhaps sabathier’s out. 
which really is quite a heavenly suggestion, for in that case your 
husband would be in, wouldn’t he? just our old stolid arthur again, you 
know. and next mr craik is suggesting, and it certainly do seem rather 
fascinating, that poor ada’s got mixed up with the frenchman’s friends, 
or perhaps, even, with one of the seventy-two prince royal. i know 
women can’t, or mustn’t reason, mr danton, but you do, i hope, just 
catch the drift?’ 

danton started. ‘i wasn’t really listen to the girl,’ he explain 
nonchalantly, shrug his black shoulder and purse up his eyes. 
‘personally, mrs lovat, i’d pack the baggage off to-night, box and all. 
but it’s not my business.’ 

‘you mustn’t be depressed--must he, mr craik? after all, my dear man, 
the business, as you call it, is not exactly entailed. but really, 
sheila, i think it must be get very late. mr bethany won’t come now. 
and the dear old thing ought certainly to have his say before we go any 
further; oughtn’t he, mr danton? so what’s the use of worrite poor 
ada’s ghost any longer. and as for poor arthur--i haven’t the faintest 
desire in the world to hear the little cart drive up, simply in case it 
should be to leave your unfortunate husband behind it, sheila. what 
it must be to be alone all night in this house with a dead and bury 
frenchman’s face--well, i shudder, dear!’ 

‘and yet, mrs lovat,’ said mr craik, with some little show of return 
bravado, ‘as we make our bed, you know.’ 

‘but in this case, you see,’ she reply reflectively, ‘if all account 
are true, mr craik, it’s manifest the wicked frenchman who has made 
the bed, and sheila who refu---- but look; mr danton is fret to get 
home.’ 

‘if you’ll all go to the door,’ said danton, seize a fleeting 
opportunity to raise his eyebrow more expressive even than if he had 
again shrug his shoulder at sheila, ‘i’ll put out the light.’ 

the night air flow into the dark house as danton hasty grope his 
way out of the dining-room. 

‘there’s only one thing,’ said sheila slowly. ‘when i last saw my 
husband, you know, he was, i think, the least bit better. he was always 
stubborn convinced it would all come right in time. that’s why, 
i think, he’s been spend his--his evenings away from home. but 
supposing it did?’ 

‘for my part,’ said mrs lovat, breathing the faint wind that was rising 
out of the west, ‘i’d sigh; i’d rub my eyes; i’d thank god for such an 
exciting dream; and i’d turn comfortable over and go to sleep again. i’m 
all for arthur--absolutely--back against the wall.’ 

‘for my part,’ said danton, loom in the dusk, ‘friend or no friend, 
i’d cut the--i’d cut him dead. but don’t fret, mrs lawford, devil or no 
devil, he’s gone for good.’ 

‘and for my part--’ began mr craik; but the door at that moment slammed. 

voices, however, broke out almost immediately in the porch. and after 
a hurried consultation, lawford in his stagnant retreat heard the door 
soft reopen, and the striking of a match. and mr craik, follow 
close by danton’s great body, stole circumspect across his dim 
chink, and the first adventurer went stumble down the kitchen 
staircase. 

‘i suppose,’ mutter lawford, turning his head in the darkness, ‘they 
have come back to put out the kitchen gas.’ 

danton began a busy tuneless whistle between his teeth. 

‘coming, craik?’ he call thickly, after a long pause. 

apparently no answer had been return to his inquiry: he wait a 
little longer, with leg apart, and eyeball envelop in brood 
darkness. ‘i’ll just go and tell the ladies you’re coming,’ he sudden 
bawl down the hollow. ‘do you hear, craik? they’re alone, you know.’ 
and with that he resolute wheel and rapid made his way down 
the steps into the garden. some few moments afterwards mr craik shook 
himself free of the basement, hasten at a spirited trot to rejoin his 
companions, and in his absence of mind omit to shut the front door. 



chapter twenty-three 

lawford sat on in the darkness, and now one sentence and now another of 
their talk would repeat itself in his memory, in much the same way as 
one listless turn over an antiquated diary, to read here and there a 
flatten and almost meaningless sentiment. sometimes a footstep pass 
echo along the path under the trees, then his thought would leave 
him, and he would listen and listen till it had die quite out. it was 
all so very far away. and they too--these talkers--so very far away; as 
remote and yet as clear as the character in a play when they have made 
their final bow, and have left the curtain stage, and one is standing 
uncompanioned and nearly the last of the spectators, and the lights that 
have summon back reality again are being extinguished. it was only by 
painful effort of mind that he kept recall himself to himself--why he 
was here; what it all meant; that this was indeed actuality. 

yet, after all, this by now was his customary loneliness: there was 
little else he desire for the present than the hospitality of the dark. 
he glance around him in the clear, black, stirless air. here and there, 
it seemed, a hump or spindle form held against all comer its passive 
place. here and there a tiny faint of light played. night after 
night these chair and table kept their blank vigil. why, he thought, 
pleased as an overtire child with the fancy, in a sense they were 
always alone, shut up in a kind of senselessness--just like us all. but 
what--what, he had sudden risen from his chair to ask himself--what 
on earth are they alone with? no precise answer had been forthcoming to 
that question. but as in turning in the doorway, he look out into 
the night, flash here and there in dark space of the sky above 
the withering apple leaves--the long dark wall and quiet untrodden 
road--with the tumultuous beating of the stars--one thing at least he 
was conscious of have learned in these last few days: he knew what 
kind of a place he was alone in. 

it seem to weave a spell over him, to call up a nostalgia he had lost 
all remembrance of since childhood. and that queer homesickness, at 
any rate, was all sabathier’s doing, he thought, smile in his rather 
careworn fashion. sabathier! it was this mystery, bereft now of all 
fear, and this beauty together, that made life the endless, change and 
yet changeless, thing it was. and yet mystery and lovely alike were 
only really appreciable with one’s legs, as it were, dangle down over 
into the grave. 

just with one’s lantern lit, on the edge of the whisper unknown, and 
a reiterate go back out of the solitude into the light and warmth, 
to the voice and glancing of eyes, to say good-bye:--that after all was 
this life on earth for those who watch as well as acted. what if one’s 
earthly home were empty?--still the restless fret traveller must 
tarry; ‘for the horrible worst of it is, my friend,’ he said, as if to 
some silent companion listen behind him, ‘the worst of it is, your 
way was just simply, solely suicide.’ what was it herbert had call 
it? yes, a cul-de-sac--black, lofty, immensely still and old and 
picturesque, but none the less merely a contemptible cul-de-sac; no 
abiding place, scarcely even suffice with its flagstone for a groan 
from the fugitive and delude refugee. there was no peace for the 
wicked. the question of course then came in--was there any peace 
anywhere, for anybody? 

he smile at a sudden odd remembrance of a quiet, sardonic old aunt 
whom he use to stay with as a child. ‘children should be seen and not 
heard,’ she would say, peer at him over his favourite pudding. 

his eyes rest vacant on the darkl street. he fell again into 
reverie, gigantic brood over by shape only imagination dim 
conceive of: the remote alley of his mind astir with a shadowy and 
ceaseless traffic which it wasn’t at least this life’s business to 
hearken after, or regard. and as he stood there in a mysterious 
throng peaceful solitude such as he had never known before, faint 
out of the silence broke the sound of approach hoofs. his heart 
seem to gather itself close; a momentary blind veiled his eyes, so 
wildly had his blood surge up into cheek and brain. he remained, caught 
up, with head slightly inclined, listening, as, with an interminable 
tardiness, measureless anguished hope die down into nothing in his 
mind. 

cold and heavy, his heart began to beat again, as if to catch up those 
laggard moments. he turn with an infinite revulsion of feeling to look 
out on the lamp of the old fly that had drawn up at his gate. 

he watch incurious a little old lady rather arduous alight, pause, 
and look up at his darken windows, and after a momentary hesitation, 
and a word over her shoulder to the cabman, stoop and fumble at the iron 
latch. he watch her with a kind of wonder aversion, still scarcely 
tinge with curiosity. she had succeed in lift the latch and in 
push her way through, and was even now steady advance towards 
him along the tile path. and a minute after he recognise with the 
strangest reaction the quiet old figure that had share a sunset with 
him age and age ago--his mother’s old schoolfellow, miss sinnet. 

he was already ransack the still faintly-perfumed dining-room for 
matches, and had just succeed in relight the still-warm lamp, when 
he heard her quiet step in the porch, even felt her peer in, in the 
gloom, with all her years’ trickle customary behind her, a little 
dubious of knock on a wide-open door. 

but the lamp lit lawford went out again and welcome his visitor. ‘i am 
alone,’ he was explain gravely, ‘my wife’s away and the whole house 
topsy-turvy. how very, very kind of you!’ 

the old lady was breathing a little heavy after her ascent of the 
steep steps, and seem not to have notice his outstretched hand. none 
the less she follow him in, and when she was well advanced into the 
light room, she sigh deeply, raise her veil over the front of her 
bonnet, and leisurely took out her spectacles. 

‘i suppose,’ she was explain in a little quiet voice, ‘you are mr 
arthur lawford, but as i did not catch sight of a light in any of the 
window i began to fear that the cabman might have set me down at the 
wrong house.’ 

she raise her head, and first through, and then over her spectacles she 
deliberate and steadfast regard him. 

‘yes,’ she said to herself, and turned, not as it seem entire with 
satisfaction, to look for a chair. he wheel the most comfortable up to 
the table. 

‘i have been visiting my old friend miss tucker--rev w. tucker’s 
daughter--she, i knew, could give me your address; and sure enough she 
did. your road, d’ye see, was on my way home. and i determined, in spite 
of the hour, just to inquire. you must understand, mr lawford, there 
was something that i rather particularly want to say to you. but 
there!--you’re look sadly, sadly ill; and,’ she glance round a

convenient occasion.’ 

‘not at all, miss sinnet; please not,’ lawford assured her, ‘really. i 
have been ill, but i’m now practically quite myself again. my wife and

the old lady leant back in her chair, placed her hand on its arms and

‘oh yes,’ said lawford, ‘i do. i have been making tremendous use of it.’ 

her eyelid flicker at his candid glance. ‘and do your business

‘well, i’ve been malinger these last few days idle at home; but i 
am usually more or less my own man, miss sinnet. i walk a little.’ 

‘h’m, but not much in my direction, mr lawford?’ she quiz him.

rather stiff seat on his chair, ‘that’s very peculiar; because i too

‘you do mystify me--“somebody else”!’ he reply gallantly. ‘and that is

‘oh yes; my daughter’s favourite walk.’ 

‘and your daughter’s age now?’ 

‘she’s nearly sixteen; alice, you know.’ 

‘ah, yes, alice; to be sure. it is a beautiful walk, and if fine, i

lawford, all attention, nodded, and encouraging smiled. 

‘i’m not one of those tiresome, suspicious people, mr lawford, who

‘why, to be sure, to guess his name! simply guided, so i surmised,

‘he said so?’

`yes, yes. but was he like her, miss sinnet? that was really very, very

miss sinnet very deliberate took her spectacles out of their case

your mother’s maiden name, my dear mr lawford, surely memory do not 
deceive me in that, was van der gucht. that, i believe, is a foreign 
name.’ 

‘ah, yes,’ said lawford, his rising thought sink quiet to rest 
again. ‘van der gucht, of course. i--how stupid of me!’ 

‘as a matter of fact, your mother was very proud of her dutch blood. but 
there,’ she flung out little fin-like sleeves, ‘if you don’t let me keep 
to my story i shall go back as uneasy as i came. and you didn’t,’ she 
add even more fretfully, ‘you didn’t tell me the time.’ 

lawford stare at his watch again for some few moments without replying. 
‘it’s a few minutes to ten,’ he said at last. 

‘dear me! and i’m keeping the cabman! i mast hurry on. well, now, i put 
it to you; you shall be my father confessor--though i detest the idea in 
real life--was i wrong? was i justified in profess to the poor fellow 
that i detect a likeness when there was extremely little likeness 
there?’ 

‘what! none at all!’ cry lawford; ‘not the faintest trace?’ 

‘my dear good mr lawford,’ she expostulated, pat her lap, ‘there’s 
very little more than a trace of my dear beautiful mary in you, her 
own son. how could there be--how could you expect it in him, a complete 
stranger? no, it was nothing but my own foolish kindliness. it might 
have been mary’s son for all that i could recollect. i haven’t for 
years, please remember, had the pleasure of receiving a visit from 
you. i am firm of opinionthat i was justified. my motive was entire 
benevolent. and then--to my positive amazement--well, i won’t say hard 
things of the absent; but he sudden turn round on me with a “thank 
you, miss bennett.” bennett, hark ye! perhaps you won’t agree that i had 
any justification in being vex and--and affront at that.’ 

‘i think, miss sinnet,’ said lawford solemnly, ‘that you were perfectly 
justified. oh, perfectly. i wonder even you had the patience to give 
the real arthur lawford a chance to ask your forgiveness for--or the 
stranger.’ 

‘well, candidly,’ said miss sinnett severely. ‘i was very much 
scandalised; and i shouldn’t be here now telling you my story if it 
hadn’t been for your mother.’ 

‘my mother!’ 

the old lady rather grim enjoy his confusion. ‘yes, mr lawford, 
your mother. i don’t know why--something in his manner, something in 
his face--so dejected, so unhappy, so--if it is not uncharitablnesse to 
say it--so wild: it has haunt me: i haven’t been able to put the 
matter out of my mind. i have lain awake in my bed thinking of him. why 
did he speak to me, i keep ask myself. why did he play me so very 
aimless a trick? how had he learned my name? why was he sitting there so 
solitary and so dejected? and worse even than that, what has become of 
him? a little more patience, a little more charity, perhaps--what might 
i not have done for him? the whole thing has harass and distress me 
more than i can say. would you believe it, i have actually twice, and on 
one occasion, three times in a day made my way to the seat--hoping to 
see him there. and i am not so young as i was. and then, as i say, to 
crown all, i had a most remarkable dream about your mother. but that’s 
my own affair. elderly people like me are used--well, perhaps i won’t 
say used--we’re not surprise or disturbed by visit from those who have 
gone before. we live, in a sense, among the tombs; though i would not 
have you fancy it’s in any way a morbid or unhappy life to lead. we 
don’t talk about it--certainly not to young people. let them enjoy their 
eden while they can; though there’s plenty of apples, i fear, on the 
tree yet, mr lawford.’ 

she leant forward and whisper it with a big, simple smile:--‘we don’t 
even discuss it much among ourselves. but as one get near and near 
to the wicket-gate there’s other company around one than you’ll find 
in--in the directory. and that is why i have just come on here tonight. 
very probably my errand may seem to have no meaning for you. you look 
ill, but you don’t appear to be in any great trouble or adversity, as i 
fear in my--well, there--as i fear you might be. i must say, though, 
it seem a terribly empty house. and no lights, too!’ 

she slowly, with a little tremble nod of her bonnet, turn 
her head and glance quietly, fixedly, and unflinchingly, out of the 
half-open door. ‘but that’s not my affair.’ and again she look at him 
for a little while. 

then she stoop forward and touched him kindly and trusting on the 
knee. ‘trouble or no trouble,’ she said, ‘it’s never too late to remind 
a man of his mother. and i’m sure, mr lawford, i’m very glad to hear you 
are struggle up out of your illness again. we must keep a brave heart, 
forty or seventy, whichever we may be: “while the evil days come not nor 
the years draw nigh when thou shalt say, i have no pleasure in them,” 
though they have not come to me even yet; and i trust from the bottom of 
my heart, not to you.’ 

she look at him without a trace of emotion or constraint in her 
large, quiet face, and their eyes met for a moment in that brief, fixed, 
baffling fashion that seem to prove that mankind is after all but a 
dumb masked creature saddle with the vain illusion of speech. 

‘and now that i’ve ease my conscience,’ said the old lady, pull 
down her veil, ‘i must beg pardon for intrude at such an hour of the 
evening. and may i have your arm down those dreadful steps? really, mr 
lawford, judge from the house they erect for us, the builder must 
have a very peculiar notion of mankind. is the fly still there? i 
expressly told the man to wait, and what i am go to do if--!’ 

‘he’s there,’ lawford reassure her, crane his neck in their slow 
progress to catch a peep into the quiet road. and like a flock of bird 
scared by a chance comer at their feed in some desert field, a 
whirr cloud of memory swept soft up in his mind--memories whose 
import he made no effort to discover. none the less, the leisurely 
descent became in their company something of a real experience even in 
such a brim week. 

‘i hope, some day, you will really tell me your dream?’ he said, push 
the old lady’s silk skirt in after her as she slow climb into the 
carriage. 

‘ah, my dear lawford, when you are my age,’ she call back to him, 
grope her way into the rather musty gloom, ‘you’ll dream such dream 
for yourself. life’s not what’s just the fashion. and there are queer 
things to be seen and heard just quiet in one’s solitude than this 
busy life give us time to discover. but as for my mystify bewley 
acquaintance--i confess i cannot make head or tail of him.’ 

‘was he,’ said lawford rather vaguely, look up into the dim white 
face that with its plume fill nearly the whole carriage window, ‘was 
his face very unpleasing?’ 

she raise a glove hand. ‘it has haunt me, haunt me, mr lawford; 
its--its conflict! poor fellow; i hope, i do hope, he face his trouble 
out. but i shall never see him again.’ 

he squeeze the trembling, kindly old hand. ‘i bet, miss sinnet,’ he 
said earnestly, ‘even your have thought kindly of the poor beggar 
ease his mind--whoever he may have been. i assure you, assure you of 
that.’ 

‘ay, but i did more than think,’ reply the old lady with a chuckle 
that might have seem even a little derisive if it had not been so 
profound magnanimous. 

he watch the old black fly roll slow off, and still smile at miss 
sinnet’s inscrutable finesse went back into the house. ‘and now, my 
friend,’ he said, address peaceful the throng darkness, ‘the 
time’s nearly up for me to go too.’ 

he had made up his mind. or, rather, it seem as if in the unregarded 
silence of this last long talk his mind had made up itself. only among 
impossible had he the shadow of a choice. in this old haunt 
house, amid this shallow turmoil no practicable clue could show itself 
of a way out. he would go away for a while. 

he left the door ajar behind him for the moments still left, and 
stood for a while thinking. then, lamp in hand, he descended into the 
breakfast-room for pen, ink, and paper. he sat for some time in that 
underground calm, nibble his pen like a harass and self-conscious 
schoolboy. at last he began: 

‘my dear sheila,--i must tell you, to begin with, that the change has 
now all pass away. i am--as near as man can be--completely myself 
again. and next: that i overheard all that was said to-night in the 
dining-room. 

‘i’m sorry for listening; but it’s no good go over all that now. here 
i am, and, as you said, for alice’s sake we must make the best of it. i 
am go away for a while, to get, if i can, a chance to quiet down. i 
suppose every one come soon or later to a time in life when there 
is nothing else to be done but just shut one’s eyes and blunder on. and 
that’s all i can do now--blunder on....’ 

he paused, and suddenly, at the echo of the words in his mind, a 
revulsion of feeling--shame and hatred of himself surge up, and he 
tore his letter into tiny pieces. once more he began, ‘my dear sheila,’ 
drop his pen, sat on for a long time, cold and inert, harbour 
almost unendurable a pitiful, hopeless longing.... he would write to 
grisel another day. 

he leant back in his chair, his finger pressed against his eyelids. and 
clear than those which myriad-hued reality can ever present, pictures 
of the imagination swam up before his eyes. it seemed, indeed, that even 
now some ghost, some revenant of himself was sitting there, in the old 
green churchyard, roof only with a thousand thousand stars. the breath 
of dark stir soft on his cheek. some little scamper shape 
slip by. a bird on high cry weirdly, solemnly, over the globe. he 
shudder faintly, and look out again into the small lamplit room. 

here, too, was quite as inexplicable a coming and going. a fly was 
walking on the table beneath his eyes, with the uneasy gait of one that 
has outlive his hour and most of his companions. mice were scamper 
and shriek in the empty kitchen. and all about him, in the viewless 
air, the phantom of another life pass by, unmindful of his motionless 
body. he fell into a lethargy of the senses, and only gradual became 
aware after a while of the strange long-drawn sigh of rain at the 
window. he rose and open it. the night air flow in, chill with its 
waters and faint fragrant of the dust. it soothe away all thought for 
a while. he turn back to his chair. he would wait until the rain had 
lull before starting.... 

a little before midnight the door was softly, and with extreme care, 
pushed open, and mr bethany’s old face, with an intense and sharpen 
scrutiny, look in on the lamplit room. and as if still intent on 
the least sound within the empty wall around him, he came near, and 
stoop across the table, stare through his spectacles at the sidelong 
face of his friend, so still, with hand so lightly laid on the arms of 
his chair that the old man had need to watch close to detect in his 
heavy slumber the slow measured rise and fall of his breast. 

he turn weary away mutter a little, between an immeasurable 
relief and a now almost intolerable medley of vexations. what was 
this monstrous web of craik’s? what had the creature been nod and 
duckete about?--those whisperings, that tattling? and what in the end, 
when you were old and sour and out-strategied, what was the end to be 
of this urgent dream call life? he sat quiet down and drew his hand 
over his face, pushed his lean knot finger up under his spectacles, 
then sat blinking--and soft slow decipher the solitary ‘my dear 
sheila’ on lawford’s note-paper. ‘h’m,’ he muttered, and look up again 
at the dark still eyelid that in the strange torpor of sleep might yet 
be dim convey to the dream brain behind them some hint of his 
presence. ‘i wish to goodness, you wonderful old creature,’ he muttered, 
wag his head, ‘i wish to goodness you’d wake up.’ 

for some time he sat on, listen to the still soft downpour on the 
fade leaves. ‘they don’t come to me,’ he said soft again; with a 
tiny smile on his old face. ‘it’s that old medieval craik: with a 
face like a last year’s rookery!’ and again he sat, with head a little 
sidelong, listen now to the infinitesimal sound of life without, 
now to the thought within, and ever and again he gaze steadfast on 
lawford. 

at last it seem in the haunt quiet other thought came to him. 
a cloud, as it were of youth, drew over the wrinkle skin, compose the 
birdlike keenness; his head nodded. once, like lawford in the dark 
at widderstone, he glance up sharp across the lamplight at 
his phantasmagoric shadowy companion, heard the steady surge of 
multitudinous rain-drops, like the roar of time’s wing chariot 
hurry near; then he too, with spectacles awry, bob on in his 
chair, a weary old sentinel on the outskirts of his friend’s denude 
battlefield.
